THE RED PAGE.
JOHANNES BRAHMS
t j YDNEY musicians recently have been
devoting attention to the work of
Brahms, and during last month several
-of his compositions have been given in
public. Those interested, and music-lovers
generally, will appreciate the newly-issued
Life of Brahms, by Florence May (Lend.,
Arnold ; 21s. net). Miss May is a competent
musician who was a pupil of Brahms on the
piano, and her labour is of love. She writes as
friend rather than as critic ; but many criti-
cal passages occur in the work, which gives a
complete account of the musical life of
Brahms. The author lias spared no pains to
reach accuracy in detail, and her volumes
may be accepted as an authoritative record
of fact.' The appendices include a clear ex-
planation of Brahms’ method and a chrono-
logical list of compositions, and there are
several good portraits and illustrations.
J*
Johannes Brahms was born at Hamburg
7th May, 1833. He died at Vienna 3rd
April, 1897. His parents came from Saxony;
their name is equivalent to Eng. broom, or
to “ bram” in bramble. Their condition was
humble, and Brahms was brought up in
poverty. Early hardship confirmed a shy,
solitary, brooding disposition. In later
years of success he gained a reputation
for roughness and arrogance—an autocratic
old bachelor. Yet lie was essentially
kindly, however impatient of contradiction.
Apparently his temperament was too cold for
marriage : he was not warmly attracted by
women, though he liked to philander ;
but partly his celibacy was due to devotion
to his art. He had made up his mind that,
to succeed as a musician, he must contract
no social responsibilities.
A penetrating young Jewess gives this
account of Brahms at the age of 20 :
He sat down opposite me, this young hero of
the day, this young messiah of Schumann's, fair,
delicate-looking, who at twenty has clearly-cut
features free from all passion. Purity, innocence,
naturalness, power, and depth—this indicates his
being....He is generally extremely quiet and
dreamy. ... And with all this independent strength,
a thin boy’s voice that has not yet changed ! and
a child's countenance that any gill might kiss
without blushing....
The reference to Schumann applies to S’s
rhapsodical article in the Neue Zeitschrift fur
Musik in 1853, when he praised the un-
known lad as the musical genius Germany
was awaiting. Schumann wrote :
I have thought, watching the path of those
chosen ones with the gieatest sympathy; that
after such a preparation someone must and would
suddenly appear, destined to give ideal present-
ment to the highest expression of the time, who
would bring us his mastership, not in process of
development, but would spring forth like Minerva
fully armed from the head of Jove. And lie is
come, a young blood by whose cradle graces and
heroes kept watch. He is called Johaunes
Brahms.
Despite Schumann’s authority, this an-
nouncement was received by the German
musical world wibli doubt and derision.
Brahms at that time was scarcely known as
a composer : his greatness lay all in the
future. Sehumaiiu imposed on him the
hard task of justifying premature praise, and
he succeeded ; thou'gn even to-day his place
and value in music are debated.
Miss May gives 'an interesting account of
Brahms as pianist and teacher. It was his
habit to rise very early, and to do most of
his day s work before eleven. He was
ciiaraoteristically German in his devotion to
conee and tobacco. He was a little man—in
youth linn and frail, in age corpulent : a
blonde Saxon with fair, straight hair, worn
-long. “ His most stiiicing physical charac-
teristic was the grand head with its
magnificent intellectual forehead, but the
blue eyes were also remarkable from their
expression of intense mental concentration.”
A great walker, ho had a passionate love of
nature. ‘‘ How can I most quickly improve ?”
his pupil asked him. “ You must walk con-
stantly in the forest,” replied Brahms ; 'and
he meant iiis prescription literally. Methodi-
cally he sought inspiration in nature and in
books : he was an ardent reader of poetry
and German fiction, simple or mystic.
Brahms had certainly formed the clearest con-
ception nut only of Jhis purpose in life, but of the
means by which, he felt lie could best pursue and
achieve it, and. from first to last lie inflexibly
adhered to the conclusions lie liad come to on.
these points, if his aim was to give the most
complete possible expression in ins musical crea-
tions to tnt: very best that was in him,-Ins method,
while it satisfied an inner craving of his being, was
yet, as i believe, deliberately adopted ; and it was
to lay himself open to every kind of influence
which could healthily foster the ideal side of his
nature, and more or less completely to eschew all
others.
It would be ridiculous, at the present time, to
the completeness of his technical
musical equipment, to dilate on his easy grasp of
all the resources;of counterpoint, on his mastery
of form, of harmonic and rhythmic combinations,
and the like. These things are matter of course.
But Brahms knew that not alone his intellect, but
hjs mind and Spirit anil fancy, must be constantly
fi'urtured.if fliey were to bring forth the highest
of which they were capable ; and be so arranged
his life that they should be fed ever -and always
by poetry and literature atld art, by solitary
musing, by participation in so much of life as
seemed to him to be real and true, ami above all
and in the highest degree, by the.companionship
of Nature.
J*
As teacher of the piano, Miss May says
Brahms was absolutely ideal.
He not only showed me how to practise : he
made me, at first, practise to him during a good
part of my lessons, whilst lie sat watching my
fingers ; telling me what was wrong in my way of
moving them, indicating, by a movement of his
his own hand, a better position for mine, absorbing
himself entirely, for the time being, in the object
of helping me. . . ;
may seem incredible, but it is none the less
true, that after a very' few weeks of work with
him the appearance of my hands had completely
changed.
As pianist, Brahms needed particularly to
be en rapport with his audience ; and the
effect of his playing was always*subject to
his own mood. Some days he played bril-
liantly ; other days he “could not play at
all.’'
It was not the playing of a virtuoso, though he
had a large amount of virtuosity (to put it moder-
ately) at his command. He never aimed at mere
effect, but seemed to plunge into the inner-
most meaning of whatever music he happened to
be interpreting, exhibiting all its details and
expressing its very depths.
“ There is in Brahms’ playing,” said the
violinist Itemenyi, “ that concentrated fire,
what I may call the fatalistic energy and
precision of rhythm, which prophesy the
artist...”
But Brahms, according to Miss May, was
essentially composer.
The mistress of his absorbing passion was from
first to last his creative art, to which all else
remained secondary. He never swerved by a
hair’s-breadth from his devotion, but accepted
poverty, disappointment, loneliness, and failure
in the eyes of tne world, with all the strong faith
that was in him, for the sake of this, his true
love. He was never drawn by inclination to his
virtuoso career, to which he submitted only as a
necessity, discarding it as soon as circumstances
allowed
And of Joachim :
Whatever Joachim’s natural creative faculty
may have been, his boundless success as an inter-
preter was fatal to its development... Joachim fol-
lowed his destiny. His career is unparalleled in
the history of musical executive art. It began
when he was eight; it is not closed now that he
is seventy-four. All possibility of his achieving
greatness as a composer—notwithstanding that he
has produced one or two great works—was ex-
cluded by the time he had reached the age of
fourteen.
Creatively, according to Miss May,
Johannes Brahms is the lineal successor of
Bach, and descends from Haydn, Mozart,
and Beethoven, from Schubert, Mendels-
sohn, and Schumann. With tlie methods of
Berlioz arid Wagner his have no affinity.
His existence as an artist was staked on the
vitality of Absolute music. Berlioz con-
ceived the development of music as an illus-
tration, an exposition, of something apart
from music—a poem, a scene, a picture, a
series of emotions. Wagner strove to com-
bine music, with poetry and action in a new
art of drama. But Brahms remained wedded
to pure musical ideas —“ideas, that is to
say, which act upon the nerves, emotions,
intellect of the listener, directly through the
sense of sound, and are not dependent for
their effect upon intermediate mental trans-
lation into images perceptible to the mind’s
eye, the vision of imagination.”
This does not mean that a composer of pure
music never is and never may be pictorial; but
the cases in which he is so are, as it were, acoi- .
dental, and the- pictorial element in a given work
is not of the essence of his art, but is something
added to it—something, moreover, which does
not affect the value of the composition as. a
work of art. A composer of Absolute music may
indeed, and often does, stimulate his''imagination
by recalling a poem, a legend, a scene of nature
or life ; and either of these may leave a more or
less definite impress on his music; whilst a title
or a motto placed above a short pianoforte piece,
au orchestral overture, or, in very few cases, a.
symphony, may sometimes stimulate the hearer’s
appreciation ; but the music is not in such a case
to fie taken as meaning ” this or that in detail.
The composer aims at making his movement a
work of art complete in itself, and relies for his
effects upon his musical thoughts And their treat-
ment as such, though lie.may be willing to let his
hearers know that his fancy was encouraged by
extraneous aid.
Tiie listener may, on the other hand, jf it assist
his enjoyment, attach his own “ meaning” to what
lie hears, but lie must understand that this is rela-
tive to himself only. No one can assure him that
his “ meaning” is right or wrong. The music as
such should stand high above such interpretations,
and, if it is to fulfil its supreme destiny, must
speak directly to the soul in its own infinite lan-
guage of sound, infinite just because it is capable
of transcending the defined objects of sight.
Here a difficult subject is treated lucidly.
One may infer then, that the Pure Music of
Brahms is designed as a sound-pattern analo-
gous to the colour-pattern which we have
learned to dissociate from a picture. But it
is clear from other passages that Brahms
paid no less attention to the subjective con-
tent of Ins music than to the objective form :
it may be argued that he united the school of
Schumann with the school of Mendelssohn.
But always he sought expression in terms of
sheer music—limnixed with baser matter or
other The process is intelligible and the
method just. So we reach a conception of
each art attaining in its own way a common
goal of emotion.
THE WALLUN.
IT is bv the road of excess',’’ said William
Blake, “that we enter the palace of
wisdom ’’—or words to that effect. On
this balmy August afternoon it do&s not
seem to matter if quotation is inexact, -Flake
said other things too. He said the afternoon
is so bland and blue-eyed that they do not seem
to matter either. Possibly Mr. Swinburne will
remember when he comes to make his division of
respectable English reason and imagination from
furious Celtic fever and fancy. It is the finer art
of life to have other people remember things for
you, and yourself to keep on forgetting.. keep on
forgetting...
But wallow. Possibly once a month is medicinal.
We have just emerged. Quite unpbilosophically,
it appears; and actuated merely by a desire to do
business in the common shop-keeping way, Our
Respected Advertiser ( vide m/m)-sent round what
seemed to be a couple, of dozen American novels.
When the package was opened several of the
novels began to sing out that they were not
“ recent ” according to the circulating-library
standard, which decides that yesterday’s book is
decidedly not to-day’s, and that last month’s may
as well be last century’s when the ladies, with
eager eyes and unlimited time, come flocking.
Not applying the circulating-library standard
too rigorously, we have picked from the wallow
eight books, which have each something vital to
recommend them, and winch accordingly are
passed on for enchantment ofthe baekblaeks. For
the competition-flction-wallow there is nothing
like a good detective story ; but good detective
stories are rara nantes in the vast whirlpool of
printed paper. Next best and kindred are good
sensational stories—stories of realistic adventure
that read with a rattle and shut with a bang.
America does provide these occasionally ; and
America has developed lately a specialty which
shows how American pluck overcomes industrial
obstacles, with or without sentimental help.
Merwin’s The Road-Builders is that kind of book
—a book which realises industry in literature, and
yields at second-hand the thrilling excitement of
battle.- It tells how an American railroad was
built to beat competition, under what difficulties,
with what ingenious devices, amid what contend-
ing forces of capital. It holds the American
spirit, ambitious, enterprising, persistent, yet
good-natured in the middle battle, and valuing
the battle rather than victory : a book stimulat-
ing, encouraging, and absorbing.
A Yellow Journalist, hy Miriam Michelson, is
another yarn of struggle in the field of Pulitzer
and Hearst, where “ beats ” are all important,
and an Australian daily would be the laughing-
stock of Newspaper How. Miss Michelson is one
of the most energetic writers now in the field.
She .pulses vitality, and her speed in the multi-
plication of breathless incidents is scarcely sur-
passed by Arthur Trait). Her ethics for the
purposes of the story are those of her craft in her
environment, and appal; but there is no doubt
of her power to convey excitement, which is the
other thing needful for the wallow’s purpose.
Mr. Wjngrare , Millionaire , is by E. P. Oppen-
heitn, who has a talent and an audience of his
own. Sometimes his facts are too flat and his
sentiment too flaccid to be bothered with; but
generally speaking he is a little below Richard
Marsh, and a little above Max Pemberton. In
the new story he translates Monte Cristo to
London society—with another revenge to work out,
other millions to spend, and another hard heart
to be softened by the divine lubricant of woman.
He begins better than he- ends, but his ending is
set for him by conventional taste : a Hero must
Hug.
The House of a Thmsrmd Candles, by Mere"
Nicholson, owes partly to its title and its .
pre-eminence as one of the “ best-selling books, ’
and the interest is fainter than in some of the
others upon our little list. Still, it has an interest
of curiosity about buried treasure and under-
ground passages, and about the way in which these
are handled or footled ; and one may wallow with
it sedately.
Ben Blair is touched with the melodramatic;
and since it is not comic the epithet conveys in-
feriority, for comic melodrama, like E. W.
O’Sullivan’s, is a high and grateful form of art.
And in its melodrama there is an odour of falsity,
a touch of artificiality, a suspicion of fake : which
is a word that Bm Blair suggests as a contraction
oifactorti, the devil of the machine, as compared
with honest hand-made, man-made: though
A. C. Haddon doubtinglv links it with fetish,
from Portuguese feitico, Lat. faetitius. Bey Blair
is a trifle factitious, made for the market ; though
the fibre of the material is sound enough—Dakota
plains, an indomitable cowboy, an effete cityman,
and a wavering girl. There is so much material,
well used, that the story comes in the first class
of factory products : one gains inspiration from
its manhood, though the man wears a buskin.
Then The Delude, by l). G. Phillips.- In most
ways, this is the best of Phillips's books : it has
the best shape, the strongest personality : it is
independent of Norris, and in aphoristic ways
suggestive of Lorimer. A quick sketch of that
Amor, high finance which is so low, with an
original picture of an imaginary controller break-
ing the ring of magnates. Love and Wall-street
anew. In a sensational book-buyer’s eye there
is n’ t enough of it for the money : you run through
quickly. But every page counts, and so can be
read twice over.
The Man on the Box , by Harold MacGrath, is a
last year’s book ; but it remains one of the best of
the new Araer. novels—with just enough excite-
ment to season it, just enough love to sauce it,
and a basis of gaiety throughout.
J*
Here then, in response to a demand for “fiction
that will please a tired man on Sunday, or
brighten a woman’s monotonous task,” is a choice
of books that “ once you start you can’t stop.”
MEMORANDA
Literary Eccentrics , by John Fyvie (Lond.,
Constable ; 12s.- (id. net), is written agreeably
It tabes the old accounts of fantastic figures like
Beokford and Thomas Pay—the latter almost for-
gotten ; but, oticp known, unforgettable—and
reduces them to chapters in a history' of Eleven.
The others are Thos. Aniory, Landor, Hazlitt,
Crabb Robinson, Chas. Babbage, Jerrold, George
Wither, Mandeville, and James I—a quaint
tribe. Necessarily Mr. Fy vie omits much, but he
tells much that is diverting to read, and preserves
a good deal of the savour of his subjects. His
own attitude is non-committal; his book would
have been better if a dash of sympathetic enthu-
siasm had replaced his tone of mild superiority.
Hazlitt’s “Well, I’ve had a happy life” is perfectly
intelligible, and Mr. Fyvie. shows his limitations
when he adds that “A perusal of his biography
does not altogether give that impression.” For,
you see, it is Hazlitt’s notion of happiness that is
in question ; not Fyvie’s. Occasionally the com-
mentator’s information is deficient, as, for example,
in his doubtful reference to Rogers’s publish-
ing enterprises, which repaid him surprisingly
well. Sometimes his phrase is inexact: "he had
built for himself, like Coleridge’s Kubla Khan, a
succession of lordly pleasure-houses.” The book
will please elderly lawyers and literary doctors,
and. will provide moral lessons for an earnest
clergy. The publishers have helped it with a
cunning form and portraits well printed, and the
title is fortunate.
Big sales in American fiction will presently
mean reduced prices for readers. The Idol. 50c.
American book is sold in Australia for 3s. 6d.,
sometimes with the omission of a gilt top, some-
times precisely similar. And not all of the
books are surplus stock, to be explained on the
dumping theory. The Mayhew Publishing Co.,
having acquired a new book by C. F. Pidgin
(author of Quincy Adams Sawyer, which sold nearly
500,000 copies), is reducing the price to 50c. for
the same class of book, in anticipation or in hope
of another half-million sale.
Dr. Roth issues his last report as Q. Protector
of Aborigines, thus completing ten years’ valuable
labour. Q. Govt, will continue to issue his ethno-
graphical bulletins.
Frederick Wedmore, for nearly thirty years art
critic of Lond. Standard, collects in Whistler and
Others (Lond., Pitman ; Gs. net) impressions and
judgments of two-score artists and more. The
essay on Whistler occupies some thirty pages:
the remainder of the book prints or reprints
gleanings from the whole field of art. Wedmore
is an independent critic,'careful in style, measured
in phrase, and his opinions are worth pondering.
In nearly every case they leave a great deal to be
be said : many of the articles are sketches merely.
Yet we do not find one in which there is not
something said that was worth saying—not one
which does not make some addition, whether
little or less, to the fund of artistic thought.
Of Whistler, Wedmore is whole-hearted
admirer. He asks what reasons prompted W.
to the exercise of mediums so numerous, and
to the acquisition of the various technical skill
those mediums demanded; and he finds two
reasons: one Whistler’s extraordinarily deep
artistic sense of the appropriate % -Jhe other, his
enjoyment of change.
It was not for nothing that his emblem was the butter-
fly. The “ soul of things,” if you like ; but at least asoul
inconstant, transitory; flitting here, flitting there ; and
so alive.
In the preface, Wedmore talks scandal of his
English audience, and this fragment is an example
of his thoughtful writing-;
It would v-~ a satisfaction to suppose that as Time
, ortunities increased, there was amongst
English public some appreciable progress in
uFtSful perception and in sincere and personal enjoy-
ment of the things of Art. Honestly, I cannot a’“r that
this satisfaction is mine. There is something in our race
that makes it a difficulty to extract either from the
admitted bourgeois or the “ cultivated ” person vtho ha-
rushed to Siena or the Lombard plains, any judgment
upon Art comparable irf sagacity or clearness with that
of the first artisan you may collar in the first French
provincial Museum.
Little can those of us to whom Art is a part of Being
welcome the parrot-like repetition of admiration scholas-
tically gained for some performance whose appeal may be
of History or Antiquity, hut is not of Beamy at A-
Enjoyment, were it genuine, would be displayed much
oftener in face of the Art that records something of the
full and varied Life we know. It would be given ill more
generous measure to the fresh and individual vision oi
the common land and every-day skies, of country and of
town, of hill and Coast—to the pourtrayal, with the
resources of the newer technique, of the characters, the
charms, the pleasant faults even, of the people who move
us to-day.
But I do not .discern in- average English folk any
encouraging growth of sensitiveness to the vision of
these things. Have they their eyes on the real object?
Have they their fingers—l sometimes ask myself—on the
real pulse ?
This is the demaud of the Superior Person, yet
even the demand of-the Superior Person should
be met occasionally.
N. Y. Bookmm refers to a remarkable book
on “The Unity of Origin of Language,"
written by Troinbetti —an Italian student and
almost universal linguist', who comes to the con-
clusion that “The languages of Europe, Asa.
Africa and Oceania are related and haveayeom-
mon origin. Moreover, since I have recognised
evident connections between the different Ameri-
can languages which I have been able to study up
to now, and since these same languages offer
distant relations with those of Eastern Asia, I d»
not believe that I can hesitate to proclaim that
there is one unique origin of all languages.’ In
the Oceanic tongues of Australia and Polynesia
Trombetti finds relation with the Semitic
tongues. “Thus, all the languages of the so-
called Ancient World form two groups, the Euro
pe;m-Asia-Oceanic group and the African group:
and these, as said above, are not. irreducible-
The link between them is to be found, according
to Trombetti, in the Caucasian and the 'Biscayan
dialects.”
A.C.S.

Towns’ Great Victory.
A TRIUMPH for The FREEMAN and WALLACE
Electro-Medical Curative Treatment.
GEORGE TOWNS, Champion Sculler of the World.
WE publish here the testimony and photograph of GEORGE
TOWNS, the great Champion Sculler of the World, who,
from a small beginning, by dauntless effort and determination,
attained the fame he holds to-day. The world rings with his praise,
but what would his position be were it not for the splendid health
he possesses? Without health a man’s effort is nullified.
It is only recently that Towns profited by the advice tendered him,
when in a doubtful state of health, run down by arduous training, fatigued
by the great sculling tasks imposed upon him. Having a knowledge of
the wonderful skill of the FREEMAN and WALLACE MEDICAL
SPECIALISTS, he visited them, and took a course of their Electro-
Medical Treatment, and in a few weeks felt a new man in health and
strength. Here is his testimony, added to many others given by brilliant
exponents of the athletic art who have passed through the hands of the
FREEMAN AND WALLACE SPECIALISTS.
Dear Sirs,—
/ have from time to time noticed
that you have successfully treated
many athletes in the athletic world,
and have often heard your Electro-
Medical methods of treatment eulo-
gised by men of note as being most
effective. Having need of medical ad-
vice, I, upon recommendation, under-
went a course of your Electro-Medical
treatment. / soon felt better and
strong, and perfectly confident that /
could stand any strain hard and con-
tinuous training might impose. / used
your Medical Battery. It is the Best
Electrical Appliance / have used.
(Signed) GEORGE TOWNS.

Gloomy Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pagan Dirge.
Sweet Love, that yearned and art so early dead !
Dear Youth, that joyed and art so swiftly fled !
Fair Hope, that sang and art so surely sped !
Hail and farewell!
Our hearts throb faintly, like a distant bell
That tolls through mist and sleet the funeral
knell
Of Love that fed on Faith, and starved and fell,
And sobbed and died.
Youth passes. There is naught on earth beside
To bring us joy while yet a space we bide,
Chained to this clay. Gloom heaped on gloom.
Outside
The long, dark night!
Fond Hope, that came in spring-time, star-be-
dight,
Giopes now, all crippled, in this fading light;
So haggard-eyed, whoso eyes were once so bright,
Shaming the sun !
Our friends fade into shadows, one by one ;
All mired and drabbled are the dreams we spun ;
We are sore spent, our day yet barely done,
And scant of breath !
We pray thee seek us, soft and silent Death,
Thou strong deliverer of the stained and sad !
O, haste thy pace that thus long loitereth,
Lest this slow-crawling misery make us mad 1
MAOEILAND.
FRANK MORTON.
Sad Memories.
I can remember still, and still without one drop
Of weakness in mine eye,
The brown bark farm-house on the hill, the wind-
mill top
Slow girding at the sky.
The six-foot water dam, its sides thick packed
with hoofs •
Of many thirsty sheep,
The clover and the pinlc-lipp’d brides, the strutted
roofs
Of other farms asleep.
The stack of rain-stained hay, the stack of hay
yet sweet,
The double milking shed,
Its arch sunk inwards, pierced and black with all
the heat
Of fifty summers fled.
And I remember, plainly too, the she-oak tall,
Whose fingers in the night,
Whenever sudden zephyrs blew, took hold the
wall,
And shook my heart with fright;
For I was but an infant then, and full of fear
And weird imaginings,
Long routed since, past human ken, to leave me
clear
For manhood’s greater things.
And there it was my father died (as good a soul
As ever trod life’s path);
Death took him, in his chair, beside the burning
bole
Of wood upon the hearth.
And there my mother heard him speak, one ev’n-
ing-end—
Long after he was dead—
Whereat she kissed me on the cheek; then,
reverend.
Lay silent in her bed.
I hate the farm-house on the hill, its wind-mill top
Slow girding at the sky ;
I think, and think upon it still, without one drop
Of weakness in mine eye.
N.S.W.
HUGH McCRAE.
The Outcast.
He walked the street from end to end
With hollow, listless, leaden tread ;
Without a home, without a friend,
And nought whereon to lay his head.
And yet there cheered him as he trod
The pavements dark and drear and dumb—
A faith in man, a trust in God,
A buoyant hope of good to come.
Door after door he tried in vain
With wounded knuckles beating low;
The sleepers woke and slept again,
Or named him fool and bade him go.
No friendly fingers turned the lock,
He heard no cheerful voices hail;
It seemed his doom to knock and knock,
And fail, and fail, and always fail.
A late moon silvered land and sea,
And made on ; street lie ghostly white;
Two homeless things—a wind and he—
Went seeking shelter through the night.
Yet this he felt in spite of all—
Behind some door as yet untried
In humble cot, or house, or hall,
There dwelt a friend who would not chide.
A friend with goodly wine to greet,
And words more warm than wine to pour ;
Yet, in that long and friendless street,
Where should he find the willing door ?
“ Knock on and on, thou wanderer wan ! ”
The voice within his bosom cried—
*' Thy trust in God, thy faith in man—
They shall not fail nor be denied.”

RODEBIG QUINN.
“ George,” from a remote corner -
At Port Hedland (W.A.) the Afghans with their camels
have completely annexed the carrying trade. On the
Port Hedland to Marble Bar road they cut rates until
they forced the white man right’out of the business, and
as soon as Dost Mahommed had the monopoly, up Went
the rates. Storekeepers are now at the mercy of the
Afghan. There arh other alien evils. Hedland Advocate
(2/6/’O6), in advocating that the Chinese and Japanese
should be segregated in a special quarter, says
“To some people hereabouts (P.H.), a cleanly-dressed
Chinaman is as good a neighbor as any white man, but
there is no gainsaying the fact that the majority of the
present occupants of Japtown would do any health-
loving community a service by dying immediately,
and such a dirty set of buildings as there exists constitutes
a menace to the public health and progress of that part
of the town.”
A bakery in the town, conducted by a Chinaman, was
recently inspected, and the Chinese baker was found to
be “ suffering from chronic eczema.” The health officer
also condemned the premises, which were found to be in
a filthy and disgusting state. The assertion is freely
made Port Hedland way that Chinese and Japanese are
smuggled in anywhere from Broome down to Perth off
the boats (worked by Chinese and Malays) trading be-
tween Perth and Singapore.
“ Gip ” : Interesting, here, to watch the
march of the animals to the slaughtering yards
when a beast is about to be killed. (Why is a
bullock always called a beast, by the way ? Some-
body says to one, “Killed to-day?” and one says
to somebody “ Yes.” “Sheep?” “No, a beast.”
And a bullock isn’t half so beasty as s’m’other
things.) From the moment the bullock is run in
the dogs, &c., are on the watch, and directly the
butcher goes for his knives and cleaver the pro-
cession starts. ' the lead, a dignified black dog,
accompanied by i lumpishly playful yellow pup
that bites inanely at the black dog’s mouth.
Next, a stumpy-tailed terrier of aristocratic ap-
pearance. After him, three black cattle dogs—
black-and-tan mother, with two waddling pups.
One very hairy and aged dog walking painfully.
A three-legged cat. Lastly, two ordinary cats,
one sow, seven suckers, and four large pigs. Not
of the procesh, but coming from another direc-
tion, 25 or so magpies. The dogs sit round and
watch the proceedings with interest. The cats
wait on the fence. The pigs grunt expectantly at
a civil distance from the dogs. The magpies "are
right there on the spot. They all get their share
of the offal. It is gruesome how they seem to
know in advance. If ever, in a future state, lam
reincarnated as a bullock, and I see approach me
a procession of a black dog, a pup, a terrier, three
cattle dogs, hairy dog, tripod cat, two cats with a
leg at each corner, medium pig, seven small
pigs, four bloated pigs, and 25 magpies—when I
see that ghoulish gathering coming my way I shall
charge the fence, and either break through or
commit suicide. And if I break through I will
trample on the four bloated pigs and the tripod
cat, anyhow.
“Gip”: It is said that foxes have nearly
wiped out the rock wallaby on Monaro. I know
they have practically done for the turkeys.
Noticed, the other afternoon, a beautiful flock of
turkeys on the wrong side of the creek. There
had been a smart downfall, and the creek was up.
The turkeys, unable to cross, camped on the bank
for the night. Result, next morning, 18 dead
turkeys, flesh almost untouched, but blood sucked.
Only four escaped. Foxes must climb well. They
have been known here to enter securely closed
yards and almost noiselessly kill a dozen fowls.
Unable to sleep one moonlight night,' lately, I
peeped out and saw a fox prowling round the trees
in which the fowls roost. He had accounted for
two hens, yet the dogs slumbered peacefully
under the humpy, and they are said to be good
watch dogs, too.

WILL CRIMINALS ESCAPE DETECTION
BY REMARKABLE DISCOVERY OF
ROCHESTER SCIENTIST}
Man Increases Height and Cheats Bertillon System,
HOW ANYONE MAY GROW TALL.
United States Government Official Offers Himself as
Proof that the Height can be Increased.
SEVERAL INCHES MAY EASILY BE ADDED BY
MR. MINGES’ NEW SYSTEM.—HOW
IT IS DONE.
(Special Correspondence.)
Brighton, N.Y., February 4.—At last, after
years of study and endless scientific experiments
for increasing the height of those who are so un-
fortunate as to be short in stature, a Rochester
scientist has solved the problem, and given to the
world a greater gift than Edison’s electric light;
Marconi’s wireless telegraphy; or the Bell tele-
phone ; for what can be more inconvenient or pro-
ductive of more discomfort, than to be short ? The
short man or woman is always at a disad-
vantage; in business, society, on the stage or
rostrum. The great orators, statesmen, gene-
rals, presidents, have rarely been short men.
l
'/A
//A
1
Mr. K. Leo Minges, a Rochester
Scientist, who Makes Short
People Grow Tall.
Not that the short men are not just as brainy, but
their stature is against them. The great society
leaders are not short women. It seems there is no
longer any excuse for anyone being short, if you will
just follow out Mr. K. Leo Minges’ discovery for
increasing the height. Mr. Minges himself once
knew the disadvantages, the trials and tribulations
that beset the path of the short man. He deter-
mined to grow tall. He tried everything he could
think of, and everything anybody could suggest.
He delved deep into the study of anatomy and
physiology. Finally, when he was almost
ready to give up in despair, he discovered one
of Nature’s marvellous secret laws a law
by which the human body can actually be made
to grow. Mr. Minges tried his new discovery on
himself; it worked like a charm, and to-day Mr.
Minges is 6ft. 2in. tall, and as straight as an arrow.
He next solicited others to try the system, and
behold their height was also increased. He adver-
tised far and wide"to get persons extremely short
in stature to try his system and put it to the most
severe tests possible. The results were simply
astounding. The following extract from the New
York Herald shows what can be done: —
“Post Office Inspector Williams says the
Bertillon system of identifying criminals is
a failure; and he offers his own person as
proof of his assertion. Mr. Williams was
measured by the Bertillon system, and went
to work to prove how little use it was in
detecting felons. He was weighed at the time of
his measurement, and tipped the scales close to
one hundred and ninety pounds ; his height was
recorded at five feet two inches. Now his weight
is one hundred and fifty pounds, and he is five feet
four inches in height. The change was brought
about by training he undertook. The ability to
increase his height was the one thing that broke
down the certainty of the Bertillon .system. It
was known that a stooping thief might learn to
walk erect; one with cross eyes have his optics
made straight; or a man with birthmarks be freed
from these signs of identity ; but when it was dis-
covered that several inches could be added to the
height of a suspected man, the detectives who
relied upon the Bertillon system were thrown into
confusion.”
Mr. Minges has written a remarkable book,
which fully explains the secrets and the science of
increasing the height. It tells how he himself
grew tall; and how many of his friends have done
so. He sold the copyright of his book, to the
Cartilage Company, of Brighton, and. thi3 com-
pany proposes to give away 5000 copies simply
for advertising purposes. But there is little
doubt but that the returns which will eventually
come from this substantial kind of advertising
will in the end prove very profitable, as nearly
every short person who sees the book will want
a copy, and there will be a perpetual demand for
it. Until the 5000 copies are exhausted, any
person who wishes to increase his or her height
may get a copy absolutely free, simply by
addressing the Cartilage Company, Dept, 52 u
Brighton, N.Y., U.B.A. Please do not write
through mere idle curiosity, as the book is an
expensive production, and is offered tree only to
those who wish to make practical application ot
the information contained therein. _

SACKING A CIVIL SERVANT (IN N.S.W.).
Uriah Heep Carruthers: “You see, Mr. Oliver, you should have been 'umble. You shouldn’t have quarrelled. ‘They were acting against
the public interest,’ you say. Well, you shouldn’t have quarrelled. I never quarrel. Be 'umble like me. Swallow
anything. Be meek and ’umble, my dear sir.”

Correspondents to Tin: Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
in rite inattent ion.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
ojlicials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 16, 1906.
THE DEBT PROBLEM-FEDERAL, STATE,
LOCAL AND MISCELLANEOUS.
In delivering his Budget speech, a week or two
ago, Bigjohn Forrest said little that was really
satisfactory so far as concerned the most important
part of his subject. He doubtless did his best,
but it wasn’t a very brilliant best; it might have
been worse, yet it shouldn’t have been so bad.
The question which rises most conspicuously now
on the horizon of Australian finance is—what is
to happen after 1910, when the first ten years of
Federation, the ten years for which the Braddon
clause was fixed in the Constitution, expire ? For
that period of ten years the Australian Govern-
ment is required by the Constitution to return to
the States three-fourthsof the Customs and Excise
revenue in order to euable them to pay the in-
terest on their monumental public debts, or else
—if the Commonwealth takes over those State
debts before the ten years expire—it must
set aside three-fourths of the tariff revenue
to provide for the said debts. The Braddon
clause was originally intended to be a permanent
feature of the Constitution, but, at the instance of
George Reid, it was reduced to a mere casual and
fleeting obligation, which has now less than five
years to run. When it runs out—unless arrange-
ments are made in the interim for the transfer of
the State debts to the Commonwealth, or some
other agreement is arrived at to keep the States
out of the poor-house—certain really amazing
things may happen. The Australian Parliament,
if it feels so inclined, may retain all the Customs
and Excise revenue, and have a good time with
it, and leave the State Governments to repudiate
their debts, or to attempt the impossible task of
raising another £7,000,000 or so by direct taxa-
tion. This would be a sufficiently amazing situa-
tion for anybody. And, so far, nothing has been
done in the way of an arrangement. The early
attempts in that direction found most of the State
Governments loud and bumptious, full of jealousy
of the Federal authority, full of suspicion that
“Our State ” might lose in the deal, and inclined to
hang on to the dignity of having each its separate
debt, and to the pomp of each holding separate
communication through its separate Agent-
General with its separate Cohen. The expira-
tion of the Braddon clause looked then more
remote than it really was, and civility seemed
uncalled for. Now the expiration of the Braddon
clause looks nearer than it really is. and the State
Governments, though still uncivil, are feverishly
anxious.
* * * •
About half-a-dozen schemes have been put
forward by the parochial crowd for meeting the
difficulty on true parochial principles—that is to
say, without consolidating the liabilities and
abolishing the separate State debts and the
separate State Cohens. They all revolve round
some device or another whereby the Common-
wealth is to return to the States a sum about
equal to what they now receive—say £7,000,000
per annum—but there the resemblance ceases.
No two States have the same idea as to how the
£7,000,000 should be divided, and a cat-fight is
harmony compared with the dissensions on this
point. Some of the suggestions are as follows :
(1) That the £7,000,000 should be allotted according to
the amount which each State contributes to the Federal
revenue— i.e., that a State which supplies one-seventh of
the Federal revenue should receive £1,000,000, being one-
seventh of the Federal subsidy. This idea is dismissed
without a hearing. It means the eternal labor of keeping
vastly-complicated and expensive accounts between the
States, and despite all the labor and all the square miles
of paper used, the accounts can’t be kept accurately.
Moreover, this method only gives each State its share,
and the air is full of schemes whereby every State is to
get more than its share.
(2) That the £7,000,000 should be divided according
to population. This idea is received with applause by
Tasmania and S.A., is regarded coldly by N.S. W., Victoria,
and Queensland, and is hailed in YVestralia with language
such as is commonly used when oxen are going uphill.
The reason is shown in the following table, which ex-
plains how much Customs and Excise revenue per inha-
bitant each State is expected to contribute to the
Commonwealth this year, and, roughly, how much each
would get back per inhabitant if a £7,000,000 Federal
subsidy were divided according to population (population
estimated at 4,100,000) :
Estimated Net Tariff
Customs and Proposed Contribution
Excise Federal to Federal
Revenue Subsidy Government
per Head. per Head. per Head.
1900-7.
YVestralia .. £3 12 5 £1 14 2 £1 18 3
N.S. YVales .. 2 4 6 1 14 2 10 4
Queensland .. 2 3 0 1 14 2 8 10
Victoria .. 218 1 14 2 76
Tasmania .. 1 17 1 1 14 2 2 11
S. Australia .. 1 16 3 1 14 2 2 1
Average ..£2 4 2 £1 14 2 10 0
YVestralia denounces such a division as a matter of course.
Though its revenue is still abnormal, it is dropping fast
to the N.S.YV. and Queensland level, but YVestralia fails
to realise this, and reckons it will remain an abnormal
State for ever. The next three States also reckon that
they would lose by this method ; when YVestralia gets
down to their revenue level, they consider that the
tour States at the head of the list would be plundered for
the benefit of Tasmania and S. Australia. The two
last-named States approve cordially, but that fact doesn’t
help in the least to popularise the scheme.
* * « *
Then there are other suggestions fur dividing
the proposed £7,000,000 Federal subsidy as fol-
lows :
(3) That there should be fixed, in perpetuity, an allow-
ance of so much to each State, the division to be made
upon some rule-of-thumb method, partly on the basis of
present population, partly on the basis of present contribu-
tions to the Federal revenue, partly on general principles,
whatever these may be. The answer conies in the shape
of bitter objurgations from Queensland, N.S.YV., and
YY’estralia, which declare that, though they only repre-
sent a little more than half the population of the Com-
monwealth to-day, a time must come when, bytt-eason of
their great size and resources, they will represent five-
sixths of it, and no division, made on the basis of things
as they are now, could be accepted as a permanent
arrangement.
(4) That the surplus should be divided on a sort of
sliding scale, altering from year to year as conditions
change. That is to say, that there should be two
impossibilities instead of one: (a) the impossibility of
getting the State Governments to agree on a scale to
begin with, and (b) the impossibility of getting them to
agree on the principle according to which it shall slide.
And amid all this chaos Bigjohn Forrest,
Treasurer of the Commonwealth, trying to follow
the line of least resistance, comes in with his
proposal, which is as follows :
(5) That after 19X0 any new tariff duties imposed shall
be the property of the Commonwealth.
(6) That for another ten years (1910 to 1920) the State
Governments shall be credited with three-fourths of the
proceeds of the existing duties on this basis : An account
to be kept of the average amount which each State con-
tributes yearly in Customs and Excise between now and
1910, and each State to receive three-fourths of that
amount each year until 1920, if the total sum to be
divided will run to it.
(7) The Australian Government to set about taking
over the State debts ; to keep a separate debt accoun t
for each State, and to pay each State’s interest out of its
subsidy. If the subsidy, in any case, isn’t enough to
cover a State’s interest bill, the State to be requested to
make good the deficit.
(8) After 1920 the whole row about the division of the
subsidy to begin again, this scheme being only a post-
ponement of the trouble after all.
* * * *
Among these various propositions, the one pro-
Eounded by Treasurer Forrest is probably the
est. But all of them are mere makeshfts, or
temporary evasions, and not one really solves the
problem. Said problem is to find a means whereby
the whole of the State debts will be at once taken
over by the Commonwealth with a view to their
consolidation and gradual repayment, and all
Federal subsidies to the States, and all keeping
of separate accounts between States, be abolished.
And here the Australian Government comes in
severe concussion with these other problems :
(1) Under the Constitution the Commonwealth can
only take over the State debts as they existed on Ist
January, 1901, and that proviso is old and dead, and out
of date ; the debts of 1901 are so overlaid by new trans-
actions that many of them are now as hard to find as
Ludwig Leichhardt’s bones. Therefore, the first essen-
tial is an amendment of the Constitution, which should
be passed this session and submitted to the popular vote
at the coming election, giving the Australian Govern-
ment power to take over the whole State debts by pro-
clamation, as they exist at the date of the proclamation,
and to control all future State borrowings.
(2) The State debts ranged, when Hali/s N.S.YV.
statistics were last published, from £42 per inhabitant
in Victoria to £79 per inhabitant in Queensland. If these
liabilities were taken over bodily and lumped together
heroically, the average debt would be about £5B per
head, whereby Victoria would lose £l6 per head, Tas-
mania about £5 per head, N.S.YV. about £2, and the
other three States would gain by passing a portion of
their debt on to Victoria, Tasmania, and N.S.YV. There-
fore Victoria and Tasmania object bitterly, and N.S.YV.
objects a good deal.
(3) If the State and Local Government debts Were taken
over by the Commonwealth there would still be a dis-
crepancy, though not so large a one. These combined
debts range from £56 per head in Victoria and Tasmania
to £BO per head in Queensland and S. Australia. If the
Commonwealth assumed them in one huge transaction
the average debt per head all over Australia would be
about £64. That is to say, Victoria and Tasmania would
find their liabilities increased by about £8 per inhabitant,
and N.S.YV. by about £4 ; while the other three States
would be gainers.
(4) Clause 51 of the Federal Constitution lays it down
that all States must be taxed alike, and unless this clause
is also altered, it is impossible for the Commonwealth to
lay any special imposts on the States that are relieved of
a specially large debt, so as to make them carry at least
part of their own extra burden, instead of shuffling the
surplus on to the States which only transfer a small debt.
(5) By way of further confusion, it must be remembered
that the existing debts don’t, and won’t, represent a
fixed amount per head. If the population of one State
increases rapidly, a debt which is now £BO per inhabitant,
may soon be only £5O, while in the same time the popu-
lation of another State may grow so slowly that its debt
of £56 per head may onlj- drop to £52. Also, the dis-
crepaneies in the amount of revenue per head which the
States yield to the Federal Treasury would, in some
cases, help to counterbalance the discrepancies in the
debts, and in other cases they would make things worse.
And this is also an unfixable discrepancy, which shifts
round as the circumstances of the States alter.
* » » *
There is a vast difficulty in finding a reason-
ably fair method of debt consolidation
all these conflicting problems to consider. Yet it
is essential that the consolidation should be done.
By way of slightly simplifying the problem, this
paper would suggest that power should be
acquired, by an amendment of the Constitution,
to take over, not only the State, but also the Local
Government debts—the debts of municipalities,
shires, divisional boards, water and sewerage and
road and harbor boards, irrigation trusts, cemetery
trusts, and all local bodies in one sweeping act.
By this widely inclusive policy the debts of the
different States would be brought much more
nearly to a level, and they could be taken over in
a lump with much less apparent injustice. Also
the idea would rouse enthusiasm in all manner of
embarrassed quarters; it would ensure the warm
support of aldermen and other dubious characters,
and would make it much more easy to carry an
amendment whereby not only would all debts be
transferred to the Commonwealth, but the Com-
monwealth would also acquire the right to insist
that future borrowings, State and local, should
be made through it and with its permission. The
inclusion of the debts of local bodies would only
increase the total of the Federal burden by
about £20,000,000, and that isn’t much in a trans-
action which, in any case, would run to
£240,000,000. And by taking over the local
debts, which in some cases carry as much as 8 per-
cent. interest, and converting them gradually into
Commomvealth securities at 3 or 3£ percent., a
memorable saving would be effected.
There would still remain the discrepancy
between Victoria’s transferred debt of £56 per
inhabitant and the £BO per inhabitant of Queens-
land and S. Australia, and the smaller discre-
pancies in between, to say nothing of the fact
that there are at present equally bad dif-
ferences in the amount of revenue per head
which the different States furnish to the
Federal Treasury. There are three possible ways
of dealing with this matter. One is the heroic
method of lumping things, and taking no notice
of the discrepancies. This is the method which
The Bulletin would like to see adopted, but it
seems to be too heroic for general acceptance.
Another idea is to figure out on an average of a
certain number of years the amount of revenue
per inhabitant which each Statu is likely to con-
tribute to the Federal treasury ; to ascertain how
much interest per inhabitant cite Federal treasury
will have to pay on behalf of each State, and thus-
estimate as closely as possible which States will
gain, and how much they will ga.n, at the expense
of their neighbors through the general lumping
of the debts. Then it might be demanded that
these States should assign, for, say, ten
years, a certain amount of their State revenues to
the Commonwealth to reduce the discrepancy, or
by another amendment of the Constitution, the
Australian Parliament might be authorised to
specially tax these States to a similar extent for
ten years. (S. Australia, about the most hopeless
ease of all, could be required to hand over the
Northern Territory to make up part of its deficit.)
After ten years the subject of the discrepancies
might fairly be dropped. In that time many
things would happen. The population centres
would probably shift. The balance of wealth
would shift. The amount per head which each
State contributed to the Federal revenue would
change. A steady process of conversion of the
debts into a uniform Federal stock would some-
what alter the incidence of the burden, for there
is a great difference in the relief to be acquired
through converting the Westralian debt (interest,
say. £3 Bs. 7d. per cent.) and the S. Australian
debt (interest, say, £3 15s. Id. per cent.). This
method, like that proposed by John Forrest, has
the drawback that it has a ten-years term attached
to it, but there is this difference—it proposes to
end the question in ten years; the Forrest idea
is to reopen the problem in ten years and begin
the whole trouble afresh.
* * * *
Then the Federal Treasurer, having taken over
£9,500,000 of new annual liabilities in the shape :,f
interest, and having only saved £7,000,000 in the
shape of State subsidy, would have to do a
demoniac hustle for new sources of revenue. It
would be necessary then for many State taxes to
become .Federal taxes, also the Federal Treasurer
—some one quite different from easy-going
Big John Forrest— would learn economy in a
hard school. And it would be good for him to
learn economy, also it would be good for him to
hustle. And if there had to be a very great exten-
sion of the sphere of Federal influence in the
process of finding the necessary new revenue that
would be good also—in fact, it would be the best
thing of all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Penny Post Proposal.
The Australian Government will surely abandon,
or indefinitely postpone, its penny postage pro-
posal when it is Closely studied in the clear light
thrown upon the financial position by the Budget
debate. Nothing is plainer than that the Com-
monwealth has no superfluous money to spend on
sentimental extravagances. On all sides are
demands for reforms which will cost money. If
the Northern Territory is taken over—and the
Northern Territory should be taken over at
once —the extra cost of Commonwealth adminis-
tration will be at least £lOO,OOO a year.
To make a beginning in naval defence —
and a beginning should be made NOW —will
involve at the least another £lOO,OOO a year. To
fouud a small-arms factory—and the latest possible
date, consistent with a proper regard for Aus-
tralian interests, for doing that is August 16,
1906 —will make a big dip into the Treasury. The
Westralian continental railway is promised. It
will cost money. As the Kanakas are deported,
the loss of sugar revenue derived from the sur-
charge on “ black ” sugar will be great. Meteor-
ology, lighthouses and quarantine shortly come
under Federal control, and all mean expenditure.
Old age pensions—the Government is pledged
to an Australian scheme—will call for some
assistance from the general revenue, even
if special sources are tapped to supply the
bulk of the money. At every point the call is to
spend, spend, spend. Meanwhile, the Common-
wealth has reached perilously close to the limit of
three-fourths of the Tariff revenue which it
can constitutionally spend, and is within measure-
able sight of facing a deficit or new taxation. If
new taxes are asked for to supply old age pen-
sions, to help the military defences, to colonise
the Northern Territory, to build either of the
necessary trans-continental railways, no patriot
will grumble. But to provide the commercial
classes with the luxury of a penny postal system—
that is another matter ! The £300,000 which a
reduction of the postal rates would cost could,
properly applied, settle the problem of the
Northern Territory, or, as an additional vote, put
on a fairly secure basis the defence system. Can
any sensible, practical man say that either of
those two things should give precedence to a
penny post ?
♦
The Defences.
W. M. Hughes, who has been the champion in
Parliament, as Colonel Campbell has been out of
Parliament, of a self-respecting defence system,
moves again in the House of Representatives for
universal compulsory training. The debate has
not yet come to an end, but it is already manifest
that it will proceed on the old familiar lines.
Ministers and members will agree that the Swiss
Militia System “should be adopted,” and will
plead for the acceptance for the present
of some tiny instalment of reform—as “ too
great a change just now would be impracticable.”
And on these grounds the matter will be shelved.
That will happen again and again until a Ministry
is in power which has the courage to effect whole-
hog reform ; and all will then be surprised to find
how easy and popular the task was. It is not
possible to judge the popular view of an effective
reform of the defence system by the popular
opposition which generally greets the piecemeal
and expensive attempts at change put forward
from time to time in the name of military re-
organisation. The country is impatient of a
policy that adds another £lOO,OOO or £200,000
to tiie military vote when all that is pro-
mised in return is some almost invisible altera-
tion of the wretched old system. If, however, a
thorough scheme were put forward—a plan which
guaranteed security so far as human certainty
goes—the public would rise to it, and not grumble
over the extra cost involved. The position can
be illustrated by the difference between paying
3Jd. to insure your house against an outbreak of
fire by a lamp explosion only, and paying fid. to
insure it absolutely against any form of fire. The
31 d. proposition wouldn’t appeal to many. Nor
would a proposal to make it 3|d. and extend the
risk to kerosene stoves. To stretch to fid. might
mean an effort; but if it would represent real
security the effort would be worth while. That
is the essential objection to all the piecemeal
military reforms brought forward to placate the
demand for effective reorganisation ; they are not
worth while.
♦
Protection Advances.
Prime Minister Deakin shows both strength and
sincerity in regard to the fiscal issue. With the
opening of this session he announced that the
“fiscal truce” was ended, and since then good
progress has been made. Regulations have been
framed under the Commerce Act which will
protect the Australian manufacturer against
shoddy importations and thus give him most
valuable assistance in finding a local market
for his honest goods; the Anti-Trust Bill,
which can put a stop to dumping, and to
injurious competition by foreign Combines, has
been industriously pushed on ; the Protection to
the Australian distilling industry has been in-
creased by 200 per cent., and the Protection to
some Australian machinery manufacturers by 100
per cent. That is a good record. It has been sug-
gested that if Deakin pushed on with Protection
this session he would leave himself without a cry
for the general elections, and would help G. 11.
Reid’s “anti-Sosh” absurdity. But, apart
from the fact that Deakin is not the sort
to postpone for party purposes the accomplish-
ment of what he strongly believes in (his main
fault is the difficulty he has in strongly believing
in anything—Protection is an exception) there is
very little in that suggestion. Presuming the
greatest industry and a certain amount of good
luck this session, there will still remain many items
of Protection for next Par liament toattend to —quite
enough Protection to form an issue ; for the Prime
Minister’s idea of Protection, as he explained in
public recently, embraces not only an effective
tariff, but encouragement by bonuses of new
industries and the guardianship of the workers’
interests in all occupations. With that pro-
gramme, the work of Protection will not come
very quickly to an end. There is satisfaction
now in the thought that it has been well begun.
The Blight of the Land Monopolist.
At a Bathurst (N.S.W.) meeting, last week, the
position of the tenant famer was canvassed, and
one speaker declared
the tenant farmers were the worst-conditioned men in
the district. They had not a shilling, and lived in
“humpies” that were a disgrace to the district, while
close by were magnificent mansions belonging to the
landlords. In Bathurst, one man had 30,000 acres ex-
tending right into the town, He did not pay any taxes
to support the town, and the tenants were unfortunate
“ working-bullocks.” This landlord went to England,
Scotland, Ireland, and other places to spend his money.
Which was the truth, and something under the
truth. Bathurst, where men decay and wealth
has forgotten how to accumulate, is a city perish-
ing under the blight of land monopoly. All the
good land of the Bathurst Plains was monopolised
in the early days by the ancestors of the “big
families.” They got it more or less—mostly
less—honestly. In many cases it was
cleared and improved by convict slave-
laboi. The descendants of those first robbers now
pose as a haughty aristocracy, and their sweating,
rack-renting system of dealing with their tenantry
has been carried to such a degree that the district
is being depopulated, the area under cultivation
is dwindling, and an increasing quantity of land
is passing into the care of the Chinese—the only
beings who can pay the rack-rents and live. If the
Australian Parliament could only be sent on a
trip to Bathurst not with the purpose of
finding a Federal capital site, but to in-
vestigate the effects of land monopoly a
progressive land-tax would be almost a cer-
tainty next session. For the big-landlord
system at Bathurst has no good results. The
tenants are either whites who know they are
miserable, or Chinese who don’t know it, but
are miserable. The city, which was once a
flourishing centre, has the dry rot, and can neither
attract new population nor keep the locally-born
younger generation. Even the landlords begin
now' to feel the pinch. Their greedy and suicidal
policy has resulted in much of the land being
over-cropped to the point of exhaustion, the rack-
rented tenant having to take out of it all he could
in order to live. The end will be the iibandon-
mentof the district. Bathurst, the N.S. Wales
South Coast, the Western district of "V ictoria,
the rich Central district in Tasmania, the Queens-
land Darling Downs —they all cry out eloquently
for a tax which will give its quietus to land
monopoly.


A Small Holdings Bill.
VICTORIA is at present doing something with an
Improved Small Holdings Bill. This has nothing
3 . a ii to do with any scheme for breaking up Un-
improved Large Holdings. Rather, it seems to
be a semi-charitable or pauper device, whereby
a small number of men will get very small bits of
land under very mean conditions, while the
Government will comfort its soul, and the House
of Rich Landlords will comfort its stomach—not
having a soul—with the thought that something
is really being done in the way of closer settle-
ment. The Age lately described the leading
features of the Bill thus :
Each allotment is to contain land not exceeding- £l5O
in value, and each holder may be provided with build-
ings, fencing and implements to the amount of another
£l5O. Applicants must be 21 years old, natural born or
naturalised British subjects; unable, by their personal
means, to become possessed of land ; during the previous
six years they must not have been imprisoned for six
months or upwards ; if so required, they will transfer to
the Crown all their property, authorising the Minister to
*ell it to such an extent as he thinks fit, and deduct from
the proceeds the total sum advanced, and pay the balance
to the tenant.
In the first place, tenants must seive six months’ pro-
bation and, under the direction of the foreman, do the
necessary improvement work. The tenant will receive
weekly payments, if he satisfies the foreman, as follows :
For the first six months, 20s. a week ; for the second six
months, 15s. a week; for the third six months, 10s. a
week. From this, however, there will be a deduction, as
rent, of 5 percent, of the capital value of the land.
At the end of six, twelve or eighteen months, if the
tenant’s conduct has been satisfactory, he may, on pay-
ment of registration fees, obtain a conditional purchase
lease giving him 31J years in which to pay the Crown for
the land and make the property his own. The value of
the stock is to be repaid in three years by six half-yearly
instalments, while the sums advanced for buildings and
fences must he repaid in 32 half-yearly instalments
Five per cent, interest has to be paid on all the advances,
&c., &c.
It has been said (this may seem a digression, but
it isn’t one) that man has invented two kinds of
Deity. One variety is conceived to hat e built the
universe on a system which is so well devised
that, once started, the great machine runs itself.
The other kind, believed in by the more ignorant
races, is an imaginary Personage who built a jolt-
ing universe that is only kept from daily wreck
by His violent personal interference. In the same
way, there are two methods of running a country.
One is by so devising the laws that not only will
the country be prosperous and rich, but the wealth
will be widely diffused, and great fortunes and
great estates will be severely discouraged. If a
great fortune does occur, it will break up auto-
matically on the death of the mighty grabber who
got it together. As for the occurrence of great
estates, that will be made impossible by graduated
land taxes and strict limitation of the area which
one individual can hold. Under such a system
land is abundant, and its price is based on its pro-
ductive capacity—not on its artificial scarcity.
Also, under such a system, the man who
want 3 land and hasn’t the money where-
with to buy it, is able to find work at good
wages, and save up in a very reasonable
time enough to give him a start on his own
account. The system provides for no charity
except as regards the sick, the aged, or the young
orphan, and it doesn’t provide for much even as
regards them, for little is needed. And it
includes no condescending fat millionaires reach-
ing down from a great height to lend two-
penn’orth of aid to the poor. Another method is
somewhat akin to the ignorant idea of a violently
interfering Deity who always finds the axle of His
ungreased universe running hot, and always just
saves it from smash by miracles and personal
struggles. It is a policy which does nothing to
prevent a few people monopolising the land while
the landless multitude looks on outside—a policy
of great estates and great fortunes; and
its great evils are violently alleviated in a
small way at long intervals by the action
of some fat and strugglesome politician,
who seizes a few individuals out of the
mass of poverty that the system lias made, and,
with much patronage and charity, gives them a
speck of land apiece—provided they can fulfil 72
conditions, and live up to 22 rules and 116 regula-
tions, and are respectful, and don't expectorate on
the globe. Meanwhile the system remains, and
the heap of trouble remains, and the handful of
people who can be cumbrously and laboriously
relieved on the 31J years instalment plan by a
Small Holdings Act, or the like, makes no appre-
ciable difference. But the wealthy Ton’ person
prefers this method. It represents Charity,
which is a curse of his special invention. The
great land-grabber would much rather give ten
acres, or the value thereof, with much pomp and
condescension, in the name of benevolence, than
have a cold inquiry held as to the illegal methods
by which he acquired the other 100,000 acres, and
be found out. Also, he much prefers to give away
out of his great fortune an occasional copper to the
poor, whom the making of that fortune has caused
to be poor, than to live under a system in which
there would be no poor, no great fortunes, and no
charitable person handing down a farthing or a
brass button from a huge social elevation. All
which reflections tend to the idea that it isn’t an
Improved Small Holdings Act but a Neglected
Large Holdings Act that Victoria really needs,
and in that respect'Victoria doesn’t stand alone
by any means.

Some Impudence from the North.
At the Queensland “conference of local authori-
ties,” Mayor Crase, of Brisbane, is to move that
“ the longer continuance of Queensland as a State
of the Commonwealth of Australia will not be
conducive to the progress of the said State, nor
to the welfare of its industries and finance” ; and
he asks the conference to “pledge itself to use its
best endeavors ” to burst the union. What busi-
ness this is of the “ local authorities” isn’t clear ;
their work is to look after the local drains and
rubbish-tips, and they have no more call to
meddle with the Commonwealth than with the
Athanasian Creed. But, in any case, the gall
of Queensland “ local authorities ” passing severe
remarks about other people’s finance would be
superb. Craze himself is Mayor of about the
most financially muddled body in Australia;
it estimated a deficit of £12,500 eight
months ago, and hasn’t yet done one practical
thing to dodge the disaster. Some years ago it
let a contract for two miles of wood-blocking ;
price £35,000. Then muddling began, and the
final cost was £BO,OOO. South Brisbane, over the
river, was so utterly neglected by its local
authority that the Government Health Depart-
ment had to step in and clean the place up ; and
Premier Kidston subsequently had to collect the
cost with a club. Balmoral local authority sold a
£250 property for about £1 for alleged overdue
rates, and then had to pay £BOO in law costs, &c.,
over the unspeakably foolish business. Subse-
quently. when it found that it couldn’t manage
its own drain and dust-heap, it asked the Govern-
ment to take over the contract. Yet this is the
sort of local authority that Crazy invites to
manage the affairs of Australia ! Presumably,
also, the Ayr Tramway Board, as a “local
authority,” will be invited to express its opinion
concerning Commonwealth finance. Its ow a
ideas of finance are shown by this newspaper
cutting:—
At a meeting of the Ayr Tramway Board yesterday it
was decided to divide the £l2OO in hand between the
guaranteeing local bodies —the City Council and Thurin-
gowa Shire to have £4BO each, and Ayr Shire Council
£240. The chairman stated that he hoped by the end of
next year to pay up the arrears of interest which had
accumulated when the line was being constructed.
Having £l2OO available, the Board cuts it up
among the three “ local authorities ” —and hopes
to pay off the arrears of interest (owing to the
State) some day. No doubt the Board knows its
“local authority,” and hasn’t any fear of being
told to pay up its back debts before cutting up its
alleged profits. On the whole, judging by these
samples, it would be a nasty reflection on the
Commonwealth Government if its financial
management did commend itself to the Queens-
land “local authority.”
The Lyndhurst Federal Capital Joke.
The pilgrimage of Federal politicians that N.S. W.
State Premier Carruthers induced to go out and
look at some more proposed capital sites seems to
take no interest, or very little interest, in the
much-advertised .Lyndhurst. At all events, an
invitation to include that spot in the itinerary ot
the party only brought the reply that, so far, no
member had expressed the slightest desire to see
Lyndhurst again. If Lyndhurst is dead and
buried as a possible Federal capital, it is exactly
what it deserves, and, even if it had possessed
far more merits than it does,.its death and burial
are only what might be expected considering the
proceedings of its advocates. Lyndhurst was
boomed with a ferocious want of tact of which a
rhinoceros might have been ashamed, and with a
coarse arrogance that made many people
loathe the place and everybody connected
with it. The “claims” of Lyndhurst were
said to be many, but the loudest and most brutally
overbearing one was based on the statement that
the people of N.S.W. had refused to accept the
Federal Constitution until there was a guarantee
that the capital would be in N.S.W. (which was a
glaring lie); and on the other statement that the
Federal Constitution, as it now stands, provides
for the capital being as nearly as possible on the
100-mile limit from Sydney (which was such a
gross and impudent lie that it created a feeling of
nausea). The Lyndhurst push also represented a
crowd which talked largely of the special right
of N.S.W. to select the capital, of the special
claim of Sydney to all the trade of the capital,
and of the special compensations due to N.S.W.
for the “sacrifices” it had made in federating
with the other States an attitude that
was bitterly aggravating to States which
only joined the union at N.S.W.’s special
invitation, and which had no idea that they
would be jeered at afterwards as if they had
approached N.S.W. on their knees to ask assist-
ance from the lofty Ma State. Then, when there
was talk of a possible Federal capital on the
Murrumbidgee, near the proposed Barren Jack
reservoir, some of the Lyndhurst push set about
trying to prevent the building of that reservoir—
on which so much dry country has pinned its
hopes—and the boss bungler of the Lyndhurst
crowd demanded that if the reservoir was built at
all, it should be on the impossible condition of
Victoria finding the money—Victoria paying,
that is, to irrigate N.S.W. To all intents
and purposes, the Lyndhurst crowd put itself
forward as the enemy, not only of the other
States, but also of all N.S.W. except the 100-
mile radius around Sydney. It advertised itself
and its site as representing all the meanest
elements in human nature —parochial jealousy,
greed, lving, and narrow-minded anti-Australian
sentiment. When the acceptance of Lyndhurst
came to imply the acceptance of the astounding
falsehood that the Constitution ordered the capital
to be not more than 100 miles from Sydney ; and
the acceptance, also, of the theory that the other
States had in some way received large benefits or
concessions or alms from N.S.W., and must com-
pensate that State by fixing the Federal city
where Sydney would secure all the benefit; and
the acknowledgment of the right of a mere State
Legislature to dictate to the Parliament of Aus-
tralia—when it came to mean all these things,
Lyndhurst would have probably gone overboard
if "it had been Paradise. And it isn’t Paradise,
but—according to the declaration of a trusted
N.S.W. official who was specially appointed by
the N.S.W. State Government to report on
possible sites—a mere second or third or fourth-
rate spot, quite inferior to Monaro in almost every
respect. Its natural drawbacks ought to have
killed it dead in any case, but the bullying and
tactlessness of the little knot of parochialists
who advocated its selection gave it an extra dead-
ness which was something quite beyond the
average.

A Lesson to Labor.
At a very happy juncture for educational pur-
poses comes a crisis to the Labor Ministry in fe.
Australia. Premier Prick, in his effort to put into
effect his progressive programme, finds himself
confronted by a hostile Upper House, elected on
a narrow property franchise, and alien to the
sentiments of the majority of the voters. His
task is clearly to grapple with that Upper House,
and either make it mend its ways or bring it to an
end. He recognises his responsibility, and, as a
step in the campaign, contemplates the possibility
of having to dissolve the Assembly. Whether
that is the procedure proper to the circumstances
is neither here nor there ; the important fact is that
circumstances may arise—in fact, are on the point
of arising—which make that the only possible
step forward, in his opinion. But he finds the way
blocked. He holds office with a direct Labor fol-
lowing and the assistance of a band of Liberals.
The Liberals give him loyal support now. They
are prepared to continue that support and follow
him in bringing this Parliament to an end. One
condition only they make, that if they sacrifice
their seats in the cause of a Labor Government,
they shall not have to face Labor opposition in
their constituencies. Immunity from such op-
position is the price of their support. The con-
dition is absolutely reasonable. But, according to
the “new” spirit in the Labor leagues, it cannot be
granted. The Liberals must support Labor and
thus cut themselves off from all support from
outside the Labor ranks, and then be satisfied to
rave Labor’s knife at their throats in the consti-
tuencies. It is precisely the same situation as
that which has arisen in Federal politics with
regard to such men as Lyne, Isaacs and Bony-
THON. They, and others of the Protectionists who
went over to the Watson side at a crisis, stipu-
lated, very naturally, for Labor sympathy at the
rolls, and obtained a pledge to that effect ; and
i hat pledge it is now proposed—at least by the
V k torian Labor leagues —to shamelessly break.
In S. Australia, Labor politics have, so far, been
marked by a sweet reasonableness, and it seems
probable that if the present crisis develops on
the lines that are likely, Premier Price’s Liberal
supporters will obtain the immunity pledge they
ask, and that it will be faithfully observed. In
that case the lesson to the Victorian leagues will
surely be too obvious to be ignored, and the
treacherous proposal to turn hostile arms on
friendly allies in camp will, even at this late
stage, be abandoned.
