The Divine Eight of Clubs.
The Victorian clubs are up in arms and on their
hind legs shouting fierce anathema at Bent’s
Licensing Bill, and all because Bent proposes to
abolish the divine right of clubs, and reduce
these places—often mere drinking and gambling
places—to the same compliance with the law
that is required of ordinary pubs, and ordi-
nary unclubbed citizens. Nominally, what
the clubs take exception to is the provision
in the Bill that gives the police the right of access
to the premises; this is an insult to their dignity,
and makes them feel hurt. But the two points
that really trouble them are those which
require all bars to be closed at half-past 11 o clock,
and which prohibit gaming in a club just as in a
hotel. That is to say, they would like the proviso
against gambling and late drinking struck out,
but they don’t care much what legislation is
passed on these subjects, provided the police are
not allowed access; it doesn’t really matter what
the law says if no policeman may come in to see
the law being broken. The only reason that the
clubs advance why they should be exempt
from regulation is that they have always been
exempt. This is true. In Victoria, at any rate,
the provisions relating to hotels and their manage-
ment have never been applied to clubs, pranci-
pally—in recent years—because it was mani-
festly absurd to order a club to comply with
the law when the police were prohibited from
entering even if they had reason to believe
the order was not obeyed. _ But the origi-
nal reason for the exemption was a typi-
cal one. At first, clubs were aggregations
of wealthy and arrogant men who wanted to
dine and drink and win one another s money in
the society of people of their own calibre.
Accordingly, since wealthy men at one time made
and unmade the law, legislation was manu-
factured to fit their special requirements. At the
same time, however, an opportunity was afforded
to all sorts and conditions of men to band together
to defeat the provisions of the Licensing Act.
The consequence was that after a time clubs, quite
different from the first lot, sprang up like
mushrooms, and the legitimate hotels which paid
bio- license fees, and were amenable to the law
under heavy penalties, suffered. Some of these clubs
are large and partially drunk, and some are small
and not quite sober. The pretence of being
residential establishments is too holllow for
words. All alike depend for the profits that
keep them going mostly on their bars, and
the bar profits are large mainly because a
man can drink in his club at hours when else-
where, he would have to remain dry. lhe club is
a sanctuary against the licensing and gaming laws
of the State, and Bent proposes to do away with
all this, because the idea of sanctuary is mediaeval
and barbaric. Altogether, Tommy Bent will be
doing one of the best things he has yet done tor
Victoria if he insists on passing the club provisions
of his Licensing Bill as they stand. But the club
people are influential folk. They are expert
ear-biters, and are already chewing hard at
the wing on the side of Lhomass head.
They recognise that it is good business, when
chewing, to concentra-te their teeth on oiig ques-
tion, so what thev ask most intensely is that they
may not be subject to even nominal police
supervision. They say that policemen are large
and coarse, and have mud on their boots. And if
no policeman has a right to come in and see if the
bar is closed at 11.30 p.m., it doesn’t matter much
whether the law says it should close then or at
4.30 a.m.

A Meeting of Fat.
There was a notable gathering of Bure Merinoes
at Melbourne, the other day, “the room being
crowded,” according to the newspaper report,
“with pastoralists, farmers, and managing
directors of the large mortgage companies.” And
the meeting, which was presided over by one ot
the purest of the Merinoes— George Fairbairn—
spent a very pleasant time cursing both Prender-
gast’s proposed land-tax, and Watson’s. As a
cheerful variation from the usual run of these meet-
ings, no mention was made of the well-known
desire of the Labor party to “confiscate” the whole
of the land and either eat it or export it to Norway.
One Bostock went into figures, and, though he
admitted that Prendergast’s tax would exempt
18,000 farmers, and Watson’s 28,000. more,
making 46,000 untaxed altogether if the
Feberal Labor party’s scheme goes through
—he refused to be comforted. Misrepresentation
seems to be bred in the bone of theFatman. lhe
Labor party has repeated, ad nauseam, that its
tax is designed expressly to encourage the settle-
menfc of the farmers on the land ; but all Bostock
could see in it was “ a clever piece of strategy to
catch the farmers’ votes. Also, it was “only a
temporary expedient,” and something unspecified
but appalling was to follow. So the meeting,
after it had recovered from Bostock, decided to
oppose the tax because—
(l) It is contrary to British precedent.
(2) The taxes are not needed. , ' .
(3) The plausible reason, that of finding* land for immi-
grants, is fallacious.
(4) It will destroy the confidence of British investois.
This paper had a pathetic hope that “contrary to
British precedent ” was dead, or at least fatally
injured ; and that “destroying the confidence of
British investors” was also in very weak health ;
but this hope has, apparently, been a delusion
and a snare. All that was wanted to complete a
happy party was “liberty of the subject, who
was most unaccountably overlooked. Probably
he was there, but neither Bostock nor r air-
bairn noticed him. At any rate, this rattling of
ancient and dry bones isn’t likely to frighten even
Prendergast s land-tax, let alone Watsons,
which is a much sturdier youngster.
The simple-minded Melbourne Argus has pub-
lished ‘ ‘ a copy of a letter recently sent by a lead-
ing firm of Australian warehousemen to its
London branch” re the provisions of the new
Commerce Act. The Argus describes the letter
as “interesting,” which it certainly is in a variety
of ways. For example, it shows conclusively the
necessity for the Commerce Act, which the Argus
described as “harassing,” “meddlesome,” and
“ uncalled-for.” The letter points out that “false
description” includes the following :
(6) Goods marked at so many yards or so much weight,
if such goods are not as actually stated (for example,
50yd. reels when not actually so). .
(e) Goods marked “rain-proof,” silk, jute, flax, linen,
cotton, &c., when not actually so.
(e) Cotton goods marked with the number of laps or
folds by a figure, which is commonly taken by the unini-
tiated as meaning the number of yards in the piece, the
actual length being less. .
(/) Indications of width, dimensions, weight per unit
of length, when not actually so.
(<j) Goods described as hand-made, when wholly or par-
tially machine-made.
To put it shortly, there has grown up by degrees
a multitude of “trade customs” almost every
one of which is a swindle on the consumer,
and the “harassing” Commerce Act compels
importers to write in haste to their London
agents and tell them that it is necessary to be
honest. And the Argus is so much shocked at
the idea of honesty in commerce that it publishes
letters like this as a dreadful example of what
things have come to.
Melbourne is moving slowly in the direction of
unifying its many bickering municipalities. The
city lias already absorbed several outside districts,
and now Melbourne, South Melbourne and Port
Melbourne councils have agreed to a scheme of
amalgamation that will carry Greater Melbourne
right along the river district to the port. South
and Port Melbourne have still to submit the pro-
posal to a referendum. The scheme will reduce
rating in the -suburbs ; will raise one big central
authority to tight the tramway octopus when that
grasping creature reaches out its greedy tentacles
for an extension of its lease; will give a wide
field to the city’s fine electric undertaking;
and will yield a host of other advantages.
A bad feature is the long extension that is to be

fallen women ; Everard Digges de la Touche,

ing Higgins ; Wm. Knox is apparently anxious to
represent Horry, and not an Australian constitu-
ency ; Simo'-' Eraser, the dreariest public ass on

efforts of the Anti-Sosh sectarian gang ; Judkins
and Worrall are using the Lord to secure
notoriety, and the familiar way they treat the
Almighty makes a decent Pagan feel tired. Give
such a crowd a. year or two of the balance of
power, and ! I hereunder look forward a bit:

I met a martyr pale.
“Glory,” said he, “to whip and goad !
Glory to ginger ale !
And to the self-advertisement
That shall us never fail!”

We passed a heap of bones ;
And, at the sight, mine eyes were dim

Whiten and bleach ? In what belief
Was this foul mui der done ? ”

To kill each mother’s son.”

On bookmakers we dote ;

To legalise the tote.

With screams and mouthing foam ;

With statements about Rome ;

With joy our buns from ’Ome.

I see him, calm and grand,
In the Assembly’s windy hall
By the mace-bearer stand ;

His bun is in his hand !

Calm as the holy seraphim,

Last week the S. M. Herald solemnly and de-
finitely repudiated N.B.W. State Premier Car-
ruthers, and decided that even if Labor leader
M'Gowen is the alternative, it is better to have
M'Gowen. That was a long way for the Herald
to go, and by the length of its going Carruthers
can estimate the depth of his fall. Joseph Car-
ruthers is now, so far as the press goes, the
Friendless Premier of Australia. Kipston has
warm friends and bitter enemies. Bent is re-
garded with a quaint mixture of hatred, contempt,
and admiration—just when he seems to have
fallen to the lowest depth with his buffoonery and
comic songs, he arises, grunting heavily, and does
some large, strong, democratic deed which makes
him look for the moment the biggest man in
Australia. Price is much disliked and deeply
loved. “Jack” Evans has many friends in the
press and elsewhere, who say that, even if he
isn’t much of a Premier, he used to be a pretty
good sea-captain; and there are some who say
that Newton Moore might be a lot worse. But
Carruthers is the Man Who Has Evaporated.
He has done nothing large to earn public hatred ;
his press supporters have fallen away because of
his smallness and indefiniteness. He has just
dropped through a hole. Even Deform has turned
on him. He has cotne to be regarded as a man
who can be pushed about anyhow. And when
one considers how many indefinite Premiers have
kept afloat for quite a while in Australia, there
must be an amazing indefiniteness about the man
who can lose so much support in so short a time
as Joseph Carruthers has done. Even if he
went and found out a new lot of imaginary injuries
done by the Commonwealth to Ma State he would
no longer raise a pennyworth of enthusiasm.

The “ Victorian Year Book ’* mournfully remarks
that 93 per cent, of the land of the Western Dis-
trict ot Victoria is in exactly the same non-
cultivated condition to-day as it was 70 years
ago. The “ Year Book ” does not explain the
immediate causes of this stagnation, because it is
a purely non-partisan document. It simply
dumps down the statement that Western
Victoria is mostly an empty wilderness, and
leaves it at that. The explanation lies in the
huge masses of land that are held by a few
persons, and used solely for pastoral purposes.
In the Western district 181 pastoral estates
cover 2,885,786 acres, an average of 15,943 acres
apiece. This explains the stagnation. The soil
is among the best in Australia, seaports are close
at hand, and the rainfall is excellently adapted
for agricultural purposes. Cut up into 100-acre
blocks, these 181 sheep - walks would provide
28,857 farms, and might easily give em-
ployment, directly and indirectly, to a million
people. lor want of the cutting-up process,
population has remained stationary for 30 years.
The earnings of the railways are insignificant, and
scarcely pay for axle-grease. Coastal shipping is
almost extinct, and Bent’s dredges are busy
deepening harbors that will silt up again, practi-
cally unused. A heavy, graduated Federal land-
tax, pitched high enough to burst the whole
squattocracy, is the only thing that can save the
district. It is ridiculous to talk of “ resuming”
the estates—the Government hasn’t got the cash.
The land-hogs hold out for famine prices, although
land-values are at present inflated far beyond
their natural figure. A land-tax, based not Upon
the sheep-carrving, but upon the man-carrying
capacity of the soil, would paralyse the Manif'old-
Chirnside push. Moral, vote for such a tax—the
bigger the better.

British hostility to the. establishment of an
Australian navy is now cloaked, but it is none the
less bitter. The report of the Imperial Defence
Committee, received a few weeks ago, is supposed
to recommend a complete system of harbor and
coastal defence for the Commonwealth. It cer-
tainly contains no distinct condemnation of a
submarine and torpedo squadron, which it was
hoped would be the nucleus of an Australian
navy, but the committee has sought to squelch
the idea by merely not mentioning it.
I hen Captain Cresswell, the naval director,
who has just returned from a tour in
Great Britain in search of the latest in-
formation regarding naval developments, seems
to have been _ successfully blocked. The in-
formation relating to the best means of starting
an Australian navy was significantly unobtain-
able. Wherever he went among the Admiralty
docks, yards, and depots there was a smiling wel-
come, but it was invariably followed by protesta-
tions of regret that the facts he most required
were unfortunately at the time unavailable. The
way in which the official doors he most desired to
pass through slammed in his face was too consis-
tent to be accidental, and if he left the shores of
the dear old Ma Land convinced that every
department had been secretly instructed to
thwart the object of his visit, no one could blame
him.
«.
Melbourne Argus, in a futile attempt to show
that a progressive land-tax is not needed to break
up big estates, states that, during the last three
years, 448,241 acres have been subdivided and sold
in the Cabbage Garden, of which 110,2GG acres
were taken under Bent’s Closer Settlement
scheme. The inconsequential journal fails to
discern that the great demand for land, the
increased values created in part by the Closer
Settlement Board’s purchases, and (chief cause of
all) the manifest proximity of the land-
tax, have urged land-grabbers to seize the
chance before it is too late. Nevertheless,
in spite of these half-million acres cut up, the
land-tax register shows that, on February 28th,
1906, there were 1262 large estates on the register,
of the taxable value of £7,654,484, while on
August 28th, 1906, there will be 1299, of the value
of £7,310,639 ; so that, almost as fast as some
grabbers cut up and sell, others acquire and hold
for a rise ; and, of course, the grabbing of land for
that purpose is likely to be much more rapid
when a lull comes in the property market. What
a gaudy farce the present Victorian land-tax is,
and what a scandal the assessments amount to,
may be judged from the fact that, though the
register shows 1299 estates still locked up, of the
taxable value of £7,310,639, those 448,241 acres
that were cut up comprised only 63 estates, yet
they actually sold for £3,126,000 !

“Quan,” from the North
The unspeakable Brisbane Courier recently published
a cry from Cairns (Q.) stating that a farmer in that dis-
trict had paid white labor 10s. a ton for cutting his cane,
and as there were, of course, other small expenses, the
13s. per ton which he received when he sold it to the
Colonial Sugar Refining monopoly left him at a dead loss.
The moral, of course, was that cane-growing by white
labor was impossible. Then Labor-member Mann— in no
way related to T. Mann, once of the Beer-and-Water
public house—sent a reply that was simply staggering as
showing the methods of the great sugar monopoly.
At Cairns it takes only eight tons of cane to produce a
ton of sugar. Where there are State-owned central mills
to crush the cane, the monopoly is willing to buy the raw
sugar from the mills at £l2 per ton, which, after allowing
a liberal margin for expenses, is as good as £1 6s'.
or so per ton to the farmer. Where there is no State-
owned central mill the farmer has to sell his cane to the
Sugar Co., and it gives him just about half what he gets
where there is competition. Therefore, it appears that
the abolition of the Colonial Sugar Refining Co.’s
monopoly is urgently needed, and that there is nothing
wrong with white labor. It really looks as if, with a suf-
ficient number of State-owned sugar mills and a great
State-owned sugar refinery— with a system, in short,
which would make sugar a Government monopoly—the
grower might get a lot more for his cane than even the
present central mills can pay him, for raw sugar at £l2 a
ton appears still to leave a big profit to the refinery. It
sells refined sugar at £24.
j ♦
Concluding his “ Keport on the Federated Malay
States and Java,” Senator Staniforth Smith
gives, as the fruit of his inquiries, some hints as
to the control of Australian Papua—a country
with much the same natural conditions. The
most important are his conclusions as to what is
wanted:—
Reform of the land laws to, give prompt possession,
without undue red tape, to settlers. Improvement of
the roads. Appointment of a Director of Agriculture,
with a view to encouraging culture of industrial plants.
Establishment of State nurseries, with the same object.
Planting by the Government of rubber and cocoanut
trees. Exploitation of the valuable teak and ebony
forests. Engagement of an expert from the Malav States
to report on Papuan plantation possibilities. Establish-
ment of Government medical stations on the Papuan
goldfields. Sale of the extravagant Government steamer
Merrie England. (It costs £7317 a year to keep up.)
Senator Smith seems to have observed the East
Indies with intelligence and industry ; and his
conclusions in regard to Papua, formed from a
study of neighboring (and flourishing) territories
under foreign control, are certainly valuable.

Miss Tittell Brune continues to be a re-
bellious daughter of the old nobility at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney. They seem to have had
some curious ways, this old nobility of Elizabethan
times. The head of the Vernon house not ap-
proving of his one spoiled chick’s choice of a hus-
band—though, being the heroine, the chick has,
of course, made the best choice—he calls up the
butcher from the slaughter-yard to give her a
thrashing, and the butcher goes about the job as
though be had been brought up to it. The
deformed jester is another Elizabethan property
which the American author of “ Dorothy
Vernon ” works for all he is worth ; a
privileged imp, he moves in and out of the
plot, making wise suggestions, and generally
proving that, by lights, he ought to have Bur-
leigh’s job. Maurice Dudley is the wise fool of
this performance ; and probably out of'considera-
tion for his obvious Scotch ancestry, he doesn’t
make any jokes, in spite of his cap and bells.
The picturesque melodrama will run for another
week, sure ; in the last week of this brief Tittell
Brune season other things may happen.
** * *
Miss Beatrice Holloway undertook, at
Sydney Criterion, on Saturday night, a difficult
task in pourtraying “Jo,” of the Dickens’ drama,
“Bleak House.” Success must be won by the
strength and fidelity of the character sketch of
the street waif. The play itself lends no help.
With three acts and 11 scenes it fails entirely to
give any coherent idea of Dickens’ story, and
if it succeeded it would gain little, for the
strength of Dickens didn’t lie in plot or in
the carrying through of an idea to a logical
end, but in the irrelevant sketches of curious
types that he scattered through his works.
Boiled down to its story, a Dickens novel
represents the commonest and tritest melodrama;
it is the humorous pictures of humanity that make
his works live, and these pictures can’t be com-
pressed into the limits of a stage performance.
Therefore, Dickens’ works have, as plays, been
failures—mostly very bad and unwieldy failures.
“Bleak House” is no exception ; it doesn’t fail
as, say, “ Our Mutual Friend ” would do, for that
would probably be the last possibility in the way
of failure, but it is a work of very limited stage
possibilities. It gained some popular favor by
Jennie Lee’s pathetic delineation of “Jo,” the
street waif, who was always moved on; and it is
Miss Holloway’s ambition to revive that popularity
by the same means. When she is credited with a
fair amount of success—she certainly won the sym-
pathy of a packed audience on Saturday night—
a great deal has been said in praise. Probably,
too, her performance will improve with the run
of the piece. At present it is hardly consistent-
good in places, but falling off now and again.
* * * *
In the strictly subordinate parts of “ Bleak
House” the Holloway Co. shows well. Such
minor characters as Inspector Bucket (Mr. John
Hesford) and the Coroner (Mr. J. P. West) are
very well played, but most of the others pitch
their reading in far too high a key. There
is nothing unctuous about Chadband —he
is just a roarer; and Guppy is a tra-
vesty. Miss Rosa Conroy, as Lady Dedlock,
acts well in the pathetic scenes, but her
haughty pride is overdone to a cinder; and Miss
Ethel Appleton as Hortense is also far too melo-
dramatic. The production of “Bleak House” is
excellently illustrated, the stage pictures being of
the best. But it needs throughout to be played in
a lower key. That will give the star a better
chance to get to the right note of plaintive misery.
** * *
“In London Town” retires from Sydney
Royal stage after Friday, and the Australian
melodrama, “Riding to Win,” takes its place.
Though the name doesn’t suggest it, “ Riding to
Win” deals with cycle racing, and the wheelmen
are triers, and will be personated on the stage by
“ well-known local riders.”
** * *
After an interlude of three years, Mdlle.
Dolores, her Voice, and a repertory of
400 songs are again reducing Sydney to
a mush of admiration. Two concerts given
at time of writing filled the Town Hall
tb a tight squeeze. This is Mdlle’s. fourth visit,
and if her popularity goes on working up in the
yeast-like way it has done, she will have to hire a
park next time of calling, or give up all hope of
accommodating her audiences. Since the soprano’s
last visit she has learnt to sing German songs, and
all the Hochderkaisers of musical standing in
Sydney assert she sings them as exquisitely
as she does those of her own French
land. English, French, German, Italian songs,
airs from operas, ballads, old Elizabethan airs,
rustic gems and classic excerpts—the singer soared
over the vast musical field with the vocal ease
and grace of Shelley’s skylark in the opal
sky. Her voice range is from a fine D to
upper 0, and its management is such as
to place the soprano above criticism. The
Tait brothers, who are piloting Dolores, will pro-
bably induce her to give a matinee on Monday,
but her last advertised concert is for next Thurs-
day. The pianist, Franz Wells, is a player of
skill and refinement, and his hair is a cross be-
tween Paderewski’s and Percy Grainger’s; so he
has much for which to be thankful.

The most notable thing at the Tivoli these
cold nights is the hop of Seymour. The hopper is
said to be 46, but that isn’t mentioned in the bills
by way of advt. Bills and the world are cruel to
the aged. If a youngster of seven can wreck the
Hesperus in a stormier sea than other youngsters
of seven, the bills print in large type that the
cheild is nearly six, and the world is expected to
come early to avoid the crush. But it wouldn’t
matter how violently a person of 94 could wreck
the schooner; no bill would ever mention that the
wrecker was 96£. If it did, it would simply be to
appeal to the world, of its charity, to turn up, and
not be too hard on the old chap. Yet, as an
oldster of 94, he might be more remarkable than
the other is as a youngster of seven. So Seymour
has to battle through on his merits, and, in double
harness with Dupre, he can still do it. Little
Cliff (observe how they “star” the “little”) is
removing his nimble legs from the premises at the
end of the week, and it seems likely that his patter
and his other properties will leave at the same
time; wherefore, this week is a very good week
to go to the house of Rickards.
** * #
“ The Little Michus,” like “Yeronique”
from the same source, is another reminder that
French comic operas are not very humorous, as
this country understands the meaning of the word.
They have brightness, gaiety and bustle, but no
humor or satire to speak of. Leaving Gilbert and
Sullivan’s classical works out of the question, an
average Australian audience can get more enter-
tainment from the story of “The Orchid,” which
is cynical, farcical comedy, than from the
libretto of “The Little Michus” in its Eng-
lish translation. The clumsy wording of
the Anglicised lyrics, which may have been
neat enough in the French original, is an-
other noticeable weakness. The music of the
songs seems wanting in sweet melody, as a rule,
owing to the fact that the lines don’t lilt. This
defect is even more marked than it was in “ Vero-
nique,” where several of the numbers had a charm
in the vocal rendering, apart from the daintiness
of the orchestration. The present opera contains
nothing so fetching as the “Apple Tree’ song
and chorus, the “Swing” song, and the talking
song for the postillion and girls in Messager’s pre-
vious work. Neither is the narrative so spicy in
its love interest. It is the mildest of French
opera yarns. It might pass the censorship of
Judkins.
« * » *.
On the other hand, “ The Little Michus ”
gives opportunity for more grotesque fooling than
is common in modern comic operas, and Mel-
bourne dearly loves a flavor of panto, with its
musical entertainment. The action, moreover,
is very diversified in character; each of the three
scenes has its own individuality, and none of the
principal performers are allowed to behave like
irresponsible persons. They come along as occa-
sion requires. Lauri, for instance, doesn’t appear
at all in the Ist act, or take a prominent position
in the 3rd. He has a very good part, but it is not
pushed forward out of its proper place. If he
creates a more important effect than anybody else
it is because he is Lauri, and his manner is large,
and his sham red nose stands out boldly in profile
—a remarkable feature of the proceedings. He
offers no explanation of his nose. It is just a
gratuitous absurdity, and he has to spread himself
all he knows to keep it in countenance, so to
speak.
* * * *
It can’t be said that “ The Little Michus ”
displays any members of the Williamson Co. to
special advantage. In the name parts, Miss
Florence Young is associated with Miss Alma
Barber, neither of them being “little” or light
enough for her character. Miss Young is the un-
roniantic real Michu, who takes a healthy interest
in her parents’ cheesemongery, and desires to
marry the shop assistant, whilst Miss Barber is
the unreal Michu, in whose veins runs the blue
blood of General Lauri’s nose, and who sighs for
the tender passion of Reg. Roberts, the hero of
her romance. As a sentimental lover, the new
lady is at least more convincing than the re-
turned tenor, for Roberts has improved in
his vocalism only during his two years’
absence. His attempts at emotional utterance
are worse than before. He has the loud, soulless
tremolo of a Salvation Harmy preacher. Claude
Bantock is a colorless papa Michu. Mis 3 Clifton,
as Madame Michu, diffuses her customary air of
good temper, and there are important parts for
Fred Leslie and W. S. Percy. Leslie’s rasping
voice and nigger-minstrel, methods are not too
aggressive in this case ; but Percy, for his part,
frivols unintelligently instead of putting in a
quaint original character sketch. The honors of
the production fall to the well-dressed chorus, and
Andrew McCunn’s orchestra, and the general
management of the show. A great audience
crowded Her Majesty’s (Melb. ) on Saturday night.
Probably it was not, in a great measure, a disap-
pointed audience. Its ear had not been tickled by
the music, but its appetite had been liberally
catered for. “ The Little Michus ”is a satisfac-
tory thing to look back upon.

Within the past seven days this scribe has
reminded himself, after personal inquiry into the
matter, that “ Honor Thy Father,” at Melbourne
Royal, is an old melodrama revived, and one of
the most amusing in Anderson’s collection. A
couple of sorrow-stricken parents and many other
people wander into the trouble, and walk out of
it, and wander back again for the sole purpose of
making “situations.” “Honor Thy Father” is
a drama in which everybody hovers around, wait-
ing for chances to score some public sympathy at
the villain’s expense. Incidentally, the villain
loses his eyesight through being pushed into a
lunatic’s armchair, and from that time forward
his enemies merely defeat his wicked ends with-
out jeering at his discomfiture. But they never,
by any chance, drop a penny into his tall silk
hat. “Man’s Enemy” is the melodrama ap-
pointed for Saturday.

Madame Blanche Arral, a prima donna
from the Opera Comique, Paris, and an artist
whose reputation, seemingly, ought to have
reached Australia long ago, but didn t, announces
a forthcoming appearance at Melbourne Princess’s.
The advt. concerning Blanche Arral has the
charm of mystery. She will represent a pro-
grame of “song, recital, and scenes from operas,”
on Saturday next (18th), but the inevitable
full particulars had not come to light at time of
writing. Madame Arral, however, came to light
at a Marshall Hall concert last Saturday, when
her warbling of the Polacca from “Mignon ” and
Michaela’s aria from “Carmen” was a pleasant
surprise to a critical audience. No artist of her
high quality has ever previously come to Mel-
bourne unheralded by a flourish of trumpets.
** * *
The most muscular of the new people at
Melbourne Opera House is Brinn, who lately
sought the bubble reputation under the cannon’s
weight in Sydney. Brinn’s heavy juggling with
shot and shell and cannon has an impressive
appearance of dangerousness, but it is not a grace-
ful work of art, anyhow. He might as well
devote his strength to hoisting a brewer’s dray
laden , with many hogsheads of beer. Thusfar
strong men have not juggled with hogsheads of
beer. Miss Cassie Walmer, an octoroon danseuse,
is a welcome stranger at Rickards’s, and a sweetly
original jumping entertainment is supplied by
Agustin and Hartley. The lady partner in this
turn does a fair half of the work. When both
partners are blindfolded, and they finally arrive
together at the interior of one empty barrel in the
middle of a table a tired journalist feels grateful
to Agustin and Hartley. They have revealed
quaint novelties in gymnastic exercise, and the
revelation has been quick and lively.

ALWAYS ASK FOR “ALOK."
Melbourne City Council can take credit
for good showmanship in its engagement of
Edwin Lemare, from England, to take first strike
at the reconstructed municipal organ. Lemare’s
recitals have proved an amazing success, con-
sidering the lack of frivolity in the entertainment.
He opened the series to a merely large audience,
and all the subsequent audiences have been larger,
which means that the Council, after paying the
organist his fee of £45 for each performance, and
charging rent and working exes, against the
receipts, will make a handsome profit on the
speculation. The series of 12 recitals has been
extended to 16, and the last of these extras
will probably fall due on Saturday next
week. Meanwhile, Lemare is giving four
lectures, at the Athenaeum Hall, to the disciples
of Wagner. He has invaded the domain of
Marshall Hall and is receiving the homage of the
Elect, with Lady Northcote at their head.
*# * *
“The Australian Dramatic School” had
one night out at Melb. Athenaeum Hall last week,
for the awful purpose of presenting two pieces of
puerility, written by one Arthur Clarke, the young
man who runs the “school.” Regarded as a
“ star ” actor in ofce of his pieces—a tlirefi act farce
containing six characters only—Arthur Clarke
was merelyatall, unobjectionable amateur, of good
stage appearance, shining by comparison with an
undersized youth clothed in a tailor's misfit. But
in his other capacities of author and dramatic
schoolmaster, the aspiring Arthur deserved hauling
over the coals when they are hot. His comedies,
and many of the raw amateurs who walked and
talked in them, were shocking things to put upon
any sta; 3 at popular prices. An advt. on the
programme stated that more plays will be staged
at regular i: »rvals, ladies and gentlemen being
“guaranteed public appearances as they attain
proficiency.” If the principal offenders in last
week’s display were specimens of proficiency, a
performance by an unproficient lot of scholars
would probably, be rather better.
** * *
The Man who wasn’t really from Mexico
has rioted through his appointed week at Adelaide
Royal with great success. It is a foolish play ;
not in the same street with—to take a fair subject
for comparison—“What Happened to Jones.”
Also the author’s ideas of stagecraft are primitive ;
and some of the minor characters overact a good
deal. But the piece has about 300 laughs in it,
and for their sake an audience will accept any-
thing. “ The Talk of the Town” is winding up
the brief season.' Boxing-gloves play almost the
leading part in it. Palotta gets into them first,
and after that Hugh Ward is stoushed frequently
and freely. The “Palace” Co. at the Tivoli has
put on a new Strong Man—pocket edition.
He is called (for no apparent reason) Ben Hur,
and on some evenings—but not all—he allows a
701 b. stone to be dropped on to his abdomen from
a height of 3ft. Robert Scott is a rather clever
“sketch” actor in this co. G. H. Reid has
starred for one evening to a crowded house at the
Exhibition, the Gornck family continues tuneful
on the sackbut and psaltery and various other
instruments at the Town Hall, and the ice-rink
works hard to vary its attractions. There is
something extra-amusing in watching a perfect
stranger fall down flat if he is costumed as a
policeman, or as Someone’s whisky.
AT POVERTY POINT.
On the last night of R. G. Knowles at Melb. Princess’s,
he had Premier Bent among his audience. Bent sat in
the front row' of the d.c., and listened gloomily to
Knowles’ stories until he fell a-slumbering. Later on he
recovered consciousness, and resumed his smileless state
of attention. Evidently he was bored to death by the
entertainment, which is a pretty good testimonial to
Knowles’ humor. A more labored funny-man, with a
number of puerile jokes, would have kept Bent in roars
of laughter. Mark Twain, in his younger days, stated as
a fact, that he made a practice of reading portions of his
manuscript to a rather woolly-headed man-servant, who
knew him only as Mr. Clemens. The man sometimes
laughed heartily at the cheap surface wheezes, but
never moved a muscle at a dryly humorous story. He
was the infallible test of their merit (as humor went at
the time) until a great change came over him. He
smiled the moment he entered the boss’s room, and
laughed at everything the boss read to hint. He had
learned that Clemens was Twain, and his usefulness as a
test was exhausted. Previously he had been an unbiassed
critic, like Bent, incapable of understanding a non-
aggressive joke.
“ Hamer ” : “ Skull’s ” anecdote about the boy engaged
to represent guileless enthusiasm in the audience (B.
5/7/’O6) would be all right if “ Skull ” didn’t make the boy
come for his wages. It W'ould be a poor show’ that
couldn’t get an army of boys for such purposes merely by
-passing them in free. In towns where the people were
jad vised on the bills to ‘ ‘ come early to avoid the crush,” but
stayed away to avoid it, I have frequently let little boys
Pfo in at half-price to make a house, to attract guileless
passers by with the idea that the hall was pretty full, and
-something attractive must be on to bring it to that un-
usual condition. In city or country the average boy can
be relied upon to do any amount of hard work connected
with a show for the sake of a pass.

■of demolition, tumbling architecture, collapsing planks

Queenie Cadden, an Australian girl, recently back from
London, has just joined Williamson’s Royal Comic Opera
•Co. She was a member of the original Female Quartette
in England. The other members were Kate Rooney,

Melbourne City Council, after spending a huge lump of
money on its organ, now inflates its chest and proudly
declares that it has the finest organ in the world. If the
finest organ means the one with the most complicated
mechanism, then the Melbourne City Council has got the
article. Lemare, the star performer who is supplying
recitals at 45 guineas a time, has managed to drag out
some grand music from the instrument ; but then it is an
open secret that before he started serious work on it he
made the workmen unhitch a whole lot of the electric
fakements so that the machine might be easier to handle.
He considered that an organist needed sometimes to
think about the piece he was playing and not turn him-
self into an electrical engineer and devote his whole
thoughts to the complicated contraptions set in front of
him. The Council knows that its instrument is as intri-
cate as the Athanasian creed, and it consequently argues
that there is no local organist of sufficient ability to grind
a tune out of it. So a doctor of music from Belfast (Ire-
land) has been engaged to come out and take the billet
of city organist. Since the Belfast man is acknowledged
to be inferior to Lemare, he will probably unhitch a few
dozen more of the electrical combinations hung on the
organ, and possibly it may occur to the Council then that
a local organist could have filled the bill with equal credit.
But everybody on the Council just now seems to have the
“ imported ” mania badly. In their yearning for foreign
talent, the councillors didn’t even select their own
organist, but got a man in Edinburgh and a man in
London to do it for them. And, just to show that they
had no bias in the matter, these two selected the man
in Ireland, Dr. William George Price.
“ Melb.” : Miss Gena Coppin replaces her lamented
father as one of the directors of Melbourne Theatre Royal
Proprietary Company. That is the way the ladies are
coining on. Miss Coppin was familiar in the city, driving
her father about in the buggy. She was the last of a
succession of daughters in that duty. I don’t know
whether her name is Iphigenia, or something like that,
or only Gena, as she is called. She inherits a good deal
of her father’s talent, not only in finance, but in theatrical
business, though she doesn’t attempt to act. She staged
and managed the Christmas pantomime of “ Santa
Claus.” In the last Melbourne season of the Bland Holts,
Miss Coppin was intimately associated with them. The
present George Coppin has not come under my notice
for some time. He appeared on the stage a number of
years ago as George Selth, his father’s name being George
Selth Coppin. Afterwards he acted in the suburbs under
his own name. Seeing him take a character in the
“ Private Secretary," I was much interested, because it
seemed that he must be exactly like what his father was
when he first arrived in Melbourne in 1845.
Melbourne Sporting and Dramatic News reports that
“ Mr. Basil Gill was at latest appearing as Clive in
‘Colonel Newcome,’ a play founded upon Thackeray’s
‘ Pendennis.’” Ye gods! Presently we’ll be told that the
young man is about to take the part of Nicholas Nickleby
in “ Bleak House,” a play founded on “ David Copper-
field.”
The Johnny adorers of stage divinities are rather more
pronounced in New Caledonia than in this country.
When the State Fair—a Vic. show —was located at
Noumea, the girl members of the co. were haunted on
their homeward way, after the performance, by a number
of youthful Frenchmen, breathing fervent invitations to
supper. Every night the ardent followers grew more
pressing, until at last “Jim” Miller, the business
manager, expostulated with one or two of them. Miller’s
expostulation had the desired effect, it seems. Probably
his French accent broke them up. During the rest of the
season they merely sat persistently in the front seats and
looked unutterable love.
Irene Ainsley, the young Maoriland contralto who has
lately been nestling under Melba’s wing, becomes “ Irene
Zelanda ” (she might have made it “ Maorilanda”). The
advent of Ernestine Boomerang or Eustacia Waddy
Coo-ee draws closer every day. Also, the figure of Ruby
Paddymelon Australis looms mistily in the middle dis-
tance of the future.
According to the Chicago (U.S.A.) Tribune (Chicago is
the Beef Trust city), “Nance O’Neil took the poor
debtor’s oath at the" Pemberton square court in Boston
on May 22. She lost heavily in the San Francisco fire,
nearly everything she owned in scenery, costumes, and
stage effects being destroyed. Her step was unavoidable,
she says. She has postponed the production of her new
play, ‘ The Story of the Golden Fleece,’until next season.”
The exact meaning of the “ poor debtors oath ” isn’t
clearly understood on this side, but it sounds as if poor,
stormy, red-headed Queen Elizabeth was so hard up that
she had to go broke on credit.
The old theatrical phrase of “ under the management ”
of Mr. So-and-So is being superseded in America and
England by the announcement that one or other of the Jew
magnates “ presents ” the star performers of the co. in
his latest production. For instance “Mr. Frohman
presents Mr. Seymour Hicks and Miss Ellaline Terriss ”
whilst “ Mr. Gaston Mayer presents Mr. Harry Fragson
and Miss May de Sousa.” The minor members of a
company are not presented at all. They merely appear
as supporters of the principals whom the Jew manager
presents. Observe the attitude of the modern autocrats
of the theatre ! They take the popular actor and actress
by the hand and patronisingly present them to their old
friends, the public. In the ceremony of introduction the
Jew gentleman is a magnificent figure. He represents
the Theatrical Trust. He pulls the strings of Art. He is
the bloated capitalist who owns the stage.
Dear Bulletin,— l am now fearlessly organising a
monster crusade against the letting of the Exhibition,
Melbourne, for the holding of dog and poultry shows.
Even as I write one of those disgusting spectacles is in
progress. I may be a pioneer thinker, but my intellect
tells me that the tenants of the adjacent houses are quite
possibly incited by the barking, etc., to language that is
less sacred than profane. With the Rev. J. Umbuck I
dauntlessly counted 337 persons passing the building
!in a comparatively short space of time. Some
of the women were actually striding like navvies.
There were also two (2) females who entered the
institution swinging their parasols as a bricklayer
might his hod. Their language (the females’) showed the
shocking prevalence of a word that rhymes with
“sham.” I drew the attention of a policeman to this
fact, but he impudently asserted that they were only dis-
cussing pedigrees, and that the word I shall not men-
tion signified a lady dog who had become a mother.
Upon this I took his number without a moment’s hesita-
tion, though the man looked at me. But there was one
to apply the balm of merited praise. “ Mr. Hudkins,”
said the Rev. J. Umbuck, “ may I say that you are a
brave and noble man?” “Sir,” I replied, “you dome
proud! ’’—Yours pluckily, W. J. Hudkiks. P.S.—I
enclose my photograph for reproduction.—W.J.H.
“Cliff”: The advance agent of a third-class travelling
co. has the most thankless job on earth. The opening
night depends on him. If the business turns out bad, it
was his fault. If good, the managersays, “ Oh, it was the
printing that did it.” In the “smalls" agents have to
paste the printing on the walls and fences, “ work ” the
papers, arrange the hotels for the company, fix the
carrier, and keep their wits about them to circumvent
any opposition, or to disappear from the town if a circus
happens along. Meantime, they have to pretend to shout
for everybody, but gracefully allow other people to pay
when they insist on it. When the inevitable frost happens,
the company disbands, the manager vanishes, and the local
people get up a benefit for the stranded pros. But the
agent—where is he ? In a town, perhaps several hun-
dred miles away, ignorant of everything. He has spent
his last penny in wiring, he has to wait for the travelling
mailman to tell him the facts. If the agent has no trade
he is often found next as a rouseabout in the yard of the
pub., living on the thought that one day he’ll be J. C.
Williamson, or Anderson, or Edwin Geaeh. His one solid
advantage is that he is usually too far ahead of his co. to
see it wrestle with “East Lynne,” or “The Liver on the
Door-knob.”

“ Nym ”: Met the evergreen Dan Barry at Codville
(North-eastern Victoria), looking prosperous. Dan has
jilted his old love pro tern., and is running a biograph
show to crowded houses. The crowd just tumbles over
its collective self to hear Dan orate over the scenes, and
the flickergraph is quite a secondary adjunct when Daniel
is raging in full blast.
“ C.N.” : Many years ago, I was running a small show
at Newcastle, and played to good audiences until opposi-
tion came along with a wax figure of Louisa Collins, the
Botany poisoner, as its star item. My show was deserted.
We didn’t take sufficient to pay for the gas, and the only
people who would come near the doors were women, who
would step up and ask me. “ Is this where Louisa Collins
is?” I was new to the business then, and said “ No,”
on which they would depart for my hated rival. The
exchequer was running low, and 1 saw that things could
not go on very long that way, so I wrote down to Sydney
for the wax effigy of a middle-aged woman. The people I
sent to wrote back saying that they had nothing to suit
me except a figure of Queen Victoria in her coronation
robes. I thought for a minute, then I wired back, “ Send
Victoria along.” I borrowed an old blouse and skirt from
one of my song and dance artists, substituted these for
the red velvet robes on the figure, and put out a big
board with the legend: “Just added. Louisa Collins,
the Botany Poisoner. Modelled from life before execu-
tion.” The crowds rolled up in thousands and used to
stand in front of the figure saying what an awful face it
had. In three weeks I had taken all the show money in
the town, and my rival had been driven to a tent at
Maitland, where he only got a precarious living by
including a man who tied live snakes round his neck.
Don’t talk to me about Empire Day being a ridiculous
function.
Portly Charles Saunders, who toured Australia years
back as a concert tenor, goes about twenty stone in the
balance nowadays. This is against him" when singing
pretty tenor ditties about girls, but he is thought a lot of
when his clarion voice defies Philistine giants and others
in Oratorio.
“F.”: .Apropos Mrs. Elizabeth Winstanley, the first Aus-
tralian actress to appear in London, as mentioned in a re-
cent Bulletin. The following obituary notice appeared in
The Australasian (Melb.), 22/6/’67 Winstanley— On
the 10th instant, at her residence, No. 2 Webb-street,
Fitzroy, Mrs, Elizabeth Winstanley, mother of Mrs.
O’Flaherty aud Mrs. Xiinines, of London, aged 72 years.”
The Winstanleys were in Sydney in the mid-thirties. We
find two of the name—William Winstanley; sign painter,
and Mrs. Winstanley, straw bonnet-maker, both in
Hunter street in 1835. In 1844 Mrs. Elizabeth Win-
stanley kept a tobacco shop at 296 Pitt-street, five doors
North of the Clown Tavern, then kept by George Coppin.
In 1847 Mrs. Winstanley sold tobacco and snuff at 112
King-street, and in 1850 the versatile lady kept the Nag’s
Head, at 193 Pitt-street, a few doors south of the School
of Arts. This is the lady whose death is recorded above.
Her daughter, Miss Eliza Winstanley, was the actress who
took to the stage in the thirties, and played the Desde-
mona to the Othello of Arabin on the opening night of
the old Victoria Theatre, Pitt-street, Sydney, March 26,
1868. In the orchestra of the theatre was Mr. H.
O’Flaherty (musician) who resided at Mrs. Winstanley’s,
at 296 Pitt-street. Miss Winstanley became Mrs.
O’Flaherty, and played for a time under that name. She,
however, appears to have renounced the name, possibly
on O’Flaherty’s death, and reappeared as Mrs. Winstanley,
under which name she played in England and Australia.
In private life, however, she seems to have retained the
O’Flaherty, as shown in the obituary notice. The Mrs.
Ximines mentioned above vm connected, with the Vic-
toria Theatre in 1847! This scribe has a picture of Mrs.
Winstanley as “Mistress Quickly.” At the death of the
mother Mrs. O’Flaherty and Mrs. Ximines were in Lon-
don. It is understood that the former died about 20 years
ago, at Woollahra.
A recent biographer records a quaint death-bed remark
of English tragedienne, Isabelle Dallas Glyn. A kind
lady, who was overflowing with commiseration regarding
Miss Glyn’s approaching end, received the stately reply:
“ Madam, my greatest successes in life have been death
scenes.”
Alfred Sutro, author of “ The Walls of Jericho,” has
written a French playlet—“Mme. la Marquise’’—which
was produced in Pans lately. It is said to be almost too
morbidly improper for the Parisians. The Marquise is a
morphia-maniac of the giddiest character. Strangely
enough her amour with a young doctor is the only one in
which her husband bowls her out, although, in the ordi-
nary way of social life, the medical man enjoys immunity
from danger. His opportunities with the lady patient
are so private and particular. When this careless prac-
titioner gets caught, the outraged hubby poisons his wife
and desires her lover (the doc.) to fix up the mat f er plea-
santly with a certificate of death from natural causes.
The doc., however, is in favor of administering an emetic
or something, to save Madame’s life, but the husband
catches him round the throat, and, whilst they are strug-
gling on the floor, the lady breathes her last. The doctor
signs the certificate.
“ F.”: Towns in North Queensland at present should
be booked for short seasons only. Rockhampton two
nights, Mt. Morgan two nights, Townsville not more
than three nights, Charters Towers r.ot more than four,
Cairns two, Mackay two. One thing that should be kept
in mind is that the Townsville people are mad on music,
and love opera, but abhor drama. Charters Towers, on
the other hand, gloats over melodrama, and isn’t keen on
music.
“ Hamer ” : Occasionally, when some prominent city
actor is ill, or dead, or something, or wants to make
people feel his loss—which they generally don’t feel as
much as he expects—an understudy is announced to pre-
sent his part, at some remarkably short notice. But, as
a rule, that alleged makeshift has studied that part
about as long as his principal, and the remarkably short
notice is mere flam. The really wonderful performer is the
useful provincial actor, who is liable to be shovelled, at
practically no notice at all, into a part he has never seen be-
fore. He is a marvel. He reads the lines off the documents
that represent the forged will, or the warrant, or the pass-
port ; or has them pasted on the paper that contains the
news of the death of the hero’s rich relative. I once knew
an actor who undertook to play a heavy part almost on the
instant. In addition to the devices I have mentioned, he
had some lines on his cuffs, which he found handy in the
places where he wept bitterly ; some more on his mother’s
portrait, which he gazed at fondly and addressed patheti-
cally ; and he had pi< ces of paper pinned to all the scenes
with more lines. But his real triumph lay in having the
remarks he had to make when he was reconciled to his
heavy father, pencilled on that weighty parent’s shirt-
front. Never shall I forget the wrath of the weighty
parent when the news was broken to him that his biled
rag was to be made a convenience of in this way.

The story told by the husband and his
witnesses in the Mulholland divorce case, recently
heard in Sydney, differs in some particulars from
the evidence usually tendered in similar proceed-
ings. The wife, it said, was addicted to drink,
drugs, spirit-rapping, and three co-respondents
(which would be sufficient to keepa lady busy). He
claims to have been a fairly indulgent husband.
He sent her on a trip to Maoriland at a
time when, he alleges, she was beginning to
consider herself a cut above him, and to
stick out her chest —or her bust. On the
voyage—so his story goes—she met the first
“ c 0.,” who was the manager of a variety show.
After she returned she corresponded with him,
and when the servant interfered, as Merely
Mary Ann is so fond of doing, and told
the husband of the alleged proceedings, the
husband sent her away on- two more trips.
The accused parties in the case don t appear
to have frequented the “certain house” so often
mentioned in happenings of the kind, but pre-
ferred, the allegations allege, to linger longingly in a
graveyard, where, on one occasion, thelady received
a message from her husband to be careful and not
sit on her hat. (It is a considerate husband that
will look after his wife’s hat what time she is
mooning round with another man in a cemetery.)
As to whether, on any occasion, the respondent
went to the cemetery on a damp, cold day to re-
cover from the effects of drugs, and there met the
alleged co-respondent who was suffering from
neuralgia, and whether they spent the time in
copying epitaphs, the evidence doesn’t reveal.
Also, the verdict upset a good many of the hus-
band’s allegations. But it was a curious case.
The discovery in the Torrens River (Ade-
laide) of the mutilated and bagged remains of
Mrs. Habibulla may possibly tend, for a time, to
discourage the white woman with the lamentable
tendency to marry our Asiatic brudder. Mr.
Habibulla was 40, and his wife, a comely white
woman, was much younger. Habibulla was
wealthy and lived up a lane, which is a
way the rich Asiatic has ; and he seems,
also, to have been morose and grumpy. The
trouble began on Sunday night or early on
Monday morning, when Habibulla’s horse brought
Habibulla’s blood-stained cart to the house of
Habibulla’s mother-in-law. The rest was a mere
matter of investigation. The police theory is that
when Habibulla, with the horse and cart and
the deceased bagged wife, reached the river, he
forgot to tie up his horse, and while he was getting
the remains put of the cart the animal bolted. This
(again according to the police theory) left Habi-
bulla with two alternatives—either to leave his
wife on the river bank, where she might be dis-
covered, and run like all perdition after the horse,
or to submerge the wife and then run like Gehenna
after the horse. The police reckon that he decided
to get rid of the wife first, and when he did run
like the very devil after the horse he couldn’t
recover it, that contrary animal having rushed,
with the evidence behind it, to the wrong house,
and delivered the cart into the hands of the
Philistines. After that Habibulla didn’t even
take the trouble to burn the dead woman’s blood-
stained clothes, which were found in a heap under
the bed.
Bent seems to have struck one of his lucid
intervals in his Bill to help the unemployed obtain
small holdings. Prosperity is based on employ-
ment, just as reason is based on the employment
of good sense, which can be greatly assisted by
D.C.L. Whisky. In fact, every interval is lucid
to the man who regularly, but not too often,
lubricates his intellect with the Whisky of the
Wise.*
The Australian National Defence League
(N.S.W. division) has begun the publication of a
magazine, The Call, with the excellent purpose

defence of our country is the duty of all.” The

interesting. Norman Lindsay contributes an
excellent cover design, and there are articles by
.T. C. Watson, Colonel Campbell, the Bishop of
Queensland, Professor MacCallum, A. W. Jose,
and others.

Extract from a letter received by Dr.
Arthur, president of the Australian Immigration
League, from a Scotch farmer in S’Africa, who
wants to come to Australia -
I have been in this country 24 years, and every year
seetns to get worse. It was much better before the Boer
war. It has been bad ever since.
The attempt to regenerate a country by filling it
with Chinamen seems to fail every time.
THE CIRCUS LION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The spoil of cunning human hands
The tawny lion lies,
Dreaming, perchance, of desert sands
And far-off Lybian skies.
He sees the tombs of kings rise dark,
The moonlight on the plain . . .
While a pale, narrow-shouldered clerk
Makes comments on his mane !
He rises, snarling at the hands
That point their feeble fun,
And longs, upon those midnight sands
To see the red blood run.
He beats the rougii boards with his tail,
His great jaws gnash their rage,
But what can royal strength avail
Against a bar-set cage?
His muscles strain, his eyes grow dark,
He crouches for a spring. . . .
Secure, the flaccid, weakling clerk
Baughs at the Captive King !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

Ewing, M.H.R., having been Acting
Postmaster-General, speaks as an expert
when he complains that the delivery of
mails is much retarded by attractive
girls. The trouble arises from the
fact that nice-looking maidens rush
to the door to take in the letter
when they hear the familiar
rat-tat, and then detain
the susceptible young
postman with frivo-
lous conversation.
There is a sad
side to this
situation which
even the ex-
pert has not
grasped. The
sorrowfulness
is best ex-
pressed in the
love-story of a
young Syd-
neyite (s a y /
John Jones) f
whose boss
h ear tless ly
shifted him out
to a branch in
the wilderness
just as he had
won the virgin
affections of the
sweet young thing
who was most
necessary to his
felicity here below.
However, Matilda
promised to cories-
pond freely, and .John wrote twice a week.
Presently the dear girl urged him to write
oftener, so he made it three a week.
Matilda was not yet satisfied; she con-
tinued to urge him to write more and
more, and John was so delighted with
this manifestation of her pure young
affections that he wrote daily for quite a
while. Then, just as lie was starting to
write twice a day, Matilda informed him
coldly that he needn’t write at all, for
she was engaged to the postman. Her
desire for letters from the old mash was
only a base conspiracy to bring the new
mash to the door. A more honorable
Matilda, who was mashed on another post-
man, used to enclose bits of newspaper to her-
self in envelopes in order to bring the object of
her silent adoration to her doorstep. She got
8p used to taking the letter from him and throw-
ing it carelessly in the fire that she burned, un-
read, an offer of marriage from a young farmer
with over 300 acres of freehold, unencumbered.

The widening of Camp bell-street, Sydney,
begins another discussion about the improvement
of that city. The present policy seems to be to
leave plenty of room for improvement. For
instance, months or years ago, an unsightly old
hotel was pulled down at Circular Quay, and the
triangular block of land on which it used to linger
was reserved to make a little garden at the front
sea-gate of Australia. That block of land has
remained a waste ever since—a patch of dust in
summer, and of mud in winter.* Sydney City
Council, which was understood to have taken
over the block temporarily from the Harbor
Trust, hasn’t even thrown a bit of grass-seed at it.
The frowsy, unkempt, dirty spirit still rules that
body, which has the government of a city blessed
by Nature above almost every other city on earth ;
and though it dreams now and then about doing
something, the amount actually accomplished is
infinitesimal.

“ S.S,” : Archbishop Carr (R.C.) refuses to
follow the Melbourne Anglican Archbishop’s cue,
and shut down on church
bazaar lotteries. The church
raffle, he says, is not . a
gamble; it is “a pleasant
game of chance.” How any-
thing can be a game of chance
without being a gamble the
eminent one doesn’t say.
Personally, I don’t think the
church raffle is either a
gamble or a game of chance.
It is more like plain rob-
bery. I’ve known the same
article to be raffled three
times in one night at a church
bazaar, the winner on each
occasion being persuaded by
the prettiest girls available
to give up the prize “for
the good of the cause.” I
don’t know what would
happen to him if he didn’t
give it up ; that uncertainty
supplies the only element
of chance or speculation in
the business. Personally, I
think he would be Lost.

THE ETERNAL DISH.
“ I don't think much of Jones as a lawyer; he
seems to make a hash of everything he gets.”
“ It's hereditary. His mother kept a boarding-
house.”
This is a hash-house lady’s wail :—•
Some weeks ago I saw an advertisement from a lodger.
He was looking for a landlady who “ had not seen better
days.” Some people thought it an excellent joke, like
the clown’s ancient string of sausages. Probably Elijah
made that remark to the widow whose cruse of oil and
barrel of meal never diminished. But I humbly ask : “Do
you think a woman could have worse days than the evil
struggling ones when she becomes Mrs. Todgers?”
Dickens faithfully portrayed the troubles of that lady,
simply on the question of gravy. The landlord gets the
lion’s share of the money, and the unfortunate widow
has usually to feed several rapacious, lean, hungry, half-
paid mortals at about Is. a day each to make her profit.
Consequently, after some months of being Todgers, she
is sold up and goes out scrubbing. The wife of a navvy
is a queen compared to the lone woman who takes in
boarders. I’m not quite sure that shovelling coals for
Lucifer Would not be a pleasant pastime in comparison.

Two volumes of Queen Victoria’s poor but
honest letters, carefully edited by Ned VII. and
his satellites, are about to fall with a sickening
thud at the feet of an unappreciative public,
Now that our health laws have become so stringent
as to the US9 of unprinted paper for food parcels,
it really seems superfluous to forward any copies
to Australia.

“Martyr” Worrall, interviewed in Sydney
(he is not going to Fiji, but is touring Australia,
where the limelight is), was still sore in the head
because, when he was "admitted into the fellow-
ship of Thy suffering,” the railway carriages on
the road to the Cross were not of the best quality.
\lso it transpires that he took a cab to Parlia-
ment House from the railway-station (the ordinary
citizen, who isn’t on his way to martyrdom, takes
a tram). Mr. Worrall would have been safe as a
martyr even in Nero’s days; no lion could have
got its teeth through his hide, to say nothing of
the fact that a swift cab could easily keep ahead
of a lion. * » *

If your muscles ache it may be your blood
— Wolfe’s Schnapps cures’rheumatism. If you
need a bracing tonic for general exhaustion,

The really irresistible lady-killers, the
wholesale bigamists who pick up moneyed brides
as they travel around, and take a new name every
time they get married, are all of the on) Claude
Melnotte type, and well-seasoned Claudes at
that. Their stock-in-trade is “style” and fashion-
able intelligence. They are never accused of pre-
tending to be persons of strong noble character
such as women reverence—in novels. They
merely pretend to be baronets, or lords, and they
go to America for their dupes. The fellow who
called himself Lord Sholto Douglas, &c., seems to
have brought off two successful marriages per
annum, on an average, up to the time when one
of his deserted wives took the trouble to get on
the track of his Past. He talked about his
brother’s castles in Scotland, and his aunt s
palaces by the Lake of Como, and he said
“haw!” in a tone that no maiden’s heart could
withstand. Even now that they have found him
out the women don’t disguise their admiration for
his “style.” He might return to all of them and
be forgiven in every instance. He was a man
after their own taste. The charm of him is
beautifully expressed by one wife who paid 0000
dollars for the joy of calling him husband. “He
was a most likeable wretch, and could tell such
entrancing stories of the British peerage that you
could almost feel yourself being presented at
Court.” And she an American ! Any country
may have a republican form of government, but
no country thusfar has achieved a republican form
of woman. Woman doesn t reason ; she only
feels. And she likes to feel herself being pre-
sented at Court.

“Gyp’s” narrative :
That story (8., 2/S/’OG) about the humorous Sydney
rag-man who once invited a shop-lifting lady to swallow
a box of medicine in his office reminds me indirectly of
something. When the soft, good-natured young man
gets hopelessly “gone” on the bar-girl, he’s a very hard
thing to shake off. Knew one of his kind once in Sydney.
His "name wasn’t Percy, but it should have been. He
said he wrote what he called poetry, hut there was only
his word for that. His love-making, and his statements
about his poetry, drove a bar-girl of my acquaintance
nearly crazy. Finding persuasion of no avail he used
threats. One day he was going to shoot her. The next
he was going to shoot himself, but he never did—that
was the worst of it. One morning he announced solemnly
that he intended to poison himself in the girl’s presence.
She hardly hoped for so much. However, he marched
tragically into the bar in the afternoon, seized a
glass and emptied a white powder into it. Adding water,
he drank the contents, then, with a fearsome yell, he
fell back against the wall, quite livid. The girl
had been bearing up strongly against the prospective
loss of Percy, but she didn’t expect him to bellow close
to her ear in that fearful fashion. The dying shout of
Percy unnerved her and nearly blew the roof of her head
off. She shrieked for the proprietor, and just managed
to pant out that there was a perpendicular corpse on the
premises before she fainted. The proprietor was tall, and
swift, and gaunt. He whirled Percy round on his axis,
seized him by the back of his garments, and rushed him
to the nearest chemist. The distance was 100yds., and
Percy’s feet only touched the ground once on the way.
The publican hurled Percy through the door, and ordered
him to be pumped out. The chemist only laughed.
Percy had gone there for the poison, and had got himself
up so carefully as the prospective suicide—with disor-
dered hair, and a wild eye, and a slouch hat, and a hollow
voice, and a manuscript clutched bitterly in his paw—
that the chemist reckoned on taking no chances, and
charged him 3s. 6d. for a penn’orth of something with
flour in it.

THE DISSIPATIONS OF THE DESTITUTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The destitute and unemployed were more often than
not to blame themselves for the position they were in,
because they wasted their wages in drink and other dissi-
pations. . . . Gambling was rife, also, amongst the
poorer classes, who very rarely attended church, and did
not heed the warnings of the church. . . . He had
noticed lately that the collection receipts were falling
off.— Excerpts from recent sermon by the Bishop of Man-
chester.
Sons of Belial! sons of Satan ! wicked squanderers of
wages,
Dead to every voice of warning ; though the righteous
parson rages
’Gainst your terrible libations you refuse to hear his
bawling,
Though for your sakes (it’s his business) he is ever, ever
calling,
Asking you to stop your gambling, check your wicked
lust for beer,
And your awful dissipation
Which will earn your soul’s damnation —
Talking thus is his vocation —
Yet like stubborn mules you heed not, and refuse his
voice to hear.
Dead to virtue ! Dead to honor ! Dead to every pious
feeling,
You won’t notice his implorings, nor attend his words of
healing ;
But you spend your weekly substance in the liquor that's
got froth on,
And give heed not to the wailings of the gent with
sombre cloth on ;
Though he points out where you’ll go to when you leave
this earthly sphere
You refuse to heed his warning,
And, his lamentations scorning,
Pawn your watch-chain in the morning
Just to spend what you raise on it in imbibing pints of
beer.
Prodigals and dissipators ! Do you know where you are
going ?
Won’t you take the path so narrow that the godly par-
son’s showing ?
Will you still invest a sixpence in that awful vice called
betting,
Though you know each time you do it nearer hell you’re
swiftly getting ?
Now’s the time to turn a new leaf, now’s the day and
now’s the year,
From such vice your soul dissever,
Listen to the parson clever,
Or you’ll go Below for ever,
If you spend your “ nothing weekly ” in the awful stuff
called beer.
Do not mind if you are hungry; just attend Unto the
churches;
Listen to the mellow voices of the parsons on their
perches ;
Do not jeopardise your only soul immortal by not going,
;Show the Lord your earthly duty—as per preacher—you
are doing;
You can die then as you should do ; aye, without a
trace of fear,
If you leave off dissipation,
Help to swell a congregation,
And with naught of hesitation,
Place spare silver in collections that you’d rather
spend in beer.
N.S.W.
T. A. NICCODES.

“Ropata”: A novel and economical de-
parture was, not long ago, witnessed at a burial
business in a Bay of Plenty (Maoriland) township.
The doctor died, and his friends, either from a
desire to keep down expenses or to give a familiar
look to the future residence, instead of the eus.
tomarv inscription, screwed the doctor’s door,
plate on his coffin. The last glimpse stated that
the corpse’s hours were from 10 to 12 and 4 to 6.

The Dookie (Vic.) college liquor inquiry
was a curious affair. Dookie is an agricultural
college where youths of 17 to 24 learn how to rear
calves and lure wheat from the ground, and some-
body or other (whose name is being kept dark)
/poured into Bent’s ear a dreadful tale of booze
' regarding the students. The darksome Somebody
told a similar tale to Coldwater-Minister
Swinburne, and, under pressure from the Ministers,
a Board of Inquiry was appointed—probabl y costing
some money. Bent said that his informant was
Gurr, an ancient, very bald person who once sat
in Parliament, and whose son was at Dookie
10 years ago, but Gurr smote his hairless brow
and denied that he had ever said a word. The
Board of Inquiry went round Dookie, questioning
and cross-questioning, for some days, and dis-
covered that once, at a football match, one student
had half-a-mug of beer out of a demijohn brought
by the visiting team. Outside that half-mus not
a smell of beer could he obtained, and the Board
decided _ that the hidden Somebody was a false,
unjust, injurious libeller. So the Somebody now
hides deeper in the dark than ever.
The case of Henry White, of Sydney, is a
whole volume of essays on the folly of not know-
ing when to leave bad alone. White was a
solicitor, and in that capacity he made some
foolish mistake about a title, and let a profitable
client fall in. That was bad, and calculated to
aggravate the profitable client, but still it left
White a solicitor—a Gentleman One. Not know-
ing when to leave bad alone, he surreptitiously
removed from the Registrar-General’s office cer-
tain documents—the ones which showed that lie
had made the blunder about the title as already
mentioned. The result was two years in gaol,
and White ceased to be a gentleman by Act of
Parliament, and became a mere bloke. Then,
having failed to realise where to leave bad alone
before he went into gaol, he failed, when
he came out, to know when to let worse
remain where it was. He invented one
Albert Morris Morson—-at least, so the
courts have decided —and A. M. Morson returned
the lost documents by letter, and declared in
writing that it was he and not White who had
taken them, and that White had been wrongfully
imprisoned and erroneously struck off the rolls.
The result is that White has gone to gaol again
for three years on a charge of attempting to per-
vert the ends of justice, &c.—in fact, he was
hardly out before he was in again. And all this
arose through failure, to realise the point at which
the subject had better be dropped.
At the instance of his “ wife,” aged 60, a
Melbourne man, aged 70, applied, last week, for
a formal nullification of their unlawful marriage,
and the Court had no option but to grant it. The
parties were supposed to have got bonded nine years
ago, but owing to an absurd shortcoming in the
Victorian Marriage Law, the contract was not
valid. The woman is the daughter of the man’s
deceased wife’s sister. Had she been her own
mother, the marriage with her mother’s deceased
sister’s husband would have been binding, but
because she is not so closely related to the man’s
first wife, the Law forbids her to become his
second wife. The droll fact that a man who can
marry his sister-in-law must not marry her daugh-
ter came to light some two years ago, since when
the daughters of the male petitioner in this case
had been “ throwing-off ” at their cousin, who
was living with them as their step-mother. Had
she been their aunt, they would not have been
entitled to “throw off,” but as it was too late to
alter the relationship, the 60-year-old lady took
advantage of the.slop-made law to get respectably
disunited from her mother’s deceased sister’s
widower. This case opens up the question
whether a man may marry his sister-in-law’s
grand-daughter, who might happen to be of
the same age as himself, and a peculiarly
suitable family match. And, if not, why not?
A woman of 40, married to a man of 30, may have
a sister aged 70, whose eldest grand-daughter
was born at the same moment as her grand-aunt’s
husband.

Bernard Macfadden, a Yankee physical
culturist, has sent the district of Spotswood (New
Jersey) into a condition bordering on revolu-
tion. Macfadden is a crank who denounces
the wearing of clothes as a filthy habit,
and holds that man—and woman-
should live by nuts alone. He has a
following of about 200 permanent dis-
ciples, and another hundred of float-
ing population are undergoing his
physical culture processes. In order
to carry out his principles. Macfad-
den looked around the United States
for a suitable location, and finally
fixed on Spotswood, where, for the
past three months, he has had a
camp of youths and maidens who
eat raw tree-products,
sleep in the open air, and
spend laborious days
toying with masses of
iron. Their spare time
they devote to basking
in the sun or swim-
niing in the streams.
Thelocal population
of Spots wood con-
sistsof descendants
of hard old Puri-
tans, and, as
everybody—male
and female in
the Macfadden
colony goes about
in the altogether,
the local Judkinses
have raged bitterly
until their voices have
given out. Efforts have
been made to have the law
on Macfadden, but without
avail. He keeps his naked
followers on private property, and
the conduct of all his students is
D| reported by the police to be irre-
■ proachable—barring their absence
W of clothes. A blushing police cap-
’ tain, who was despatched to make in-
quiries, was met by the chief lady
instructress —a muscular damsel, who
wore her only garment (a smile) with such
nonchalance that the officer beat a flabbergasted
retreat. The arrival of winter will probably
hurst up the new creed. A nude open-air religion
has no chance in a New Jersey winter.
* * * *
America is so proud of its antiquities that,
when two Adelaide journalists were recently in
New York, an electric-car conductor pointed
to a horse-tram in one of the suburbs, and told
them it was the only thing of its kind left in the
world. The travellers were modest, and did not
boast that in their native Snailville no other sort
of tram exists yet.
v * * *
Among the voluntary instructors of their
species who held forth in Sydney Domain, Sunday
before last, was one who taught how to make
infernal machines. He had a good grip of his
subject, and minutely and in clear business style
showed how materials costing a shilling or two
could be combined in a bomb warranted to slay a
score. With ss. worth of material and a shilling
wherewith to buy, as a conveyance for his bomb,
the father of all carpet-bags at a Johnny-all-sorts’
shop, he reckoned to demolish 100 of his species.
And, despite the thrill of liis subject, a mere
sprinkling of people listened to him with a sad,
indifferent yawn.

“Gip ” (B. 2/8/’O6) writes in a cold voice, that the
man who goes in for a drink is a bore to the bar-lady—in
capital letters, and without exception. Now, the general
average man adapts himself to the lady’s attitude, and
if he talks bosh it is because she talks the same way. As
a rule, the lady with a high complexion and a correspond-
ing voice has a terrible lot to say, and says it darn badly,
and she wouldn’t understand sense if she saw it. Cer-
tainly there are some men in straw hats and other signs
of inanity who would bore anyone, but “Gip” makes no
exception at all. It’s rot to say that the bar woman gets
tired of the men. She doesn’t. She revels in their com-
pany. Also, “Gip” says that midnight still sees the
Johnnies leaning over the counter and making sheep’s
eyes at the lady. Midnight doesn’t, because the Johnnie,
his hat and all belonging to him, are emptied out of the
hotel at 11 o’clock, or a few' minutes after.

queer experiences. Shop-hands, anxious to retain

“ Roma ” complains from Victoria : -
I quite agree with “ Lambda ” (Bulletin, 26/7/’O6) as to
State-school work. Taking one consideration with an-
other, there are not many points in it. It all sounds so
nice and easy when Mr. Director Tates mandates go
forth —swimming, calisthenics, nature study, brush-work,
sloyd, cooking, sewing, singing, drill, deep breathing
exercises, science, paper-work, gardening, exhibitions,
&c. Then the old arithmetic, grammar, geography, Sc.,
must not be neglected. Every bit of work done must be
dated, home work thoroughly corrected, notes ot all
lessons carefully written, &c. Classes in big schools are
subject to continual disorganisation. Some pupils have
to be allowed out on certain days for cantata practice, 01
the pursuit of rocks and insects. Teachers have to leave
their ordinry work and take charge of these extras, the-
work of the room falling on one teacher. The kid-con-
ductors who take charge of swimming pupils have to.
walk to the water-hole, or wherever it is, or pay their .owin
fares. All these interruptions to ordinary school work:
are not taken into consideration when the annual!
examination comes. Children are expected to know the.
programme work thoroughly; so the last days beioie 1
examination are days of bitter cram for the poor kids and
slavery for the over-worked teacher. When the new
syllabus came into vogue cramming was strictly pro-
hibited, but what good is the prohibition ? The struggle
to shove a quart of facts into a one-pint kid, and to drive
the square information into the round infant, has in-
creased rather than diminished, and some day the infant
will burst in the school-room.
