After unsuccessfully charging him with
half-a-dozen offences, the Victorian law authorities
at last managed to have “ Professor ” Kenip, or
Daly, convicted of annexing a horse at Cbiltern,
away h ick in April, ’99. The upstanding person
with the extended hair was nonchalant to the-
last; and, after keeping Judge Eagleson waiting
lialf-an-hour, he casually remarked that a stranger
had told him the court would not sit till that,
hour, so he hadn’t hurried. Mr. Eagleson, having
informed Kemp that he was “an audacious and]
expert horse-thief,” sent him for three years to a
place where the inmates habitually wear their hair
short. The yarns that were told by the police before
“ Professor” Kemp’s conviction, apropos the awful
trouble they had had to lay their hands on the
wild person, were mostly rubbish —intended to
explain away their own failures and paint Kemp
as a desperado of staggering slipperiness. As a
matter* of fact, Kemp, charged with hashing his
lodging-house keeper, was in the hands of the police
even during his Sydney buck-jumping season ;
and, if the Victorian force couldn’t arrest a man
when he was in a cell, it ought to be turned off
for a while to practise on catching worms.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(With apologetic salaams to liudyard Kipling.)
Vo doubt but ye are the Women—your throne is above the
Man's ;
H'A oso argues with you, he well must lay his plans.
Smiling the smile most ivitful, tinging the tone with fear,
Choosing the word well sweetened if he would have you
heat. i
Dowered by your doting lathers, or husbands (if ye tie
wives),
So that your purse be plenished, so ye have smoothened
lives;
So ye may roll in motors built for the body’s ease—
Swift, little smelly motors—and with gowns to wear in
these.
Will your fathers slave at the office ? Will your husbands
toil in the towns ?
While ye swoop o’er the gusty golf-links in your triply
tailored gowns—
While ye rove the shops for bargains, casting man’s gains
before
The satined swells of the showroom and the broadelot h’d
beaux at the door.
(Tepid, aloof, smooth-coiSured, unthinking, unthanking
smart,
Will ye smother your woman’s sweetness, will ye stifle
your woman’s heart,?)
Idle, openty idle, in the face of the undarned socks,
Idle, except for your pleasures, and the planning of your
frocks,
Ordering, spending, buying, getting and still to get—
Matinees, Bridge, and massage—ye who squander forget
Wealth was not. made witli the mountains, it is not
dipped from the deep ;
Men, not ye, have earned it—men, not ye, should keep.
But ye say, “ It will mar our pleasure ; ” ye say, “ It is
good for trade.”
Do ye wait till your husband’s patience is worn like a
binding braid ?
Till they go forth at morning, and the noon beholds them
broke ;
Till they come home at even, bearing the bankrupt’s
yoke.
.Vo doubt but ye are the women — absolute, smart and
wise,
The tinsel and toys of life —these are the things that ye
prize.
At your own doors, on your own souls, the spending and
saving lies.
vie.
P. N. K.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Bank-of-Victoria-chairman Edward '.Miller,
M. moaned at the bank’s half-yearly meeting regard-
ing Premier Bent’s policy of paying off debtsout of revenue,
or paying foreign debts by means of local loans. “We
have remitted £1,700,000 to London this year," Miller
mourned apprehensively, “ and if we remit another
£4,000,000 next year, it may be a Strain upon our Re-
sources, and money may become scarce and dear. There-
fore, I think it would be wise to renew part of our loan in
London." Miller is preaching, in an inverted sort of way,
the doctrine that The Bulletin always preached, that it
is better to keep the money in the country than to send it
away. But The Bulletin’s idea—which is also Premier
Bent’s idea —is that it is best to pay off the principal of the
foreign debt and keep the interest in the country, while
Miller’s idea is to keep the principal in the country and
send away the interest. M.Uer, to avoid sending away,
say, £4,000,000 to repay tlie principal of a debt, just now
would renew the loan for perhaps 30 years at 31 per cent,
and send away £4,200,000 in interest, and then have to
send away £4,000,000 to repay the original debt after all
—total sent away, £8,200,0 )0. The Bulletin would rather
send £4,000,000 away, and be done with it. It wants to
send away just about half as much money as Miller does.
Moreover, just now Australia has its pockets full of gold,
and can afford to send away £4,000,000. If it renews the
debt, the final and peremptory demand for the money
may come when its pockets are uncomfortably empty.
Miller said in his speech that it was an unhappy state of
affairs when money was dear. His policy would culmi-
nate in a time when money would not be simply dear —it
would be compoundly dear. And then the banks might
once again “ stand or fall together,” as they fell together
before.
* » • »
One good thing will be remembered to the
credit of Premier Garrutliers —his Bill providing that
N. Government stock registered in London may be
transferred to Sydney, but that - stock registered in
N.S.W. must not be transferred to London; in other
words, that foreign debt may be transformed into local
debt, but that local debt can’t become foreign debt. In
this matter Bent, of Victoria, was ahead of him, but it
was a good deed none the less.

This extraordinary result for 1906 is partly due to the re-
covery of £24,758, paid as municipal rates on the lines. Even
so, however, it is a prodigious return ; and no wonder
that, in a burst of joy, the directors wipe out the last of
the goodwill item “ Interest and sinking fund on deben-
tures of Tramway Trust, paid during construction of
lines.” The balance of this item was £31,366, and it is
now wiped off in one act. The following (giving totals
of the various reserves) shows what has been done with
the profits that have not been paid in dividends :
1905. 1906.
Capitalised profits £230,000 £230,000
Renewal reserve 270,546 269,841
For return of capital .. .. 77,200 109,902
Dividend reserve 12,000 12,000
Depreciation of investments .. 20,000 20,000
Other depreciation 45,000 80,000
Undivided profit 27,668 25,711
£682,413 £747,454
Less goodwill item in process of
extinction .. .. .. 31,366 Nil
Net reserves £651,047 £747,454
Showing net additions of £96,407 for the year, after
paying a dividend at the rate of 24 per cent, on the
actually-paid capital. Apart from the £24,758 previously
mentioned, the year shows £29,094 more profit than its
predecessor. Traffic receipts (£479,035) were £19,550
larger, interest (£22,797) was £4707 more ; and on the
other side rates amounted to only £10,915 as against
£14,248. These items account for most of the remark-
able spring in profits. The small balance is accounted for
by the fact that, in spite of the increased traffic receipts
of £19,550, working expenses were actually reduced.
Fuel, insurance, repairs and maintenance, wages
(£125,390), and general charges amounted to £232,145, as
against £234,375 the previous year.
What a lucrative business it is the foregoing
shows. But it is actually more lucrative than even that.
For it also Daid £45,000 into a sinking fund to extinguish
the debenture debt of the Tramway Trust. The munici-
palities of Melbourne formed this Trust and raised
£1,650,000 to build the tramways, which they then
handed over to the co. till J uly 1, 1916, when the whole
undertaking will pass back to the municipalities. The
co. has to pay interest, provide a sinking fund (which
till 1905 was £33,000 a year, but now is £45,000), and
maintain the lines, &c. The co. itself put up £250,000
to equip the lines; subsequently £230,000 of profit was
transferred to capital. After paying interest on the
Trust’s debentures, and the sinking fund, and writing
down leaseholds, and paying for repairs, and spending
£BOl7 on renewals, the profit, apart from the recovered
rates (£24,758), shows £52 13s. per cent, on the £250,000
paid by shareholders. The co. has now given many of its
hitherto underpaid men an extra sixpence per day. It is
a small, belated, but welcome mercy ; and it almost looks
as if the co. could afford it.
The co.’s paid capital now is nominally £480,000
in 960,000 shares. In liquid assets, after paying its debts,
it holds £590,148. Of non-liquid assets, exclusive of
leaseholds, it has £394,334, mostly freeholds. Together,
these items represent £984,482, which is equal to 20s. 6d.
per share. Possibly some idea of what the co. may do in
the next 10 years may be got from what it has done in
the past five. Take, then, this comparison :
June, ’Ol. June, ’O6.
Liquid Assets .. .. £400,303 £876,443
Freeholds 253,455 250,903
Other Property .. .. 154,436 143,431
£808,194 £1,270,777
Less Debts .. 67,347 286,295
Surplus .. .. £740,847 £984,482
An increase in assets of £243,635 in five years, after
paying for renewals, and providing for dividend. That is
to say, the co. has 20s. Od. per share now, of which a
fraction over ss. has been provided in the past five years ;
and it has 10 years more to go. One thing, however, to
consider is whether, though it has written down its non-
liquid assets, it will be able to get book value for them.

The year’s experience was really better than these figures
indicate, because £lO,OOO was appropriated to writedown
premises, and the staff got a bonus of £3500, while £3500
was handed to the officers’ pension fund, instead of £3OOO
in 1905 and £2500 in 1904. During the year 50,000 shares
were offered to shareholders at a premium of £1 ; but,
though a considerable amount has been received on
account of them, they do not rank for dividend till
Ootober Ist. The old issued capital was in 100,000 shares
of £7 10s., of which £2 10s. was paid ; 60,000 more shares
are now allotted, and a further 80,000 are in reserve. Of
course all the shares were applied for. A 50s. share
carrying 12 per cent., issued for 705., shows a return of
£8 Us. 6d. percent. ; and anything like that isn’t liable
to be spurned. It practically means a bonus of 30s. or
£2 per new share.
The figures of the National Bank of Maoriland
fairly well explain how the continued progress is main-
tained :
1905. 1906.
Notes in circulation .. £247,126 £268,872 Inc., £21,746
Bills in circulation, &c. 415,309 489,908 Inc., 74,599
Deposits .. .. 3,218,481 3,440,456 Inc., 221,975
Liquid Assets .. .. 1,079,425 1,148,623 Inc., 69,198
Advances .. .. 3,226,684 3,532,973 Inc., 306,289
Premises, &c., .. .. 80,617 72,099 Dec., 8,518
So the advances have outpaced the deposits, though the
increase in that line has been marked. In Australia, on
the other hand, thougn deposits have poured into the
banks, there has been no local demand for the money,
which has consequently been shipped to England, to be
let out there at about 2 per cent. The Commercial Bank-
ing Co. of Sydney has 64 per cent, of the public’s money
in unproductive cash, and in other liquid assets ; in 18
months its London balances have sprung from £15,000
to £1,917,034. But the National of Maoriland has been
able to find highly profitable use for both its own and the
public’s money, and only 27 per cent, of the latter is re-
presented by liquid assets. Thus the Maoriland bank
has only just half the N.S.W. bank’s proportion of
idle and semi-idle money ; and if it can make only 1
per cent, out of the public’s money in use, that means
£11,500 a year to the National. Australia is as good a
country as Maoriland ; but Australia has a fool tariff, and
anti-Sosh foolishness, and a horror of a progressive land-
tax, &e. ; while Maoriland has fairly high Protection, a
progressive land-tax, compulsory land resumption, &c.
Arid the difference between those two policies means, at
the very least, £11,500 extra profit for the Maoriland in-
stitution. Probably it actually means nearer £23,000 ;
for the Maoriland bank can not only use more of its
money, hut it doesn’t have to chase clients and offer
them loans at ridiculously low rates. On its shareholders’
funds the Commercial of Sydney made last half-year at
the rate of £6 4s. 6d. per cent, per annum. Here is the
record of the National of Maoriland ;
1900 .. £l3 3 2 per cent. 1904 .. £l3 17 2 per cent.
1901 .. 16 10 6 „ „ 1905 .. 10 13 11 „ „
1902.. 17 12 7 „ „ 1906.. 10 8 3 „ „
1903.. 13 7 2 „ „
And the profits on which the more recent figures are
based are net, after large deductions for writing-down
premises, &c. Even the bank clerk has shared in the
prosperity ; not only has a fine pension fund been built
up for him, but he gets a bonus. Yet it is hard to say
which shudders most at the thought of a graduated land-
tax and the other Maoriland horrors —the Australian
bank clerk or his boss.

1904 .. 1576 6 „ „ 1590 4782
1905 .. 2122 6 „ ~ 1590 532 5314
1906 .. 2171 6 „ „ 1590 581 5895
This co., like most trustee cos., charges a corpus com-
mission (from 1-) to 2| per cent.) on the capital value of
the estate, as well as an annual commission ; but it does
not show how it deals with the corpus commission—
whether it credits itself with the full amount when the
estate comes in, or whether it reserves some of it to help
the annual commission pay the subsequent expenses of
management. If the latter is not done, then, as the
annual commission does not always cover expenses, it
might easily happen that the co. would show no profit
at all in a year in which no new estates of much value
came in ; or, to make a profit, expenses might be cut
down to the detriment of the administration of trusts.
For a business of such great importance as they transact,
trustee cos., as a rule, supply ridiculously little informa-
tion as to their methods. The Queensland co.’s figures
are erratic:—
1901. 1904. 1905. 1906.
Revenue .. .. £6967 £5995 £6658 £7245
Salaries, fees .. 3464 3417 3426 3656
Other expenses .. 1007 877 972 1283
Profit on funds p.c. £B/11/9 £5/4/10 £7/1/3 £7/2/1
The variations suggest that the corpus commissions are
not spread out evenly over the period which it is esti-
mated a trust estate will take to wind up. On the other
hand, the commission may be taken credit for in lumps
as the estate is disposed of—and there is less to complain
of in that system. For the first time the value of the
estates in hand is shown —£1,408,236. The expenses
(apart from depreciation) are equal to 7s. per cent, on
that amount. This is extremely high—almost, if not
quite, the highest in Australia, so far as can be made out
from published figures. The Ballarat co., with less
estates on hand, works at ss. 2d. per cent.; and that has
been viewed as high. The S. Australian office, with
£1,413,954 of trusts—j ust about tlie same as the Queensland
office —is run at a cost of 4s. Bd. per cent. ; and the
bigger offices get down to about 2s. 6d. per cent. Pro-
bably there have been special circumstances about the
year’s work ; but, even so, the rate, judging by past years,
is high, and accounts largely for what, for a trustee co.,
is a poor return on capital and reserves. Whether the
people interested in the trust estates are getting the ad-
vantage of very superior administration is another
matter, and, as to that, the figures tell nothing.

A steady, profitable little business, about which not too
much information is given to either shareholders or
clients. Capital and reserves show for the half-year a
return at the rate of £8 10s. 3d. per cent, per annum.
The co. owes £7052 ; and among its assets—accounting
for nearly half of the total —is the item “ Sundry debtors,
£10,880,” which is vague. All revenue is lumped into one
item, so what the investments are yielding is hidden ; and
they may therefore be absurdly over-valued without any-
one being any the wiser. The law relating to 'r us tee cos.
badly needs attention. As matters stand, a trustee co.
could have lost all its capital and reserves, and still
flaunt enticing figures in the public eye. This co. isn t
that sort of co., as far as The Bulletin knows; but it
isn’t the law that stops it from being that sort.

1904 2155 4 p.c. = 1156 750 249 1129
1905 1320 3„ „ 900 420 1549
1906 1280 3„ „ 900 380 1929
This is deplorable. Year by' year shows a shrinkage in
profits, while the Australian imports of apparel and soft-
goods for tlie first six months of the year represented
£5,062,848 as against £4,461,188 in the first six months of
19U5, an increase in foreign imports, mostly of goods that
this and other Australian co.’s could make, of £601,660.
The co. pays a wretched 3 per cent, dividend; but it
shouldn’t, for it lias apparently charged nothing
whatever against revenue for two years for wear
and tear, and an annual 6 per cent, on ma-
chinery and plant alone would absorb £1393.
Fortunately, unlike some others, the (Jastleinaine Woollen
00. is not drifting into debt, though it lias £2900 of
debentures and £6224 of an overdraft. But if it isn’t
drifting into debt to others, it is drifting into its own
debt; that is to say, it is using up its plant (which repre-
sents its capital) and is not replacing it out of revenue.
And that can only continue for a little while, until the
plant has to be thrown aside. The present tariff has had
a fair trial, and has proved utterly ineffective. The
brutal alternative now is to sack the employees, scrap the
plant, and write off the capital as lost, or raise the tariff.
Going oil as at present will mean death in the end, and a
short, sharp shock is better than a lingering agony.

A little more activity in Queensland mining, a little more
settled feeling in the sugar business, and this co. is able
to show £2182 as available for writing down the book
value of assets to cover depreciation, &e. But though

<ssc. With this it has now been able to pay off half the de-
bentures, so that only £10,576 remain ; and that is a
relief, for they carry 6) per cent, interest.

Of this half-year’s profit £2OOO is turned into the reserve
fund. Apparently this reserve is independent of the
allowance for depreciation; anyway, the net profit is
stated at £5474 “after allowing for reserve and depreci-
ation.” But if the £2OOO is reserved profit, there is no
reason why it should not be shown as profit; the chances
are, therefore, that it is a reserve for renewals, and, to
some extent, is also a sum set aside for depreciation.
Whether the true net profit is £5474 or something con-
siderably more is therefore open to doubt. Compare the
position with that of five years ago :
1901. 1906. Increase.

But it still remains unsettled whether anything, and if

and loss account, £6 2s. 6s. per cent, per annum ; in the
past half-year, £5 12s. 9d. per cent, per annum. That
suggests either that the book-value has not been vigor-
ously written down, or that the works into which capital
has been put in recent years are not as profitable as the
older works were. This is a case, however, where one
has to almost entirely trust the directors and “go
blind ” ; and the man who can get inside information has
all the advantage.

Rockhampton Gas and Coke Co. (Q.)
Dividends and Written De-
Profit. Dividend Tax. Off. licit.
Dec., 1903 ..£1614 {^ P p.c. P o 7d! [ = £7l3 £747 795
June, 1904.. 1501 tfp.c. pref. „ 116 706 116.
Dec., 1904 .. 1666 \ ” 719 831 ~
June, 1905 .. 1237 9 p.c. pref. „ 116 1237 116
Dec., 1905.. 1337 L 717 504 -
June, 1906.. 1573 Nii 1573
This co. paid excessive dividends for many years, but
omitted to provide for depreciation or renewal; in other
words, it paid its dividends out of capital. Then, finding
its decayed assets ridiculously over-valued (the auditors
declined to sign the balance-sheet without remark), it
proceeded to write them down out of profits and set the
latter aside for renewals. But it was too late ; many of
the assets could no longer earn any profits.
There is an electric-light as well as a gas department;
the former cost £12,000 or £15,000 at least, and now shows
a steady loss without reckoning anything for wear and
tear. On top of their other troubles, shareholders have
the debenture trouble—£3o,ooo of it at one time, though
now reduced to £20,000. The co found out, what doesn’t
seem to have yet occurred to some others, that the
debenture-holder calls around for his interest regu-
larly, whether the plant that his money was
put into is worn out or not; he calls round
just the same, even though to run the decayed
plant means losing money. Altogether, the Rock-
hampton Co. is a valuable concern—to other people. It
shows them, among other things, how neeessary it is to
find out what provision a lighting co. is making for the
renewal of its plant, &c. ; and it also conveys a hint as to
the unwisdom of keeping a debenture-holder on the pre-
mises when the profits are big enough to gradually get
rid of him.

Besides brewing beer, the co. has gone into business as

These further figures show some other movements :
Dec., Dec., June,
1902. 1905. 1906.
Mortgages, debentures, &c.. £116,669 £124,602 £124,889

a lien on certain assets only, as a rule, while the deben-
ture-holder is practically a preferential shareholder, who
has a first claim on profits. The indebtedness is heavy,
and if money is coming in from the sale of lands the
debt isn’t shrinking, as it ought to do. What amount, if
any, is written off buildings and plant to cover deprecia-
tion is not disclosed ; it looks doubtful whether anything
at all has been written off in the past half-year. Any-
how, it seems evident that nothing has been written off
buildings. Under those circumstances, it is doubtful
whether the 6 per cent, dividend was more than barely
earned. And a co. with a debt much larger than its
capital, most of it represented by perishable plant,
buildings, &c., ought to show a wider margin than that.

There is also a contingent-liability reserve account,
£320 ; to this £6l has been added during the year. These
contingent liabilities, against which bills of sale are held,
total £7357. If anything is written off for depreciation
it is not disclosed. On the other hand, the items build-
ings, plant, &c., are steadily swelling, so additions are
being charged to capital account; and the capital is bor-
rowed. Last year overdraft, &c., amounted to £4014;
this year to £5463. Nearly everything depends upon
whether full provision is being made for bad debts and
wear and tear ; only the assumption that they are being
provided for in an unusually generous way would justify
the present dividend. And there is no sign that they are
being provided for in that way.

The capital, which stood at £16,420 six months ago, is
now £30,000. Other important changes have occurred in
connection with this co.’s policy of expansion. Twelve
months ago the co. owed the public only £717, and
its liquid assets, after paying dividend, were £3013.
Now it owes £34,960, and its liquid assets are
only £1143; and, moreover, further obligations are
being incurred. The principal liabdities are mortgages
£11,050, and bank £21,998. How the latter is secured is
not mentioned. It is an unsatisfactory feature of N.S.W.
company law that a co. may' issue debentures giving a
creditor a complete preference over others without dis-
closing the fact in its balance-sheet. Whether this is a
case of that sort can only fie guessed. Anyhow, here is
the fact: on a capital of £30,000, this co. now shoulders
the risks attending on an investment of about £72,000;
and to help capital it has poor little reserves of £5243,
nearly all of which shareholders have themselves put into
the business. They' are now practically' in the position
of having £32,000 of cumulative preference capital,
standing between them and dividends ; and yet they put
up their dividend to 10 per cent., and set aside a
wretched £226 to safeguard themselves, not to mention
getting themselves out of pawn. It is stated that the
net profit is shown after providing fully' for depreciation,
but the amount provided for depreciation isn’t men-
tioned, and cannot be computed. The same thing was
stated some years ago ; yet in 1904 it was found necessary
to practically write off all the alleged reserves. No doubt
if things go very well, the co. may make money out of
its borrowings; but it has taken very large risks, and its
present policy doesn’t seem to be to reduce the risks,
even when it has the chance.

* * * *
’Frisco has shocked the New Zealand Insurance
Co. into making itself “Limited.” Simultaneously it
issues 25,000 new £lO shares. The reserves were £440,986
in November last, and £300,000 will probably about see
the disastrous ’Frisco business through ; but the directors
are not willing to drain the reserves so severely. Evi-
dently the co. isn’t going to worry much to get Californian
business after this; the secretary has been sent to
’Frisco “to scrutinise every claim in the interests of
shareholders ; and the directors have decided to abide
strictly by the policy conditions.” In other words, the
co. is going to get out when it can. Foreign offices are
all pretty much alike, even when it is an Australasian
office that happens to be the foreigner. The new paid
capital of the N.Z. Go. will be £500,000, when the whole
of the £lO on the new shares is called. Dividends re-
cently have been 15 per cent, on £250,000 ; but the
average profits have been a little more than double that
for the past five years. The present shares, also £lO, are
only paid to £2 ; and presumably no more will be called
up on the new issue than on the old. The new shares are
to be issued “in such manner and on such terms as the
directors may appoint,” which seems to mean a stiff pre-
mium.
* ♦ * *
The South British, another of the big Maori-
land offices, isn’t “Limited” either ; but in any case its
shareholders would be liable for £lB per share, £1 paid,
it is comforting for insurers ; but it is a nightmare for
shareholders. For even though the South British has
reserves of £369,517, the New Zealand Co.’s experience
shows that, for practical purposes, the reserves are not
wholly available to pay a vast claim with. A co. usually
takes risks in proportion, to some extent, to its reserves;
and even a vast claim doesn’t reduce its risks anything
worth mentioning, wherefore it can’t afford to see all its
reserves go. With big reserves it can carry on, and make
a recovery ; without them it is lost for ever.
* * * *
For the first time since 1893 the United States
Treasury is buying silver for minting. Between 1890 and
1893 it made vast purchases of the metal under the Windom
and other Acts with the idea of keeping silver at an arti-
ficial value for currency purposes, and thesilver-miners had
a glorious time supplying the demand. In 1890 the price
ran up to 4s. 6|d. per oz. for standard metal. Then the
Indian currency was changed, Rand and Westralia
doubled the world’s output of gold, and the bottom fell
out of the bimetallist’s dream and the American silver
legislation simultaneously. The United States Trea-
sury was stuck with an enormous hoard of silver,
just as France had been previously, and it has
taken years to use up the excess. Meantime,
no new silver mines of any importance have been
discovered, and, though the output has been maintained,
it has only been through developments in metallurgy,
which have made it possible to treat poorer ore and waste
dumps at the old mines. What may be done in the way
of discovering new mines nobody knows ; but apart from
that, the outlook for silver seems bright. There probably
will be no high prices—because so many countries have
gone back on silver currency—but it seems very unlikely
that there will be any very low prices. The bottom was
touched in 1902-3 when a shade under Is. Old. was
reached.
* * •
Tin was quoted by cable at £191155. on Monday,
a jump of £l2 10s. Sydney Daily Telegraph couldn’t
stand that, and revised the cable, knocking off £lO, add-
ing a note saying what it had done. The spring, whether
it was £2los. or £l2 10s., was no doubt due to the United
States’ Treasury action in regard to silver. Tin and silver
are very sympathetic, mainly because the yellow person
who is by far the largest producer of tin is paid in silver,
and a rise in silver means increased cost of tin-production.
The good filling of old, worked-out places *in
the Broken Hill Prop, has been the salvation of the men
who have been working in Block 13 during the last 12
months. A gradual settlement has been taking place,
and if it wasn’t for the compact filling, the 300 ft. and
400 ft. levels would have gone with a run months ago.
There is a big crack between 300 ft. and 400 ft., and a fall
of ground occurred there a week or so back. The trouble
is mostly near Patterson shaft. Between there and
M‘Culloch shaft, ground which was beginning to bear too
heavily on the timber had to be let down, and it blocked
up the drive. Weatherly’s stopes are in work again, and
this will make up for any deficiency in ore-winning
caused by the Block 13 subsidence.
* * * *
The situation at B.H. Block 10 is not getting
any better. The mill is treating little more than half of
what it is capable of, and even that ore is being snatched
from any available corner. The supply is still mainly
coming from the 1215 ft., where the north drive is being
carried 10ft. wide—practically a drive and a stope at
the same time. This ore is good stuff. Big supplies
of timber are being dumped on the mine, and, by
the look of things underground, it will all be needed, for
a slight movement is still going on. The ropes are run-
ning through Campbell shaft, which has not become any
worse. At surface, it is intended to put in four Wilfleys
at the mill for the re-treatment of slimes, which it is
becoming difficult to keep within bounds.
* * * *
That wounded giant, the Sulphide Corpora-
tion’s Central mine, Broken Hill, is beginning to stir
again and fill his lungs. The emergency mill —the main
mill collapsed through a “ creep ”in the mine—is not yet
all ready, but a half section is being given a spin, also two
grinding pans. In opening up the broken ground a little
ore is being won, and this is being put through the
emergency plant. There is good ore in the crosscut at
the 1000 ft., which is to connect the Extended with the
main shaft; and that is 200 ft. below the present main
workings.
* * * *
J* -D* Kendall, who reported on Great Cobar
(N.S.W.) when the big deal nearly came off three years
ago, is on his way out to take charge. He is travelling
via the United States in order to get prices for an addi-
tional plant, sufficient to bring up the output to 15,000
tons a year.
1P • • *
he Bull-owned and mortgaged Pinnacles (far
west N.S.W.) is squeezing out a squalid existence by
picking over the dumps. The stope at the 270 ft. has a
height of about 60ft., and yet there isn’t enough timber
in the mine to make many packets of toothpicks. This
will have to be seen to before work is started again on a
decent scale.
* # * *
Shuttleton Mines (N.S.W.), following up the
good returns from the 24-ton parcel of sulphides treated
at Waratah, has dumped down on a comfortable ore body
first at the 380 ft. level and again at 410 ft. So far the lode
hasn’t been proved in length, but for a width of 14ft. aver-
age values can be taken at 8 per cent, copper. Shuttleton
is in 130,000 shares, of which 65,000 are paid to 10s. and
65,000 are contributing; and as most of the subscribed
capital is still available, the very important question
arises as to future policy. Mine has probably now enough
ore to warrant erection of a couple of reverbs.; but, seeing
that Crowl Creek furnaces are handy, and that that mine
has just bought 200 tons of seconds, there is sound reason,
for the present, in a policy of selling the ore mined to the
Crowl Creek people. This policy would prove the
Shuttleton, one way or the other, at the smallest cost.
* * * *
N. Wales’ newest discovery of alluvial gold
is at Wamboyne, 40 miles north of Wyalong ; but so far,
that is no justification for the rush of applications for
permits. Prospectors (Nelson and party) have sunk three
shafts, and bottomed at 28ft. on pipeclay, containing 2ft.
of wash. Prospects run up to 2dwt. to the load —which
is much too poor to rush to ; still, Nelson, an old Lamb-
ing Flat digger, is impressed with the locality.
• * * *
The wondrous finance of the Brisbane pro-
moters! Mount Canindah was in 300 shares at £25. It
struggled on with calls of £1 a month and the shipment
of small lots of picked ore, and drew close and quick
against documents. Now it has converted itself into
300,000 shares of 2s. each (it was to be 300,000 of £1 at
first, but considerations of stamp-duty cut down the
nominal figures by 90 per cent.). All the capital it has to
begin life on as a big co. is £1275, representing the last
call of the old syndicate. The little mill has started (20
tons a day capacity or thereabouts) on carbonates, and a
reverberatory is going up to deal with the concentrates.
There is some high-grade ore in the show (quantity not
estimatable) and a lot of low-grade (quantity similarly
unknown),; but that £1275 of working capital makes it
look like another “ skite ” mine.

Hampden-Cloncurry Co. has purchased the
Duchess mine (Q.) for £15,000 cash and 15,000 Hampden
shares. Thus the Duchess syndicate gets back the
£15,000 it paid the Kenned.ys for the mine, and has 15,000
Hampden shares to show for the £3OOO spent in develop-
ment. The lease recently pegged by the syndicate at the
old Tip Top camp, Agate Downs, is thrown in. Lonsdale
Smith, the new manager of the Hampden and the old
manager of the Duchess, recommended the purchase on
the strength of 4000 tons of 30 per cent, ore in sight and
“ probably in sight.” The existence of “ inferential ” ore
is a bad thing to figure on, and the fact that some mem-
bers of the old Ohillagoe crowd are behind the figuring
doesn't better it much ; for they don’t seem to have a
great deal of luck in guessing ore.
« * * *
O. copper mine (N.Q.) is putting up a record
in mine managers, having already uSed up.four, and
started on the fifth. They have a good man now, pro-
vided he can get along with his men ; but in any case he
is faced by more than one trouhle, want of water being
the most dangerous, though for the present there
is fairly good water to be got from the shafts.
Shareholders are continually asking why they don’t sink,
and, after the Mt. Garnet experience, well they might.
When Garnet was in full swing it was said that the ore
was practically unlimited, and shares were sold to about
£4. Over £200,000 worth of ore was taken out without
producing a dividend. This ore was all got above the 150 ft.
level, and was mostly carbonates. When the management
got a little deeper, the carbonates gave way to dirty
zinc, &c. The capital being exhausted, the co. collapsed.
What with the failure to sink, and one thing and another,
O.K. can’t get into the South’s confidence ; it takes only
the vaguest rumor to knock the market out. Then the
North (backed occasionally by Adelaide) buys ; and shares
creep up. But, until sinking is done, and new-manager
Shepherd gives his word that the' ore down there is all
right. Melbourne and Sydney will stand with O.K’s. in
their hand, read}' to throw them out at the first wink.
* * it *
Chillagoe (N.Q.) can mine and smelt at very low
cost. Extraction of copper from Chillagoe ore itself is
slightly over 3 per cent, copper and about 4s. 6d. worth
of silver. About two-thirds is oxidised fluxing ore, most
of it from an open cut. In June, production of copper
was estimated at 192 tons; 91 tons of that and 50760 z.
silver were from Chillagoe ore, and the balance of copper
and nearly 17,0000 z. silver were from outside ore. Chil-
lagoe evidently depends upon the available supplies of
oxidised fluxing ore, containing low copper values, other-
wise it would be ridiculous to smelt ore of such low grade.
Wallaroo and Moonta has been for years concentrating
better ore than Chillagoe smelts, and metallurgist Green-
way may not have been far out when he suggested con-
centration works for Ohillagoe.

* 9 * »
Catherine Reef United is another sample of
the goods the patient Bendigo-man makes his money out
of. All Bendigo knew the mine must come again ; so,
while the outsider thiew his shares on the market when
calls were made during the last few years, the confident
Bendigonian picked them up at a few shillings. They
were not quoted often in Melbourne those days, and
Sydney didn’t know them. Late last year the mine
started to “come,” on the strength of big stone at
2200 ft., and by the end of December shares stood
at 50s. At that point and during a later slump
a Sydney speculator came in. A fortnight ago
the return was sweetened by some rich stuff taken
from the far western side of the formation, and
5300 z. was reported from 400 tons, followed by an
eighteen-penny dividend. The market promptly jumped
past 72s. —at which point Melbourne to some extent, and
Sydney to a larger, came in, and the patient Bendigonian
went out. Yields of that size can’t be kept up ; the
formation is big (up to 60ft. wide), but patchy ; and
there happened to be a rich run available at the moment.
South New Moon, Bendigo, has frightened the
wits out of the specking gang of share dealers in Mel-
bourne. These chased the shares down the hill from the
time they were up at £l2, making profits all the way.
At first timid, they grew holder and bolder in their
attack. But the striking of stone in a crosscut
at 1060 ft. caused very much the same scatter as
takes place among a flock of crows when they see
signs of fight in an intended victim. Shares were there-
fore grabbed at, and so prices were rushed to a point
altogether out of proportion to the importance of the
strike. This is a pity ; for every supporter of mining
would like to see the same punishment given as befel
some of the offenders when South New Moon was on its
original up-grade. The stone now met with is 240 ft.
from the boundary, and there cannot be more than
enough to act as a sweetener to the low-grade quartz
which the co. has in every other section of the mine.
Had the management drawn a little more sparingly on
the rich stone beaten out in the past, this would not be
the case.

What the Long Tunnel and the Extended mean
to Walhalla (Vic.) is shown by the figures for the past
quarter. First-named handled 6656 tons for 69620 z.,
showing an average of loz. 22gr., and it paid
in divs. £4800; while the Extended broke 3763 tons
for 63310 z.; average, loz. 'Bdwt.; divs., £14,400.
Long Tunnel, so far, does not seem to be getting
much to brag about in the north-west ground, show-
ing more than ever its luck in opening’ up rich stone
south of the Extended boundary. Co. has put itself safe
by buying Hannan’s lease, west of the parent mine. As
there are outcrops of stone distinct from Cohen’s reef
out in that direction, the board ought to bore the country.
Directors have plenty to do just now, worrying over the new
electric pumps which, so far, don’t seem to be “fish,
fowl, or good red herring.” Until they prove to be one
of the three, there is an embargo on the opening up of
the north-west ground, on account of the water left in it
when work was stopped there by the co. in the old days.
Sydney’s next nibble in Victorian mining will
be at Long Tunnel; and the bait will be the purchase of
the West Long Tunnel, which may get Cohen’s reef some
day. So far as the near future is concerned, the deal
means very little ; but Sydney wall hug it as an excuse
for coming in, as usual, so late in the day.
* * » *
Cathcart Proprietary, Ararat (Vic.), was one of
the shows to which Bew'ick-Moreing’s long arm reached
in the search for an alluvial catchpenny to help them on
their way through Victoria. They got in cheap enough
for anything ; certainly on terms which the Minister of
Mines, in liis interest for shareholders, ought to have
revised before accepting the transfer of the lease. Now,
at the mines, rich wash has been pricked. The mascotte
has been ex-Spring-Hill Leads Browne, whom the
old directors failed to appoint at the twelfth
hour. Bewick, Moreing and Co. brought him in
from Rutherglen to advise them, and he hit the mark.
As the Ararat field was a great alluvial diggings, there is
far more reason to go deep-lead mining there than at
Moolort. The Cathcart lead so far has not been wide,
and the runs of gold in it are narrow. Their richness,
however, leavens the lot up, and as the claim has a big
area, Bull, if properly advised, ought to have a good
show ahead.
The alleged thefts of gold from Westralian
mines is making the Boulder bubble. The head of the
detective department, in a report to the Government,
stated that he believed hundreds of thousands of pounds
worth of gold were stolen yearly. The Chamber of Mines
put the steal at about £400,000, as there was this differ-
ence between the gold exported or received at the Mint
and the amount reported to the Mines Department.
Bewick, Moreing and Co., however, say it isn’t more than
£60,000 or £70,000. Scantlebury, who started the hue
and cry, gave the figure at £1,000,000, on the authority
of Warden Finnerty. A Royal Commission is to inquire
into the matter, and there the thing will end. That an
enormous amount of gold was stolen in the past there is
no doubt; but, as every big mine has now a change-room
and a searcher, and very few of the rich bonanzas remain,
the quantity got away with, at least by miners, must be
small. The scare will do W.A. mining no good ; and if
the official reports are accepted, it will be a still heavier
blow to 8., M. and Co., who have been paid fat fees to
protect shareholders’ interests.
* * * *
Recently Great Boulder, Kalgoorlie, has been
pushing- on development much more vigorously. The
main shaft is down 2050 ft., where the twentieth level will
be driven. The lode is opening up very satisfactorily at
1900 ft. ; it is very wide in places—2sft.-—and is worth 11
to 12dwt. Edwards shaft to the south, which long stood
at 1200 ft., is down 1900 ft. ; and Hamilton shaft, to the
north, is down about 1350 ft., where the next level will be
opened. This latter point is interesting-, as Hamilton
shaft was started in June, 1904, to prospect a lode cut by
the diamond drill at 1335 ft. That lode was said to be worth
25dwt. for a width of 10ft. This is supposed to be the
Ivanhoe East lode, which has developed so satisfactorily
at the 800 ft., 1000 ft., and 1200 ft. levels. A new mine has
now been opened up in this long-neglected north lease,
and must greatly lengthen the life of the Boulder. There
is no reason why the lode should not eventually be proved
right up to the surface. So far, the average value is
about 20dwt. for a good payable width.
■*r * * *
Boulder Main Reef’s call of 6d. per share, pay-
able on August 1, was not notified by cable. It looks
very much as if “colonial” holders are to be, or have
been, frozen out, as has often happened before. The worst
of it is there appears to be no redress.
* * » *
Harvey Patterson, on his recent visit to Kal-
goorlie, pegged out two 24-acre blocks on the alluvial flat
east of Brown Hill. As there are several feet of alluvial
covering the ground, no prospecting has been done on
this flat except by fossickers looking for deep leads. The
venture looks unpromising; but Patterson, who is a
believer in the divining rod, reckons he is on a good
wicket, and is game to back his belief in cash. He has
let a contract to test the ground down to. 500 ft. by drill-
ing. If the venture succeeds there will be a wild rush to
peg adjoining blocks. Interesting to recall that Patter-
son tested, with the divining rod, the South Gippsland at
Vosperton before acquiring it for the Queen Margaret,
and also the Nine of Hearts at Malcolm. Neither has
come up to expectations ; still they are not blanks.
* * * *
North End, Kalgoorlie, has managed to raise
fresh capital, and is adding a Babcock and Wilcox boiler
and air compressor to the plant. It is said that a
vigorous policy of development is to be pursued, but
vigor does not appear to be one of the attributes of those
in control. Property’s chances are good if concern only
gets a run.

The late Tom Doyle’s old lease at Kanowna
(W.A.) has just produced 4360 z. from 65 tons, bringing
up the total since its acquisition by the present holders
to 7780 z. from 109 tons. The gold realised nearly £3OOO,
and, as the lease Was bought for £l2OO, the venture must
have proved all right. The ground is very hard and the
leader small, but it is a good proposition for a working
party.
* * * *
Gregory Hills (W.A.) is the scene just now of
much wild romancing by alleged prospectors, and some-
body will be left with an offspring to nurse. The Reward
lease was first pegged out in October, 1903, by Bob Ki rkpat-
rick and Charlie Richmond ; but their samples, tested at
the State battery at Laverton, proved so unsatisfactory
that the lease was abandoned. In February, 1904, Rich-
mond again went out for a Laverton syndicate and re-
turned with scwt. of samples, which also proved too poor.
In 1905, Richmond had a third try in company with
Jim Thomas, but the result was again a failure.
Tom Whitford and Steve Wake then went out, each on
his own, and both claim the Reward lease. Whitford
floated his show in Adelaide arid Wake in Boulder, and
developments may prove interesting. The country is
auriferous and tempting, or Richmond would not have
returned three times ; also, it is quite possible that Whit-
ford has struck something good. But it is not a new
find, as quite a dozen prospectors were there before Whit-
ford or Wake ever saw it.
For some months past five opal miners from
White Cliffs have been exploiting a show called the Union
Jack, three miles south of Ooolgardie (W.A.). Thin veins
of opal, in many cases of good quality, have been found,
and the prospectors reckon that, sooner or later, they
will strike a pocket and a fortune. The discovery of a
pocket would mean a wild rush ; but until they do find it
the prospectors will be regarded as quite harmless.
* * * *
“Mul”: “T.’s” account (B. 12/7/ 06) of the
Pilbarra (W.A.) fields is much too gaudy. There are not
“over 500 men knocking out from £4 to £lO per week tin
collecting,” arid if “the work is light, as mining work
goes,” it must not be forgotten that Pilbarra is the next
station to Sheol. The average maximum shade tempera-
ture in one month of last year was 109. Wages men are
poorly paid at £4 10s. a week, and you can reckon up
what sort of place it is from that. There isn’t one decent
gold mine in the territory ; the place is kept alive by
alluvial tin workings, and three years or so will see their
finish. The poor man who thinks he can make his fortune
on Pilbarra is advised not to go.

Another factor in reducing the mining costs,
especially in large mines, is the general use of “Little
Wonder” Hand Hammer and “One Man” Compressed
Air Drills. Broken Hill mines use them extensively. The
managers favor these drills for their great strength and
simplicity having only one working part; also for
economy in air consumption as they require only about
25ft. free air per minute. Miners prefer them, as work is
made much easier; for tributors, “Wonder” drills are
the means of bringing in larger returns. Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne and Sydney, have de-
scriptive catalogues for those interested, and attention
is drawn to an exceedingly simple and ingenious patent
water attachment for allaying dust.*

Est., 1594.

Two sets of improved plunger pumps, Cornish
type, are at present being constructed for the “ keystone ”
shaft of the Loddon Valley Goldfields (Victoria) to the
order of Bewick, Moreing and Co. Each set will consist
of two lifts of 250 feet, the total lift being 500 feet. The
plungers are 26in. in diameter and 10ft. stroke and the
discharge from the two sets will be 6,000,000 gallons per
day. The valve boxes are made in separate pieces instead
of forming part of one H piece, and the valves are of the
compound flap type. The total weight of the two sets of
pumps is 150 tons. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company has this work in hand. This company has
constructed and erected most of the big volume deep
alluvial pumping plants now working in Victoria.* [n]
Not content with the splendid results obtained
by their concentrator, Messrs. W. L. Card and Son, the
inventors, have been working hard and experimenting
persistently to improve it. Whilst so engaged they made
what they consider the greatest discovery of the last 20
years in the concentrating art and that is their two-plane
adjustable deck. This with other important improve-
ments is embodied in the Improved Card Concentrator
which the licensees, N. Guthridge Ltd., are now
manufacturing.*

* * * ft
The Cathcart Proprietary Gold Mine, Ararat,
was the first mine in Victoria to adopt electricity as motive
power for pumping, hoisting and puddling. This plant
commenced work in September, 1904, and has been in
constant and successful operation ever since. It consists
of two return tube boilers, 160 horse-power engine, 110
kilowatt dynamo, switchboard, an electric hoist capable
of lifting 800 tons in 24 hours, an electric three-throw'
pump raising 25,000 gallons of water per hour and a 35
horsepower motor driving the puddlers. The dynamo
also provides electricity for 40 lights by means of which
the surface and underground workings are lighted.
Experience obtained on this mine during one year’s
service proves that: (1) electricity makes an actual saving
in fuel amounting to about £350 per annum ; (2) the
dynamo and motors cost practically nothing to maintain ;
(3) the cost of lubrication is reduced to a minimum ; (4)
electric motors require much less attention than
steam engines; and finally (5) electricity is much
more flexible than steam power, permitting ready exten-
sion or change as mining conditions frequently require.
The contractors for this installation were the Austral Otis
Engineering Company Limited, corner of Hanna and
Kavanagh-streets, South Melbourne, Sole Agents for Ganz
and Company, Budapest, Hungary.* [1

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New' Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fiuming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,

Hill, and has ordered Babcock and Wilcox water-tube

engineers, Messrs. Bewick, Moreing and Co., placing-

the South Blocks, also at the Hill. These instances
further prove the assertion that, wherever economy in
fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. This company stock boilers at “ Haymarket,”
Sydney.*

Aboriginalities.
“ W.P.8.” gives way to enthusiasm :-
He Oape gooseberries growing wild. There is nothing
extraordinary in the happening. Why should they not
grow here as well as anywhere else? At Norfolk Island,
'way down the South Pacific, these berries used to grow
in profusion and had to be dug up to prevent the Island
resolving itself into one large berry. How did they get
there 1 Couldn’t have been the birds, surely, and I
haven’t yet heard that the plant was indigenous or any
other word like it. The common or garden variety of
gooseberry did not appear, in my time at least. Yellow
guavas, with deliciously pink insides, grow wild at Norfolk
Island, and, in season, present a lovely study in yellow.
Birds are intensely fond of the fruit, likewise cattle.
Lemons of the large kind, big enough to wear a No. 7 hat,
are also in a state of wildness. And whatabout loquats ?
Phew 1 If anyone loves fruit let him go to Norfolk Island.
It is only a small spot stuck away on its own in the South
Pacific, but there is quality, if not quantity, to be had.
When one tires of guavas there are bananas, oranges,
mandarins, passion fruit (wild), peaches (wild), rose-
apples, cherrymoirs, red guavas, strawberries, peanuts,
figs and many other nice things. There are no cocoa-
nuts, and they won’t bear there. A lot of people are
mistaken about N.I. A business man went there
once to view the natural resources—they are not vast 1
When he came back he put in his bill, which showed J 620
for hotel expenses and £5 cab hire. There are neither
hotels nor cabs within 1000 miles. So that man would
appear untruthful. Norfolk Island is an oasis. A mere
speck on the ocean, and yet it enfolds all that Nature
(and fruit) delight on. I lived there, so I ought to know.
* * * *
“Starr”: A sulphur-crested cockatoo,
owned by an old lady at Mildura (Yic.) punt, ought
to win the cup for age. He has been 44 years in his
present billet. Drovers become inarticulate in
their struggles to curse him dead. His practice is
to sit well up in a huge gum-tree while sheep are
being run on to the punt, and issue cross orders to
the dogs, and deride the men. Some of the
drovers on that track would give almost any
money for a tree-climbing dog that could pursue
the cocky to his skyey bower and frighten seven
demons out of him.

“ Reily ” : “ Elmore ”is right. The wild
bee should be protected. Twenty years ago, when
there was a hive in every second tree, I’ve known
40 or 50 nests to be destroyed in one outing by
improvident honey-seekers, who gave no thought
to season or bee mortality. The wild wee beastie
is a curio in those places now. Bee-farmers,
though, have inadvertently benefited the insect.
Honey is cheap, and Australia would rather buy
it than hunt for it, so the bush-bee gets another
chance, such as it is.

“Kaban”: “ Werrimoo ” (B. 19/7/’O6)
gives Mrs. Wheeler’s honeymoon in London as
the probable occasion of the first introduction of
Coo-ee ’ to the Cold Country. Allow me to
state that I have discovered a use for “coo-ee”
much further north than London, and in an alto-
gether unexpected quarter. William Black, the
novelist describing a deer-stalking expedition in
the Hielan s, speaks of “ the startled ‘coo-ee ’ of
the long-beaked whaup.” The “whaup” is not
a bilious attack, but a bird known as the curlew.
Although it never leaves the British Islands itself,
it probably knows some feathered “cobber” who
does, and so the sound has spread.

“The Mussel” : Be snakes (B. 19/7/’O6).
Ihe tradition that the bereaved partner of a
recently defunct snake comes round to identify
the remains within a few hours after the shock-
mg tragedy does not seem
so wonderful, when you
come to think of it. The
snake does not lead a her-
mit's life ; if he did, the de-
crease in the birth-rate would
mean the extinction of his
species. Therefore snakes
must be more or less gregari-
ous. It seems unreasonable
to suppose that two snakes
meet casually, like swag-
gies out-back, say “Good-
day,” and pass on. I do
not know whether the
snake’s marriage-tie is per-
manent ; but the said tie
must at least extend over
some appreciable time. So
there appears to be nothing
wonderful about one snake
holding an inquest, or, at
least, turning up to drop a
tear or wag a woeful tail
over his late partner. When
a man kills a snake he
always feels more or less
bloated out with pride, and
challenges doubting friends,
between beers, to come out
and measure it for them-
selves; and this accounts for
the discovery of the second snake
before it has had time to pack. Also,
what is to prevent two snakes of the same sex
batching together? It is common to see the
tracks of two snakes across a dusty bush road,
and the tracks only a few feet apart.
* » * .
“Gip”: What a difference between the
exclusive high-bred magpie and the friendly old
black-and-white fellow that hangs about the door.
When I make my appearance in the morning, the
latter—about a dozen or so—greet me this way !
“ Mornin’, missus ! Cold, ain’t it ? Terrible frost
again last night; can’t get a drink this mornin’.
Hurry up and hand out our breakfast ; been
waitin’ here since daylight.” Then I stroll across
the paddocks to a quiet gully among the hills, and
I hear the cultured notes of the black mag :
“Here’s that impossible person coming again.
Do you think it’s necessary for me to move ? How
very boring ! Turned off, has she ? Thanks ! She
is such a dreadful person !” Thus do the black
mags talk languidly on in the spreading wattle.
Surely their plaintive drawl is the most woeful
and melancholy of all bush sounds.

“Reily”: Bunny is a very second-rate
pest in parts of this (Inverell, N.S.W.) district.
A spear-shaped grass seed —a drought result-
ranks classes ahead. It covers whole runs, and
mobs of sheep are tortured to death. Their flesh
fairly bristles with the seeds, which penetrate
wool, skin, and meat with ease. The suffering
animals are a misery to humane owners, and a
dead loss all round.

“ W.P.8.” : Oh, I say, let me say some-
thing re the long-discarded-habit discussion.
While pacing up and down the balcony
of my hotel like a savage beast, the
other evening, my thoughts were dis-
turbed by the howling of a member
of the Salvation Army in the street
below. Two girls were leaning over
the balcony, and, as I passed, I heard
them laugh, and caught the word
“pennies.” They turned to me, and
asked me if I. happened to be the proud
possessor of a coin. They were sweet
little tilings, so I dug the sum of two
pennies from out my trousers. They
disappeared inside, laughing, and I
proceeded to wear the balcony out.
The member, who was still howling,
was making it apparent to all and
sundry that once (20 years ago, he said)
he had been a had, bold, wicked man,
and knew not the straight path. His
friends had said he was past praying
for, and gave him up. But (glory!) he
was now saved, etc. Presently the
two girls emerged, still giggling in-
anely, and approached the balcony-rail.
I ceased my promenade to watch
developments. Down went the pennies
in a white-hot state. The member
stooped to pick them up, and fervently
said, “God bless you, sisters and
brothers.” As he grabbed the pennies,
which had fallen close to one another,
he reverted to his long-discarded habit.
I could scarcely believe my ears, but
there it was, and I should think that
that saved person had to start on the
straight path all over again.
“ Nulla ” : Said Blime Bill, who, doomed
(by the police) for a certain time to walk the
earth, had just returned : “ Yairs, these cockies
is early birds orlight. I didn’t expect me break-
fast in bed, y’ know ; but, I tell yer, I was won-
derin’ where th’ ’ell th’ sun ’ad got ter when th’
bloke I was graftin’ for wakes me up an’ tells me
not to be wastin’ th’ forenoon. ’E ’ad ther robbo
ready, waitin’ ter go ter town, an’, climbin’ in,
we jogs along. We musta gone twenty mile,
when I ’ears somethin’ flyin’ off ther cart, an’,
blime, yous blokes mightn’t b’lieve me, but it was
fowls what had been roostin’ there—it was just
cornin’ daylight.”
“ T.F.M.” : I like to read the stories that
appear occasionally in English magazines about
life in the Australian “backwoods.” They are
! full of useful information and very entertaining.
Have just finished a yarn of that type in Cham-
bers's -Journal, entitled “Hunting Wild Horses
in Australia.” A youngster, living in the back-
woods—a good rough-rider, as most Australian
youngsters are—while on his way home from
school sights a mob of wild horses just outside his
pa’s boundary fence, and in the mob is a beautiful
grey filly with a long, silvery mane and tail. The
young rough-rider, who is also an expert in throw-
ing the lasso, determines to make that filly his
own, and so, having first obtained pa’s permission
to absent himself from the backwoods seminary,
he sets off on his own next morning to hunt down
the filly. He makes an early start, just when the
sun is tipping the mountains, and, after riding
hard all day among diggers’ nests and wombat
holes, he succeeds, as the sun is sinking behind the
distant hills, in lassoing that filly just as she is
about to disappear in a thicket. The filly is at
full gallop, and so is the young Nimrod ; and yet
to spring from his horse and give the rope a twist
round a tree is but the work of a second. When
young Kemp captures the filly (T think the
youngster’s name must have been Kemp) he is
away in the heart of the forest, many miles from
any trace of civilisation ; and as it is getting dark
there is nothing else for it but to bivouac for the
night, which he does, after tying the filly to a tree
and killing a few opossums for the dogs. How
the opossums are killed the author does not men-
tion, but possibly they are lassoed also. Like
most Australian youths, especially if they happen
to get benighted in the backwoods of Woolloo-
mooloo and similar solitudes, our hero is an early
riser ; and, as the first grey streaks of dawn shoot
through the tall gums next morning, he unhobbles
the filly, and, after handling her for an hour or
two, leads her home. It is a simple tale of life in
the Australian forest where wild horses come and
put their heads in at the window of the commer-
cial academy, till at last the teacher goes out in
disgust and captures them to prevent them inter-
rupting the history lesson.
* * * *
“ Gr.M.” : Strange that, notwithstanding
his great intelligence, the dog may be found with-
out presentiment even of personal peril. An in-
stance. Our dog (a young “lightweight”) and
that next door (a bull-terrier “heavy”) are on
bad terms. Through a hole in the fence the
terrier, after hunting his own territory, daily
enters and works ours in search of provender,
forcibly taking from our dog any bone on which he
may be engaged, and biting him if he resists. To-
day he lies in his yard, sick from over-eating. To
tempt him his owner placed a sheep’s liver before
his nose ; he couldn’t touch it. Our dog has been
in, realised the situation, eaten the liver, and,
before retiring, registered the size of his teeth in
the terrier’s ear —fleeing before the unearthly
howl that this excited. He is now basking in the
sun, day-dreaming, apparently without the
faintest prevision that by to-morrow, at latest, he
will have an idea of what the ’Frisco earthquake
was like.

N. S. Wales has now 2730 full-blood
aborigines and 4180 half-castes. As compared
with last year, the full-bloods decreased by 41.
In 1882, when the first census was taken, there
were G 540 full-blood aborigines.
POETRY AND PROSE.
N.S.W. Fisheries Board talks of stirring
up the Chief Secretary to put a price on shags
heads. The Government is spending money freely
on the introduction of fish ova into river and lake
waters, and keeps a big live Board made of meat
to manage things, but all the time the shag—the
insatiable foe of young fish —is practically pro-
tected by the law, and spends its time undoing all
this work. It is an offence to discharge a firearm
at the ravenous cormorants that infest the lagoons
of Sydney Centennial Park and neighboring
waters. These lagoons could, in a few years, be
made fish-nurseries for the State if the birds that
prey on young fish were kept down.
LINES TO MY BRIDLE HAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hand o’ mine I
Idle, ambitionless hand o’ mine 1
Time has made wreck of
The days lang syne
When over the neck of
Old Number Nine
Wheeling the stragglers into line
The reins hung loose in you !
What is the use in you,
Hand o’ mine ?
Hand o’ mine !
White and woman-soft hand o’ mine !
Once you were brown with the
Kiss of the sun
(Light that went down with the
Days that are done);
Now you are groom of the
Paper and pen,
White as the tomb of l he
Days that were then.
Hand o’ mine !
Careless, comrade hand o’ mine !
You steadied the glass
While I poured the wine,
And you held the lass
While her waist I spanned
With a glad right hand !
Maybe ’twas rude of you ;
Never repine !
What is the good of you,
Hand o’ mine?
U.S.A.
WILL H. OGILNIE.

DISSOLUTION OP PARTNERSHIP.
NOTICE is hereby given that the Partnership hereto-
fore subsisting between us the undersigned NOTALLA
HABBIBULLA, MUCK MADEEN, MOASS, AFZUL,
ADJUNE, GOOLOB, and AKBAR AZIMULLAH, carry-
ing on business as Camel-owners and Carriers at Broken
Hill and in the West Darling District, New South Wales,
under the style or firm of NOTALLA HABBIBULLA, has
been DISSOLVED as from the 18th day of July, 1906, so
far as concerns the said Notalla Habbibulla and Afzul,
who retire from the said firm.
Dated at Broken Hill the 19th day of July, 1906.

“F.H.R.” : Be “ Waste Paper Basket’s ”
yarn (B. 26/7/’O6). A snake will attack, and even
chase, any person who intervenes between it and
“home” if that person takes the aggressive ; but.
the chase is a very feeble one. Twice have I been
chased by snakes—once by a brown and once by
a black, but never by a black and white snake or
a check one. The black snake merely charged me
so as to get a clear run at his stump, into which
he whirled like a black pudding down a dog.
The brown serpent was more annoyed. I was
going across a ploughed paddock, carrying a small
hag of broken quartz specimens. When I met
• Mr. Brown I told him I was sorry I had forgotten
my walking-stick, but I threw the bag of quartz
fair on his back. The soft soil beneath him was
his salvation. He moved on a little, and then 1
repeated the dose. Something annoyed him
perhaps he objected to being pelted with a wild-
cat. He pursued me for about twenty yards, but
[ do not think he seriously wanted to catch me,
for a snake is one of the quickest things alive if
it really means going, and a ploughed paddock is
not good for a man to make records on. I believe
“ W.P.8.” to be a Meat Trust.
* * * *
“ Yarrab ” : A detail observed in George-
street (Sydney) during the week impels the belief
that some of the Mulga contingent know a thing
or two. I observed an unbarbered horse and un-
fashionable turn-out, the latter seating a pair of
country shes, halt outside a meal factory. The
girls leaped out with great energy, tethered the
moke, produced a screw-wrench, and loosened the
axle-nut. Wrench and nut were then packed into
a small bag, and in went the girls for their forage.
Then the force of the proceeding struck me.
Taking off the nut made the removal of the turn-
out an impossibility. The idea was precaution
against the city vehicle thief. The insulted ex-
pression on the face of an adjacent leaner was
good to see, and he said “ By. cripes ! ” in a bitter
tone.

“ Quambat ” (whose address is illegible)
writes to say that, when a dog has taken a
poisoned bait, you should seize the animal by the
tail and swing it round your head till you are at
the last gasp. When you drop the dog it will
be sick, and next day there will be nothing wrong
with it, bar a certain limpness and flabbiness
about the tail.

“P.X.”: The climbing kangaroo is the
most interesting animal of the North Queensland
tableland ; he is more interesting and not half as
dangerous as the wild cat, which also flourishes in
those parts. The wild-cat lives chiefly on the
juice of Southern Jugginses, while the climbing
kangaroo is a vegetarian. A good many of the
latter have been caught, but they mostly die on
the way down, probably because of errors of diet,
1 bey are little fellows, rarely bigger than a wal-
r 'y> an d spring from bough to bough rather than
climb; and, like the’possum, they do most of their
tree-top wandering by night. They make a noise,
too, like the ’possum, but louder.
NOT NUMEROUS, BUT DENSE.
VISITOR : “ The population of Tinpan is not very dense.”
RESIDENT : “ Garn! There's heaps like Mickie here.”
What is Queensland going to do about its
prickly-pear curse ? It is a most serious evil, for
the pest is slowly but surely locking up millions of
acres of good country. Even as far west as Ounna-
mulla and Charleville squatters are fencing off
areas which are hopelessly given up to it. Less
than 50 yearsago the pear, coddled as a hedge plant,
was just beginning to get a hold on the Darling
Downs. To-day it has gathered in four million
acres of country. With no special steps taken,
what will be the position in another 20 years?
Will the accursed cactus be in possession of Queen-
street, Brisbane, by then ? There is “ another
side,” and a profitable one, to the rabbit curse ;
but there seems no likelihood of working up a
trade in potted prickles or fricasseed pear.
“Crow”: Saw a gin peacefully smoking
with a young piccaninny close by. A tame crow
-L s n ■ i j «■
''' as on !l near fence ’ Gin c,ozed off ’
Crow flew down, pecked the piccaninny on the
head, flew back again, and quietly watched the
gin soothing the offspring and looking round for
the trouble. When all was again peaceful in the
lubra’s home, and she settled down again, the
crow repeated his performance, but the gin’s sus-
picions were aroused, and without waiting to em-
panel a jury she hit the crow in the waistcoat
with her yam stick, much to the surprise of the
murky fowl.
SALLY.
[For The Bulletin.]
Ridin’ by ther river,
On ther way ter plough ;
By er little cottage
Fair gal milkin’ cow.
As I says “ Good mornin’,”
Says “ Good mornin’ ” back.
Turned awhile ter watch ’er
Ridin’ down ther track.
Then, o’ other mornin’s,
Fren’s we got, yer know ;
An’ I called ’er “ Sally,”
An’ she called me “ Joe.”
Useter stop ’n’ gossip
Sometimes quite er while ;
An’ I liked ’er manner—
Sorter liked ’er style.
Sorter loved each other. . . .
Then ther river rose
Through ther spring sun meltin’
O’ ther mountain snows.
Nuthin’ extra dang’rous,
Wasn’t quite bank-’igh,
’N’ one morn, es usual,
I went ridin’ by.
Looked across ther gateway—
Neither cow nor gal;
Silence roun’ ther cottage—
Wondered : “ Where is Sal ? ”
Then I sees ’er mother
From ther river come,
Moaning' ’n’ a-weepin’.
Thinks I : “ This is rum ! ”
Got it from ’er slowly. . . .
Told indoors ter stay,
Fred, ther youngest kiddy,
Somehow slipped away ;
Itunnin’ ter ther river,
Tripped upon ther bank ;
Saw Sal run ter save ’ini :
Grabbed’m. Then they sank.
Never saw ’er after,
Swept away ter sea,
Laughin’ Sally Morris
Brave es gal could be.
Yet it sets me thinkin’
Of ’er, even now
When I see gals sittin’
Milkin’ of er cow.
MAORILAND. TE AUOHA.

Nulla: An infusion of blue-gum leaves would doubtless
be good for a poisoned leg; it would contain some of the
antiseptic eucalyptus oil. But it would not be an ideal
remedy Land Tax : The “ unimproved value ”of land
does not mean its original value when selected, but its
present value, apart from the value of all crops, build-
ings, etc., upon it. Suppose next your farm of 500 acres
there is another area of 500 acres of the same soil and
equal in every way, but not cleared or improved. The
value of that unimproved area would be equal to
the unimproved value of your land, and would be
the value on which you would pay the tax, if
that value were above the exemption limit

The noble forests of our land
Beneath man’s devasting hand
Soon will have vanished, leaving there
This country lying, gaunt and bare.
No wooded clothing, now she’s old,
To save her from her death of cold,
But lo ! new Woods arise with healing sure.
All hail then—Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure !

There are nine indirect references to Jack
Wren in Shakspeare. The most salient are :
“The wrens make prey where eagles dare not”
(“ Richard III.”); “ The poor wren, most diminu-
tive of birds, will fight” (“Macbeth”). Shak-
speare makes no mention of Collingwood or the
tote.
Itan ” : Re queer names. A family, which
used to reside at Singleton (N.S.W.), was bur-
dened with these : Rex, Baron, Count, Queenie,
Duke, Princess. The nextones were to have been
called Emperor and Justice of the Peace; but
there were no more next ones—Nature presum-
ably revolted.
Ex*Senator Ned Harney, Westralia’s most
eloquent and expensive lawyer, emigrates to the
Cold Country shortly. Harney is giving up a
local practice; said to be Worth from £4OOO to
£5OOO, to start from scratch in Lincoln’s Inn
Fields, or thereabouts. He has been 10 years in
W.A.. and could have had a judgeship had he
Wanted it,
The story goes round Maorilitnd that one
of Seel don’s West Coast admirers was in Welling-
ton wheh Holman Hunt’s picture, “The Light of
the World,” was being exhibited. He stood
before it for some time; and then in ad© just one
remark, “Well, 1 doti’t think much of it. It
isn’t at all like Dick.”
The lately-deceased Geo. W. Waterhouse
put up an unusual record in politics. He ran two
Ministries in South Australia —the first lasted
nine days in 1861, and the second, which closely
followed the first, hfing out for nearly two years.
Nine , years later he bobbed Up as Premier of
Maori land, and though he did nothing remark-
able ill any one of his Premierships, still it was
something of a record.
Louis Horwitz, tile Hamilton (Vic.) soli-
citor, who is doing a long tferin in Geelong Gaol
for a disorder of the chest, has been Undergoing
an examination in insolvency for weeks past.
The assignee, though an expert accountant, has
been unable to make head or tail of some of the
clever man’s accounts. Horwitz, however, could
always explain them in a twinkling. His actuarial
methods were of the most complicated descrip-
tion—a fact that leads to the conclusion that the
first requisite for a clever embezzler is an intricate
system of bookkeeping.
The “ mana ” of Rua Tapu (if that is his
name ; The Bulletin lias had eight letters from
Maoriland giving five different spelling of Rua), the
Maori prophet, who foretold that Jesus Christ and
Edward VII. would meet at Gisborne last month,
has vanished, and he is now a deteriorated seer,
sneaking home to Urewerabv the back road. The
Gisborne natives met him on his arrival with a
cold stare, and invited him to camp in his own
tents outside the pah limits. They have a pro-
phet of their own there, one Wereta, who met his
rival in wordy strife, and left his reputation as a
preacher hanging in tatters on a bush. The
pakehas who took advantage of the Maoris’ tem-
porary insanity, and bought land from them at
ridiculous prices, will get the worst of the deal,
as the magistrate in charge of native lands will
not sanction the sale. Not only will the pakehas
not get the land, but they have little hope of
getting the money back. Consequently, most of
Rua’s followers had their pilgrimage on the
cheap.
THE BACKBONE OF THE COUNTRY.
PARSON (on tour): “ Yes, my friend, your

NATIVE: “ Oh, I unnerstand! You want nice
girl, eh!”
One of the shortest terms in Queensland
Fossil House was served by W. H. Rawlings, who
died, the other day, within a fortnight of his
appointment. Twenty-five years ago Rawlings
and the late “Billy” Browne (who joined with
Morgan in forming the Democratic Government
that displaced the old Tory continuation of
Boodle) walked into Irvinebank carrying their
swags. Both developed lung disease, and to-day
they both lie in Toowong cemetery.
In the official list of members of the House
of Reps., nearly every man is called “ The Honor-
able.” Some wear the title because they are, or
have been, members of Ministries; others, because
they were in the first Commonwealth Parliament.
These are the men who do not bear the foolish
prefix: Carpenter, Culpin, Frazer, Gibb, Hutchi-
son, Lee, Johnson, Kelly, Liddell, Lonsdale,
Maloney, McWilliams, Robinson, Storfer, Thom-
son (Q.), Webster, and Wilson.

The late Bishop Langley was less tolerant
than his brother, the Sydney Archdeacon. When
the Bishop was Archdeacon, some of his church
officers, one Sunday, placed on the communion-
table a bowl full of the beautiful works of God
in the shape of white flowers and ferns. The
Archdeacon noticed it during the service, and
stopped in the middle of that dreary work of man,
his sermon, to shoot the bowl and the beautiful
works of God through the vestry door.
Mr. C. E. Pilcher, K.C., is on board the
China on his return to Sydney and fat briefs.
And again it is in the air that N.S.W. Chief
Justice Darley, now aged 76, intends to retire next
November, when he has completed 20 years of
service, and that Pilcher will be on hand to refuse
the appointment or else to rush at it with his
mouth open. There are, however, instances where
Irish Judges have held on to the Bench at ages
far in excess of Mr. Parley’s. Chief Justice
Lefroy sat until he was something like 90, and
Baron Pennefather, at over 80, had been blind for
years and yet hung on to his job and his horse-
hair. Ireland seems to have supplied the boss
records of the Empire as far as concerned the
lean and slippered pantaloon lingering toothless
at the fount of justice.
This is a true incident in the career of a
massive Melbourne professional gentleman
whose special province is the law. Mack
is taller and broader than two ordinary
lawyers, and when lie fell ill, as a result
of wet, cold, and exposure on a walking
tour, and contracted fever, the family
was driven to the necessity of calling in
two lusty, male nurses to hold him
down in his bed, one for day, the
other for night-shift. One morning,
when the day-shift came on, he found
the night-shift looking so peaked and
unwound that he invited him over to
the corner to be stimulated, and, as the
patient was just then sleeping in peace,
night-shift went thankfully. Aquarter-
of-an-hour later Mack was to be seen
walking through Fitzroy Gardens in a
fluttering shirt and a belltopper, and
carrying in his hand a black brief-
bag. By the time the stray giant had
reached the Treasury reserve he had
a small army after him, and if he had
not been interfered with he would
have sailed down Collins-street to his
office in his amazing dress. But three
policemen and a cab intervened, and
Mack was wheeled home again, still
carrying his little bag, and still
wearing the pure, black belltopper of
a blameless life. The little bag was
most characteristic. No Melbourne
business man is respectably dressed without his
small ’ f)^ c k bag _
