From Adelaide
Philanthropist Charles Goode, of this city, at present
wandering about in cold parts, put his foot into it t’other
day. He was standing on the platform of an English rail-
vvay station when the Cardiff train passed through. A
elenryman leant out of a carriage window and accosted
o,d °- H - Goode, saying “ Don’t you know me? I m James,
o{ Adelaide.” Goode jumped to the conclusion that it
was the giddy and notorious James who lately left W. A.
with a female person; and being a righteous old gentle-
n™n’.he s ™ ppe< } back and declined to recognise the
offender. Then he wrote out to Adelaide telling how he
had given the glassy eye to a very bad man, and an Ade-
laide paper published his letter. Next day, a perfect
storm of correspondence assailed the paper, pointing out
that old C.H.G. had turned the cold shoulder of mockery
pn the wrong pareon-that this Rev. James was a blame-
old Goode, who wouldn’t insult a perfectly honest
shepherd for any money, will be a broken-up man when
he hears what he has done.
Mrs. Moorhouse, wife of Bishop Moor-
house, of Melbourne anrl Manchester, whose death
in England is chronicled, was the centre of a
sensational incident in Melbourne, over twenty
years ago. The Bishop, in a burst of indiscreet
eloquence, inveighed against the State education
system, on the ground that under it children
practised immorality. When badgered by indig-
nant questioners, he gave what professed to be
illustrations, based on the confidential informa-
tion of ladies. That did not satisfy the angry
questioners, and the incident closed with his
admission that he got the story from his wife,
who, it was presumed, had heard it in a vague
sort of way from some other lady.
Ex-Australian Railway Commissioner
John Matbieson, recently dead in England at the
age of 60, began his career at 13 with the Glasgow
and South-Western Railway 00. (Scotland), and
rose from booking-clerk to superintendent. In
1889 Thomas M‘llwraith made him Queensland’s
Chief Railway Commissioner. He held the posi-
tion until 1896, when he became Victoria’s Railway
Commissioner. Then, in 1900, he retired to Eng-
land, and became manager for the Midland Rail-
way Co. In view of the loud demands for an
imported Railway Commissioner with foreign
experience for N.S.W., it is interesting to note
that Mathieson’s 14 years Australian experience
led to his appointment to control an English
railway system worth £lB9,ooo,ooo—nearly as
much as the whole Australian public debt.
“ Ropata ” : Read in Fitchett’s paper—
Lifted, or some such title—that Australia pos-
sessed a second Macaulay in the person of. Fit
chett himself. This week a friend sent me a
second copy of the magazine, and I set myself to
study the new Macaulay. In a five-page article
written by Fitchett I find a monotony of expres-
sion and a persistence in repetition which are the
best of all answers to the groundless and impudent
eharge of Macaulayism. The word “historic” I
counted six times—“historic palace,” “historic
ridge,” “historic name” (twice), and “historic
interest ” (twice). The adjective “ grey ” is only
used four times—“grey city,” “ grey parapets,”
“grey walls,” “grey lanes.” The word “his-
tory” occurs five times; “imagination,” three
times; “blood,” four times (it’s a wonder there
wasn’t more blood than that); “classic,” three
times; “battle-scarred,” twice; “gallant dead.”
twice; “heroic dead,” five times; “heroic
scenes,” twice. “Shot-pitted Cashmere gate ”he
uses twice. And all this in five small pages. In
the same number Fitchett’s name, exclusive of
advertisements proper, is mentioned twelve .times,
and a photograph of the man is likewise given.
Tough as a piece of working-bullock and
hard as a drawn wire nail, old Frank Hann,
explorer and ex-squatter, has again returned to
civilisation, after a big dive into the Never-Never,
between Weld Springs (W.A.) and the South
Australian border. Hann was a North Queens-
land pioneer when Prime Minister Deakin was a
schoolboy, and his tracks from east to west and
from north to south of the continent would re-
semble the course of a spider returning from a
heavy evening out. It is mostly the tracks of
explorers who were fitted out with every comfort,
and carefully protected against personal loss, that
occupy the best places on the maps of Australia,
so Hann’s efforts to build up the geography of
the country on his own have not been generally
published. He loves the wild, free life of the
explorer, and grieves that the area that wants
exploring is becoming circumscribed. Several
years ago he arrived in Perth for a spell,
accompanied by a retinue of black followers,
one of whom was an unbleached Venus dressed
in a fetching costume of. white calico with
Turkey-red trimmings, and no corset to speak of.
It took a long time to disperse the admiring
crowds that followed Hann’s footsteps down Hay-
street, and a lawsuit with his landlord followed as
a mere trivial detail. The veteran should now be
nearing the 70th mile-post, but is still going
strong. He is much annoyed because the Perth
papers insist on terming him a prospector ; and
darkly hints that he will write up his peregrina-
tions. With a good literary man behind him the
book should be fine reading.
“ One who knows ” writes :
Writer was behind the scenes when Charles Oliver ruled
the N.S.W. Lands Department, from 1880 to 1888, and
admired the capacity and clear-headedness of the man.
What commanded most respect was his unflinching de-
termination to cleanse the administration, whilst doing
liberal justice to individual officers whose positions were
abolished for economic reasons. It is on record that
some 200 clerks, draughtsmen, and others, were
dispensed with by him, at a saving to the country’of over
£70,000 per annum in salaries, allowances, rents, &e., and
the administration simplified and made more speedy. It
is also on record that, during 1885-6, the enormous
number of 1,500 pastoral runs were divided by him
under the provisions of the 1884 Act, with great skill,
tact, and discrimination, without giving rise to the
slightest breath of scandal. Had he so chosen,
nothing could have been easier than to have
acquired wealth, far in excess of the sums recently
sworn to before the Royal Commission as paid, sub rosa,
for improvement leases. Another act that redounds to
Bis honor seems to have been completely lost sight of—
the discovery of far-reaching swindles whereby the public
estate had been defrauded of about 50,000 acres of choice
conditionally-purchased lands (in respect of which certi-
ficates of title had issued, although the conditions of the
law had palpably not been complied with). The dismissal
of seven officers concerned, and the recovery of all the
land to the State, followed. Yet this is the man who is
now to be turned adrift, as a reward for honesty, by the
Ministry of “Reform”!
Some recent aspects of a posturing parson :
Parson Worrall, who succeeded in making Tommy
Bent, Tommy’s Ministry, and most of Tommy’s Parlia-
ment look ridiculous, has been engaged ever since in
doing the same thing for himself, and again he has suc-
ceeded. Worrall has been posing as if he were a torn
martyr just released after great suffering. Exhaustion,
nervous break-down, nervous break-up, debility, and
all sorts of things have suddenly afflicted Worrall
since he faced the Bar of the House. “I think
a sea voyage would do me good,” he wailed to an
interviewer. Then he telegraphed to his wife, saying
that he was leaving for Fiji in a few days. “A talk in
Fijian would do me good,” he explained. Here folks
remembered that when Worrall was crusading against
Bent and posing as the about-to-be-martyred-one he had
told his audience that he had many a time, in far-off Fiji,
among savages and other things, faced greater dangers
than anything Bent could fetch along, and he had not
been afraid. They got curious about those dangers. Some
infidel who said he had lived in Fiji for about 30 years,
wrote to the papers, and said that the Rev. Worrall had
talked through his hat. The Fijians were quite harmless,
and the missionaries, instead of facing danger, faced good
meals and comfortable beds, and grew fat and lazy.
Then Worrall fell over himself to write and say that he
had been misreported. Certainly he had never faced
danger from the natives, of whom 90 per cent,
are good, mild Methodists; he meant that the
missionaries sometimes met hurricanes, and influ-
enza, and typhoid, and tilings like that, which, of
course, are not peculiar to Fiji. Unfortunately, the
same day one of Worrall’s greatest supporters dipped
his pen in gore and wrote on behalf of the parson. This
man said he was with Worrall in Fiji. The natives were
fierce and wild. Worrall had several times been in grave
personal danger. There had been awful murders, and
cannibalism had been practised in the islands when
Worrall was there. Worrall hadn’t been eaten, but he
might have been. There were tons of danger. The two
letters were printed together, and Worrall, who had been
a joke, became something of a public shriek.

Two Gippsland (Yic.) pioneers, dating
from the early 50’s, passed out, the other day,
within 24 hours of each other. John Carter
assisted in the surveying of a large portion of
South Gippsland. Captain Thomas Leggatt,
coastal navigator, is remembered for his adven-
turous voyage from Melbourne to Port Albert in
a coracle similar to those used by the early
Britons.

JAS.WARD, Brickfield Hill, sydney.
Devonport, a suburb of Auckland (M.L.),
recently decided that it would have a Ss.ddon
memorial, and, after much discussion, proposed
that it should take the form of a technical school,
to be maintained out of the rates. The proposal
was greeted with wild applause. A “ painful
impression ” was created by a bowelless councillor
who rose to point out that the borough already
bad an overdraft of £4500; he thought the best
memorial to the late Premier would be a special
rate, to be called the “Seddon Rate,” the money
to be devoted to paying off the indebtedness of
the municipality. He said that the fact that the
borough had been taken out of pawn in his honor
ivould be a good way to keep the dead statesman’s
memory green. But. as said before, the only
result was to create a “painful impression.”
What a number of candidates have an-
nounced themselves for the coining Australian
elections as Freetraders who believe in moderate
Protection ‘‘owing to the circumstances in which
the country is placed ' ”
Bigjohn Forrest is badly out of place in a
Ministry that pushes forward any sort of demo-
cratic legislation. John has already blurted out
the old Tory yell that a land-tax is robbery, and
in the Cabinet, whenever he is anything but a
mere excrescence, he is a drag. At the last Aus-
tralian elections, John was allowed a walk-over,
and spent his time in stumping the country
against Labor and Liberalism. This year he is
to be opposed by a Labor candidate, who will
probably be beaten, but his appearance on the
scene will help to keep the Bulky One busy in his
own constituency.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 14, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Judkins wail against horse-racing and
gambles in general is having effect. The Exes,
led off by absenting themselves from Caulfield for
both big meetings. The Talbottle himself
wandered out last Saturday, but he doesn’t
count any. His lady is the Governor, and
she got up a rival show for the first Caul-
field meeting, giving a mild musical party at
Stonington that afternoon. For the second" she
attended an art show. Dame Northcote spent
the two race afternoons opening cat exhibitions
and’listening to Madame Arral trill in glad song.
So it looks as if the Vice-regal dames are leagued
with the lugubrious Judkins.
Caulfield for once failed to realise a sensation in
clothes this August season. Fashionables, whose
interest in race excitements seems on the wane,
turned out to both meetings in winter togs, and
looked anyhow. One honeymoon pair—Mr. and
Mrs. Charlie Lempriere—returned to publicity in
time for the second meeting, he carrying the
bride’s cloak all day—a touching sample of devo-
tion and servitude.
There was something of a sensation about the
last Marshall Hall concert, in the appearance of
Madame Blanche Arral, a -petite star from the
Paris Opera Comique. No preliminary puffs had
tickled the curiosity of the public, but a few in
the know had circulated word that Madame would
be a Treat(with a capital T), so the concert proved
the biggest of the matinee season; And Madame
was a Treat. Small, shapely, chic, she captivated
the audience with chiffons of charming black, with
large roses in green, mauve and pink as a bold
relief. The dense-black coiffure was dressed in the
Frenchiest fashion. There were only two numbers
down for her. The first was from “ Carmen.”
It poured in sweet liquid fashion—real tears in
its tragic wake. Applause was violent. The
advent of a singer who had smiles and tears in her
fresh, flexible voice, and the magnetism of health
and temperament, was expressed heartily. The
second number was a bigger hit. The “ Polacca
de Mignon ” sparkled with crisp brilliancies.
Five recalls (causing a repetition of the last merry
thistle-down passages) did not nearly express the
feelings of the crowd.
Inquiry as to Madame Arral’s past—musical
and operatic past, that is—led to interesting dis-
closures. The songster starred for five years in
the biggest theatres in Paris. She has been
decorated by the Czar and other despots and bad
characters, and has a number of odd trophies of
star nights in all parts of the world of song. She
owns, with Calve and Melba, the novelty of a
Masonic medal, which vouches for the fact
that Madame has been initiated into three
degrees of the goatish brotherhood. Also,
the artiste is a cat-fancier, a chiffon-col-
lector, and owns a Yankee husband. Her
health is a matter of joy—or her joy is a
matter of health. On Saturday night, when she
illuminates jewels of song with dress and gem
and gesture, she promises to be at her best
towards the finish. “I am all de melody—all
joy—all esprit after many numbers —not tired,
never.” And Madame gives an ecstatic glance
to the ceiling, which takes Providence into her
gratitude.
The typist now takes turns at matrimonial
scoring with the footlighter. One of her has
captured the chief of a big merchant's office. An
engagement is announced. The news is a shock.
No heart affection was suspected, none of the
usual moth-and-the-flame circlings noted. The
clerks conclude that all the preliminaries and
most of the philandermgs must have been
negotiated on the machine.
The Gymkhana, at the Glaoiarium—commonly
pronounced the Glassy Harem—was an assorted
joy. The crowd struggled for a view—struggled
till it stuck fast. It all meant considerable help
to the Blind Association. On the ice, the frolics
were run by the cream. The biggest hit of a
mixed bill was Miss Nancy Grice driving a team
of man. It was the boldest achievement on the
course. Miss Grice hunts the fox. This night
she handled the reins of her two-legged team with
firmness, and had the lust of sport in her eye as
she sped round the Glacial expanse at a rate that
suggested a motor run amok. Judge Molesworth
ran a snowy four-in-hand. Miss Lewers was
whip for a blue and white team which included
Miss Lesly Madden. Prof. Brewer, doing his
lightning glide with Miss Marsden, looked like a
beautiful blackbird swooping through space with
a small white dove. Prof. Chambers carved
images and flowers on the top crust of the ice, and
otherwise acquitted himself expertly.
The glad tidings of a wandering Victorienne’s
engagement is to hand—Miss Elaine Molesworth
(eldest daughter of the Judge who wears a jovial
boxer perpetually atilt) to Lieut.-Colonel Walker,
an officer stationed in the Punjab. The Miss in
question has the sporting look of her dad, and is
a comely young person, with a half-defiant, half-
humorous smile. She was never without it—no
matter how depressing the circumstances or
solemn the spree, I often wondered if she put it
on with her hat of mornings, or slept in it. It
was pleasing. The sex doesn’t smile enough; but
that’s the fault of bad teeth. The charmer only
is happy who goes through life with her own
natural pearls, and can laugh to full range without
a qualm. The unfortunate with borrowed molars
and blank intervals in the department of speech
hasn’t laughed for years. The human laugh in
its perfect form involves a bright, happy show of
original pearls. No family should be without it.
The Stonington Dame turned up at the news-
boys’ hop the other night, and garbed herself
splendidly for the crowded occasion. Her chiffons
ran to lilac mauve and deep orchid tones, with
any amount of spangle-and-diamond illumination.
Himself was there also, wearing his usual clothes
and a strong expression of disapproval. Aide
Crichton looked a woefully inefficient caddy. His
pilotage of the tall Exes, suggests a sprig of very
curly playful mignonette guarding two proud and
lofty poplars.
A pithy scribe shed this happy thought, the
other day: “We have come to a time when
everyone who does not happen to be a gentleman
is a lady, except the new woman, who, while she
has ceased to be a lady, has not yet become a
gentleman.” The Block creates the lady-man and
the man-lady. There are dozens of Johnnies
abroad these days, “ perfumed like a milliner” and
haberdashed like a Christmas-tree doll. While at
the Bridge tables, silent women play with male
finesse and earnestness. They whiff at a cigarette
now and then, and quaff a whisky-and-soda a little
more often. They’ll sit through half the night
playing, will whistle at their losses and slap their
legs buoyantly when fortune smiles. But even
the best she Bridge-player fails something, as a rule,
in being the perfect gentleman in manner, taste,
temper, and horse-sense.
The fashionables who have time and taste for
the yelper and the champion scratcher in the
domestic pet line have been going crazy over the
annual show of the Kennel Club. The display of
dog, cat and parrot started on Friday. Sixty-
eight cats were competing ; but there were others.
The marvellously groomed and massaged terriers
had satin cushions and point lace hankies, and
just a stylish touch of ennui. The names of the
hounds and bull-pups were of the most impressive
order. The spinsters and bairnless mothers who
exhibited pets had gone to extensive trouble to
find astounding designations for brutes that
couldn’t argue the point.
Miss Margaret Baskerville has some fine
bronzes, clay studies, and water colors on view as
a result of her two years’ study in sculping and
palette centres abroad. Rodin’s school of art is
responsible for one of the most remarkable clay
achievements—the figure of a lithe, muscular,
graceful athlete. The poise of the figure, the
taut muscles of one leg, the ease of the other, the
development of the chest, the fine, strong head,
and the general expression of hardy, manly youth-
fulness all render the work rare and beautiful.
“ Boy on a Dolphin,” a clay relief, is an admirably
conveyed fancy. “ June ”is a poetic bit of clay.
Three remarkable bronzes call attention to Miss
Baskerville’s almost masculine art. There is a
strength in securing the bold outline of limb or
head, and a dainty grace of expression pervades
her work. Miss Baskerville’s travels and study
far afield have worked wonders.
Miss Kitty Murphy, of Ballarat (sister of Cogh-
lan’s brewery), changed her name last Wednes-
day at the instance of a Mr. Tarvey. The
bride is one of a family that, some years ago,
became indebted to Tatt.’s Sweep. In the first
place, an aunt won a first prize—some £lO,OOO or
so, and this wealth descended, at aunt’s demise,
to Miss Kitty and her sisters. Subsequently Pa
and Ma Murphy won a prize running into four
figures. The story goes that the girls likewise
had a flutter in Tatt.’s which turned out almost as
successful at the parental speculation. It seemed
as though Tatt. had been designed by Providence
for the comfort of the family.
Talking of Murphys, Dr. “ J.” of that name
and of Kew, is due at one of the Melbourne altars
this week. He marries Miss Ethel Millane, one
of the prettiest nurses who ever sported cap and
apron at St. Vincent’s Hospital, where good looks
are common to the uniformed staff.
The Glaciarium received no permanent injuries
from Highlander Guy Madden’s recent fall. The
stout youth merely bruised great areas of himself.
Even the skates were not injured, so the Glaci-
arium management thinks itself fortunate.
Mrs. Frank Cave, the breezy and cultured
dame of the Spanish Consul, stands sponsor to a
scheme to swell the funds of the Foundling
Hospital by means of a theatrical matinee of
child performers, coached and put through
their paces by Miss J. Brenan. The show is
fixed for the second Saturday in September.
A stylish pair went right away down to the
old-fashioned part of the town, the other day, to
get married. It was Squatter Lang’s lawyer son,
with Miss Wilga Umphelby for accomplice. The
old St. James’ Cathedral was the locale, A spree
at Menzies’ followed on a small, subdued scale.
The Victorian Artists are crowing this year.
Vice-regal exhibits helped the show a lot, for the
smart sheep of Toorak flocked along, and in a
spurt of excitement started to purchase. The
Exes, themselves have been buying pet canvases,
and the Art Society is joyful. The attendance
has been the best for years, and the sales abso-
lutely a record.
The Talbottle dame has facile French. She
delighted the Alliance Franchise by pattering
sympathetically at the recent soiree. The tall
Ex. kept her lorgnette completely out of the con-
versation, so she must have been quite pleased.
It’s only when the grey dame is ruffled or bored
that she employs the refrigerating glasses.
No wonder there are not enough Mary Anns—
or nearly enough—to go round the State families,
when £250 is being offered at Prahran in prizes
for the yodeller and the elocutionary aspirant.
The result of the competing spirit in youngsters
is to make them clean crazy to be at Gray’s
“ Elegy ” and Tosti’s “ Good-bye ” from the por-
ridge hour till bed-time. And cheap teachers (?)
go round, offering lessons in appropriate gesture
and canary scales for a church coin, or even for a
grilled chop. Every suburb has its abiding pro-
fessor or professoress—capable or otherwise—and
its casual instructors. “ Nelly Bly ” taught while
you wait, and Chopin’s “ Appassionata,” or any
other concert gem, is to be acquired on the time-
payment cottage piano in two lessons at one-and-
sixpence apiece. Then a sum like £250 is promised
in prizes, and every chick in the State aspires in
song and perspires in the gyrations of elocution to
grab a slice of it. Why can’t some good com-
mittee of citizens offer a fat sum in trophies for
the largest family, and set the competition spirit
aflame in that useful direction ?
A parson has made the boldest proposal up to
date. The victim was an advanced spinster in
mourning, and in a letter of sympathy which the
shepherd addressed to her, he hinted at the loneli-
ness of her life, and spoke of her as one s
young, so fair, so dowered.” When she replied)
“Not at all,” or something just as casual, he wrote
frankly, dropping all metaphor and timidity. It
wasn’t a sentimental appeal. It was based on a
solid metal foundation, and wound up piously:
“ Think of the good your great fortune would do
in my poor parish.” Then the spinster gnashed
her teeth hard and wished to have him by the
scruff of the neck. When she had cooled down
she announced her weariness of “ Kerristianity
and her determination to try a little Paganism as
a change. The shepherd hastily withdrew, and
now counts up the loss of her patronage.
Talking of parsons. In one very beautiful
Suburb where the proverbial wattle trembles on
the river-bank, and the hills, after leaning to re-
fresh in the playful lagoon, undulate towards
“that inverted bowl the sky,” a parson-widower,
who hath his quiverful, is searching hopefully for
a second mate. The progeny number close on a
dozen, and every Sabbath they form a pathetic
brood at the kirk of their father. The sight
should appeal to the motherly and the lone
spinster. But it doesn’t. The eligible Misses of
the parish hold off. In vain he makes his after-
noon calls, and his tender remarks anent the state
solitary. His family haunts his steps. It hovers
over his chair in the parish drawing-rooms, and
comes up between his ingratiating smile and the
affections of the eligible Miss.
A returned gadder of keen observation gives an
amusing account of the present little batch of
Australians located in Ed. Vll.’s capital. He
says the struggle of the ’Roolander to toot-toot
herself right into the vortex is tremendous. And
there’s always a gay countess or marchioness or
two ready to sink all class differences in the interest
of, say, sport, and to fraternise some with the
mere Australian ; also there’s always a poor
wretch of a Vere de Vere only too glad to be
financed and feted by a sheep-squanderer. But
the visitor to the Hub of the World soon finds
that she is only hurt by such aristocratic patronage,
and through association with the frivolous countess
the steady-going, righteous viscountess is lost to
one’s sight and ken for ever. Also, he noted,
there is a disposition on the part of the most staid
gadder to look frisky and fit. The picture of one
notable Victorienne senior endeavoring to play
the giddy young lamb among the swell Londoners
was funnier than the thought of Bent having a
deportment lesson.
Marshall Hall books his passage for Britain.
He takes six months’ vacation, and leaves his
baton at home—but in his bag will be a few
original opera-scores and a set of golf-cleeks.
Mrs. Potts has been stopped in her shining
hours at Menzies’ by illness. A recent buggy
accident has misplaced her confidence, or some-
thing just as important, and she retires from
view for awhile to get re-adjusted.
The surprise of “ The Little Michus ” opening
night was the ad vance made by Miss Alma Bar-
ber. Her piquancy and pretty stage coquetries
took on at once.
On appearing in the partially undressed circle
on the last Waldron co. night, Miss Florence
Young was suddenly cheered by the gallery
throng. Florence lost her stolid air of self-assur-
ance, and didn’t know whether the cheers were
meant for her. She took several hurried glances
round, saw herself the cynosure of all eyes, and at
last bobbed her head towards the gods. Florence
was actually blushing, and the crowd, rather
pleased with her overwhelmed flush, cheered some
more.
Toorak’s fashionable wanderers are hurrying
on with their race-chiffons for the Randwick
carnival. Already Madame Masseran, the French
moulder of the form fashionable, has steamed off
to whalebone the stylish for September, and the
race-goer gets equipped with a hustle to follow on.
Mrs. Burton Stewart takes measures to draw
attention to her willowy and floating presence.
She sets up a big blaze of gem illumination, and
has been relieving the grey term of grass-widow-
hood by a succession of smart tiffin parties and
dinner sprees at Menzies’.
The Holy City gets its hair in crimp for the
marriage of Miss Shylie Blue and Mr. Herbert
Rymill, in the second week of September. One
trump card the bride-elect has in the attendance
of Governor Le Hunte’s only chick as bridesmaid.
Even in the Apple Isle the Kitchen Lancers are
in vogue, and the casualty-list of the polka-halls
is great. A correspondent sends word that at a
recent dance a Lancer was dashed against the wall
and suffered severe concussion of the brain. This is,
indeed, taking one’s pleasures violently. In this
Yarraside capital many a broken limb and mis-
placed ear are relics of a jaunty Kitchen Lancers.
Years ago, when the buoyant Hopetoun Ex. started
swinging a loose and flying limb in the Quadrille
d ’ honneur, fashionables took to the rollicking
kick-up, and let go in what became known as the
Kitchen, or Rowdy Lancers. The suburbanites
followed suit with a kind of Rugby football game.
The list of partially-killed and wounded has been
active ever since. The death of J. F. M’Carron,
at the Town Hall after a Lancers scrap one
season, scared dancers a little, but soon it was for-
gotten and the tornado sets were resumed. There-
fore, medical men haunt the ballrooms. They
know how profitable the Kitchen Lancers are.
The Talbottles alone look coldly on such scrim-
mages. They execute their duty hop with the
gravity of a jury returning a verdict of “ Guilty ”
without any recommendation to mercy.
Miss Bess Norris, the miniaturist who went
abroad from this State with her microscopic
brushes and capacity for taking pictures and
pains, is doing well in Europe. At last advices
she was stationed in Fogtown brushing in the
features of Mrs. Campbell-Praed on a fragment of
ivory. No talk yet of a return to these golden
wattle-haunts, which is best proof of success. In-
fidelity in an art aspirant to this Paradise argues
prosperity abroad.
My Adelaide correspondent writes :
The Girls’ Realm, that polite periodical for the damsels
of England, contains, in its July issue, a photograph and
short biographical account of Maude Puddy, Mus. Bac.,
the Adelaide girl who recently went to the Kaiser’s country
to study. Under the heading “What Girls are Doing,”
the magazine recounts each month the deeds of any girl
who has developed the genius habit very badly, and Miss
Puddy’s inclusion is regarded as rather an advertisement
for Tortoise Town.
Adelaide ’Varsity broke forth into some wild proceed-
ings this year in honor of the visiting lacrosse teams.
The Sports’ Association, besides various dinners and other
foods and mild excitements, gave a ball to all the girls of
its acquaintance. The usual and ordinary University
hop eventuated some months ago, but this affair was far
more youthful and joyous, and was an invitation instead
of a subscription proceeding.
Re par (B. 26 7,’06) about a girleen who was turned out
of a public place of amusement. Tortoise Town once
knew something of the same kind. The demoiselle in
question belonged to society, and the only charge against
her was her exuberant mirth and her unbridled shouts of
joy. She took life gladly with both hands, and, when she
took it, the rest of society couldn’t hear itself think. She
was even charged with having held conversation from a
theatre box with friends in the stalls and aimed sweets
at them and hit the wrong people. The management had
so many complaints that it was obliged to ask her to
withdraw her patronage and keep it withdrawn. How
long the ban lasted I don’t know, but the matter was
squared up somehow ; and now she goes where she likes,
and is a very quiet girl.
The small economies of a certain vice-regal dame are
furnishing food for feline conversation in a city slower than
this. The small offspring of said dame was learning to
trip the light fantastic prop, together with certain other
offspring of the very select, and it was arranged that the
kiddies should have a very select and private spree, and
dance a gavotte, or minuet, or something, before
their proud mammas. They were to wear simple,
pin-spotted, home-made white muslin frocks, and scarlet
shoes and stockings ; but, just when everything was
arranged, the vice-regal lady baulked at this wild extra-
vagance. She said her daughter had bronze shoes and
stockings, and she was sure they would look quite nice
enough, even though the kiddies were to wear red choux
on their frocks, and it wasn’t worth buying new ones, any-
way. And so that spree fell through. The select mammas
couldn’t possibly allow their offspring to dance in a
gavotte that wasn’t all wearing scarlet legs, even when
the brown-legged exception was a vice-regal one.
A joining event which might have caused some excite-
ment in the Holy City had that place of steeples not
neglected its opportunities, was that of Miss Hilda
Kyffin Thomas and Mr. Leonard Hack. Bride is a daughter
of Register proprietor Kyffin Thomas, and groom a
brother of Guli Hack, who has taught much of the young
S’Australian voice to trill and warble. Both families are
somewhat extra, but did not publish the fact by a loud
and splashy wedding. The Voice Breathed to relatives
only. m

TRYPHENA.
The popular councillor, Johnny Hines,
who died in Adelaide on Aug. 8, was for very
many years Parliamentary caterer, and in the
good oid times of champagne picnics he made lots
of money out of legislative feedings. He retired
not long ago, and gave his talents to municipal
affairs. Death came from blood poisoning, as a
result of cutting his corns.
Professor Henderson, lecturing on Tenny-
son, at Adelaide University, told his audience a
story which the old bard was fond of. Said
George the Fourth to a friend in Portsmouth :
“They tell me you are the biggest scoundrel in
this town”; and the friend replied, mildly, "I
hope your Highness is not come to take away my
character.” The professor was not game to give
his audience the other story which Tennyson used
to bracket with this as the neatest repartee on
record. Says the King of France to a courtier:
“They tell me you and I are much alike. Was
your mother much about the court?” And the
courtier answers in a matter-of-fact, information-
bureau style : “No, sire. My father often was,
but my mother not at all.”
The case of Charles Thornton, of Fitzroy
(Yic.) and Inglewood, and, at present, of Pentridge
He got nine months in ’B4 for forgery ; three
months in ’B7 for larceny ; 12 months in ’B9 for
breaking into a store ; then three months, one
month, and three months ; then, in ’9l, four years
and two floggings for robbery in company ; then
several more sentences. The other day he got
another ' three months for larceny and was
committed for trial on a charge of burglary and
another of larceny. What with his frequent trials
and his expensive maintenance while in gaol, the
State would have saved much money and worry if
he had been in gaol from the start, especially
if he had been kept at profitable employment.
On the other hand, there isn’t any evidence that
anybody was available in ’B4 capable of saying for
certain that Thornton was a person who would
cost hundreds of pounds in trials if he wasn’t
locked up for life. One can always tell at the end
of his life the man who ought to have been put
inside and kept there ; the trouble is to tell it at
the start. And that is the Thornton problem.
“ O’F.” : Retirement of Melbourne police-
magistrate Panton reminds me. Is there no re
tiring age for J.P.’s? It is the custom at Mel-
bourne City Court for the P.M. to stay away on a
Saturday morning and leave the serving-out of
justice to the senior J.P. present and his
colleagues. I don’t know the ages of these gentle-
men, but the 75 year-old Panton looks a boy by
comparison with most of them. As for their
judicial wisdom, I have a profound disrespect for
many of Panton’s decisions, but if ever I |am
threatened with arrest in Melbourne I will try
hard to avoid appearing in the City Court on a
Saturday.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 14, 1906.
Mr Dear Arini,—
The busy bee, the strenuous ant, and the hard-
working beaver—these pretty creatures are mere
drowsers in the sun compared with Miss Rawson,
who is now head of State Government House.
Her labors are of the dreariest description—
District Nursing Societies with heavy personages
prowling around in the gloaming of barn-like draw-
ing-rooms ; small bazaars with large clergymen,
bran-pies, cakesand aprons around ; and receptions
given by useful society women to Girls Friendly
delegates from the Great Soak. (Didn’t eleven
inches of rain fall in as many minutes on London
recently ?) These, and scores of the like jamborees,
make a large drab lane through the conscientious
girl’s life. Her attendance at Professor Wood-
house’s six lectures on Ancient Greek Scripture
made a luminous break in the drabness. That
section of society, whose knowledge of early Greece
began and ended with the picture, “A Reading
from Homer,” now knows its way pretty well
about the track that Prof. Woodhouse has blazed
for it. It feels as if it had personally rung the
bell at the front door of Pericles and e 'Peed the
sign in Alcibiades’ front garden, “ Beu .ro of the
Ancient Greek Dog.”
Time we ceased to hear the story about the
innocent and unsuspecting young wife (sometimes
she’s the unsophisticated young daughter) who,
after luncheon, is lured on to Bridge by her very
sophisticated hostess, and is told, when about to
leave, that she owes her entertainer £2O. Inno-
cent then goes home and in large-eyed horror tells
her husband (or male progenitor) of the sharp
practice. That stalwart citizen then pays the
gambling debt by cheque, with 7s. Gd. added in
payment of the lunch. This pawed-about anec-
dote was told a Sabbath or so ago by the Rev.
Ronald Maclntyre in a sermon given in his
fashionable church (where a fashionable gynaecolo-
gist takes around the plate), and the scene was
laid in a local frilly suburb. The exhausted yarn
has been told in every capital in Europe, and
nearly every smart Bridge-playing hostess
in London and New York has had it
related as happening in her house. It was
probably none too new in prehistoric times.
Matildadon, gambling with the teeth of a diplo-
docus (instead of cards) in the cave of another
lady, lost to her more fish currency than she could
pay, and, going home, wept to Billosaurus,
saving she didn’t know the teeth were loaded.
Billosaurus either haughtily sent round a
whale in full settlement, or he made a widow of
the lady to whom his Matildadon had lost. Rev.
Maclntyre needn’t burst his halo—-all Woollahra
and all the Points know whether the game is being
played for fun, or money, or out of pure cussed-
ness.
The report that the governess in a smart family
has eloped with a doctor is being eagerly pursued
all over Sydney. The identity of the family from
which the governess fled is fixed, but pursuers
haven’t yet caught up with the male flying-fish.
It is very rarely a medical man is caught. It is
more unusual still for a governess to lash out to
the length of an elopement.
The Spencer Bruntons bring a lot of people
together on tbe night of next washing day to
chase the. glowing hours with flying feet. (This
is a laborious way of saying there will be a dance
on Monday.) The Bruntons do a lot of enter-
taining on hoard their newly-acquired yacht, the
Gulnare. Now, which of Byron’s heroines was
Gulnare? Can’t place her from memory, but she
was, no doubt, something languorous, soft and
dark-eyed. And she nestled. All Byron’s
heroines nestled. And no doubt Brunton’s yacht
floats as gracefully as its name-giver.
Miss Mildred Lamb, who won the croquet tour-
nament at Rose Bay Golf Club, may lay the
unction to her complexion that, according to a
London specialist, she won’t injure her figure or
hand by being a champion at the game. The
next least harmless sport is said to be lawn tennis,
but it enlarges the right shoulder and hand.
Even much motoring coarsens the skin, and
reddens the nose.
Miss Lute Mackenzie (name of a pipe ! what
sort of a name for a girl is Lute ?), it seems, did
not go off unshepherded on her stage career.
Grant Stewart, of the Collier Co., is her cousin,
and she is to have a small part in the play of
which he is part writer.
When Lucas Tooth baroneted, and thereby rose
to the social grade of Cholmondeley de Battleaxe
and similar old families, quite an outburst of
paragraphs about him appeared in the Bullopolis
press. Those pars widely exploited the fact that
he is chairman of the' London branch of the
BankofN.S.W. Nary a reference was there to
the source of his income—the Australian “long
sleever ” with the froth on the top of it, and a
great deal of it for 6d. if you take a large vessel
into the jug entrance.
A. Waverley girl who went on a visit to Maori-
land will probably pluck her orange blossoms
there. The runholder (we don’t call them
squatters) who wants her to share his name and
sheep has written to the damsel’s father, and put
the case with an eloquence that Demosthenes
might envy. Said runholder is a N.S. Welshman
settled in M.L.
Another sprig of orange blossom: Miss Ethel
Rouse, of Mudgee, engaged to Mr. Wentworth
Hill, whose station is at Hay. Both parties’ bank
balances are so good that they can afford to con-
sider the lilies, and needn’t take much thought
for the morrow.
A London correspondent tells me that after
Amy Castles had warbled before the King and
<jueen, at the invitation of Ladv de Grey, the
Queen asked her “Why We had not heard her at
Covent Garden?” Amy, amongst other songs,
gave the sceptred pair “Wind in the Trees,”
“ ’Tis June ” and “ The Dove.”
Miss Beatrice Holloway’s portrait, nearly life-
size, is in Nicholson’s window, and is the cutest
thing in posing—specially the posing of the left
arm, with its curving of wrist and hand, said
hand holding a stem of white lilies.
Talking of young artistes: Miss Bee Usher, the
Sydney girl who is with “ The Squaw Man ” Co.,
in Melbourne, is understudying the star-woman,
Miss Ola Humphreys.
Mr. T. E. Donne, Chief of the Maoriland
Tourist Bureau—the Department for the Pro-
tection of Moss on the Wanganui River and for
the Prevention of the Battening down of Geysers—
arrives in Sydney, on Thursday, per Wimraera.
The newly-established Sydney Agency (to guide
the tourist over the moss and geyser land) has the
biggest brass plates in George-street. And, when
night falls, out rushes the legend “New Zealand
Exhibition,” done in electric fire over the
building. This design bobs in and out all
the evening, which shows the artfulness of the
management. If it were simply a plain glare you
might pass by, only remarking to yourself that it
is a well-lighted street; but when you turn the
corner into the glare, and next moment it is
blotted out, you naturally look up to see who
biotted it, and then the great electric inscription
flares out again, and you get curious, and ask a
policemen where Maoriland is, and he says he
doesn’t know. Sydney costumieres tell me that a
whole swarm of Maorilanders are over here enjoy-
ing the blessed weather we have been having. I
don’t wonder at it. If I could, I would enclose
you a bottleful of our weather for your admiration.
Leading lady and leading man of a certain
leading stock company in Sydney refused dona-
tions to the Brough memorial “on principle.”
“Brough,” they said, “never encouraged an
Australian artist.” Wouldn’t employ one at all,
unless an amateur of “good family,” who could
come on for little or nothing in the way of coin.
Well, what about Pattie Browne and Hilda Spong
to cite two whose names crop up at first time of
thinking ?
Miss Tittell Brune’s girl admirers are of a dis-
tinctly specialised class, and it would be worth
something to get at the intellectual significance of
their admiration. From certain millinery ateliers
here, certain madames tell me, their youthful
apprentices go in a body to see their idol in that
“ How-now-baggage ’’ piece, “Dorothy Vernon.”
They go at six o’clock (if they quit work earlier they
go earlier) to the theatre, taking their “ tea” with
them, and cheerfully wait through two blank
hours. Not since the now Her Majesty’s opening
night until Miss Brune’s appearance in the “I-fegs-
by-my-halidom ” play has the management had
to put up the “ House Full” notice at seven o’clock.
Actresses like Maud Jeffries have their admiring
following of the Lambswool clans. Nance O’Neil
appealed to eclectics, whoadmired without desiring
to know her personally. Gilded Johnnydom or
lacquered*Billydom crowds the stalls in honor of
the high-class leg and opulent bust of the show
girls of the chorus. But the Tittell Brune fever
is a special microbe —something akin to the wor-
ship of Baal and Ashtaroth.
The woman-writer in a morning daily heaves a
pretty pebble at the large body of advisers who
wish to see the superfluous female on the land
keeping bees. If Hamlet of to-day read that
paper he would say to Ophelia, “Go, get thee to
an apiary.” “If all the women told to keep bees
took the advice there wouldn’t be enough bees to
go round—it would be one woman one bee. Three
acres and a bee would be the ideal sought for by a
political orator for his constituents.” Any woman
who has kept bees and poultry on a small scale,
and merely as a hobby, will be dead agin keeping
them as a means of livelihood. Unless, of course,
she be an exceptional manager. I went in for
bees and turkeys once, but I didn’t own them—
they owned me. Twelve turklings were hatched
—never counted the bees —and they all died in
early turkhood, save one. This one was brought
up, so far as its morning's went, in the pocket of
my dressing-gown. No one could resist the
appealing voice of a young turkey. It has the
sweetest speaking voice of all winged creatures.
When I went a-cycling, the turkling would ride
with me also, nestling in a pocket slung between
the handle-bars, and chirruping like an angel.
She grew to be an elegant fowl, and then left me
for the harem of a base and designing gobbler
next door. The bees, too—they swarmed into
alien gardens, and those that remained stopped
indoors and kept up disgraceful orgies eating my
honey. When Igo on the land it will be to grow
things with roots and tops. In conclusion, better
to keep he’s than bees—one he, at any rate, with
the help of a proper marriage certificate.
A very drab world it seems after coming out
from a nearly two-hour draught of Dolores’
singing. Other singers have given us more thrill,
but none has held us in greater thrall. A more
unsensational star-voice Sydney has not heard,
yet there wasn’t a vacant seat in the Town Hall
at the singer’s recitals. Expert musicians differ
as to whether her voice has gone off or come on.
If it has done either the variation is not pro-
nounced enough for the ear of the amateur. But
all the experts agree about the “ marvellous exe-
cution,” the “wonderful breathing” of Dolores.
No plain, every-day breather can imagine, without
going to Dolores’ concerts, what real breathing is
like. The siren’s methods are at the highest
technical top-notch. All the singing-teachers
in the city and suburbs, and all their pupils
(it’s pretty accurate to make the wholesale
assertion), have come to the concerts, and the
majority of them say each concert is a Lesson (the
word is to be spoken reverently). One Gentle
Jane, sitting six seats from the star, went to a
friend at the far end of the hall and offered to ex-
change seats for the remainder of the numbers,
“for,” said she, “I’m sure you can’t hear down
here.” But the friend declined exchange, for
fear the singer would be heard too acutely “ up
there.” This gives an idea of thequality of Dimples’
organ. Her voice, in its restrained sweetness
and purity, travels as easily and unbrokenly as a
ray of light—a moonbeam, for choice, when the
beam slides over ice.
One reason—men chiefly urge this—of Dolores’
hold over the audience is her obvious femininity
and personal charm. With her simply-parted
hair and her round, smiling face, her baby-creased
neck and chubby figure, she makes an appeal to
the masculinity of her hearers. It’s a quite usual
thing to hear the Buster-Cross kind of listener
remark “She doesn’t put on a thing, does she?
I don’t think she puts on enough.” Referring, of
course, to the soprano’s utter want of pose or
arrogance—her lack of “dog.”
Franz Wells, the pianist, looks like the
Wretched and Melancholy Dane. His hair of
burnt-straw tone is of the Paderewski mane type.
He is a pupil of Godowski—a quiet player with
strong singing fingers, and a wrist of malleable
iron. The depth to which his hands drop below
the keyboard when at work is a revelation to some
local learners. But then “all Godowski’s pupils
do that way.” The hands seem to be climbing up
to the notes, like a monkey going up to sing in
the tree-top.
A big muff of violets, lined with pale blue silk,
was one of the floralties handed up to Dolores at her
first concert. It was sent from Melbourne. Grace
Palotta sent violets. Another offering had three
white birds in a bed of flowers. . “Dimples ” has
less weight, physically, than on her last visit.
Her frocking runs to shimmering, sheeny materials
in tones of pale blue, pale pink and silver, or
white sequined things.
Mrs. Stokes Rees, the lady-o’-war, is the latest
addition to committee of St. Vincent’s Hospital
ball. This remains the most fashionable and
most cutely-organised of all the charity Whirls.
It will pirouette on Oct. 3. Fancy sets are being
arranged by the leaders of our Four Dozen (that’s
good for us ; New York only runs to Four Hun-
dred), and Mrs. Commandant Gordon, the W. P.
Manning lady, Mrs. Deery and Mrs. Boesen are
helping things along.
The latest about Cranbrook, the residence of the
State Governor, is that the lease is about to expire
and the owner has a chance to sell the property
to a rich Sydney merchant for £25,000. Premier
Carruthers wants to re-lease for the Governor,
but the owner insists upon selling. Carruthers
will not go higher than £20,000, so he was asked
the other day if he could arrange matters so that
the merchant who wants to buy might inspect the
property. He promptly declined—the Governor
isn’t to be harried by inspections—and it is further
stated that his Excellency Rawson isn’t going to
move into any new premises. If there is to be a
move he will make one removal of it, and move by
the first boat to England.
A girl who went to wave the au revoir hanky
at the Sierra yesterday tells me that Cuyler
Hastings boarded the vessel after the gangway
had been pulled. He did the monkey act by way
of the vessel’s ladder and then turned an “If-I-
were-King ” gaze on the city from the upper
deck. Miss Holly Coffee got away in a startling
red and blue checker-board plaid and a courier
bag slung over the checks. Also went pianist
Borschkeand his hair, or the hair and its Borsclilte,
according to the order in which you choose to
place them.
The Boys’ Brigade Ball was whirled last night
under the direction of Mayoress Taylor, Miss
Margaret Harris, Mrs. Matthew Harris, and
other ladies always in the forefront of the battle
for worthy charities.
The John Hugheses (he is the M.L.C. and Vice-
President of the Executive Council) returned by
Canada yesterday. Both assumed temporary
spinal curvature before King Edward and the
Pope.
Mrs. George Cruickshank (step-daughter of
Janet Mrs. Clarke) is blossoming’ as our smartest
widow. She gives another ball presently. Her
companion is a Melbourne girl, over whose grey
eyes Sydney raves. Eyes like those of which
poet Daley wrote in “A Sunset Fantasy.”
The Pah’f’l—that obsolete warship called by the
Lower Orders the Powerful—has been giving the
Brisbane girls a treat. Naval men, more than
other uniformed persons, seem to know the gentle
art of making girl friends. They flirt with such
adroitness. Perhaps their long pickling in sea-
brine makes them, generally speaking, proof
against the microbe of Mash. Officers’ cabins on
the Pah’f’l are always aflame with flowers, and
the gassing, giggling young parties who assemble
therefor tea when a reception is “on” always
demand the history of the many photographs. Of
course they get the accurate history. Sailors
never, never digress from the straight path of
accuracy, and their adhesion to fact beats the gum
on the back of any postage-stamp I have ever met.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
The latest enemy of time is the flat roof at
Messrs. David Jones and Co.’s premises (Sydney)
That toney firm recently added a couple of
storeys to its already tall building, and
established on the flat roof a sky garden
where, in the midst of the city, ‘one hears
nothing of its noise, feels none of its dust, and
sips tea or consumes strawberries and cream under
the blue dome of heaven surrounded by palms
ferns and pot shrubs. It recalls the hanging
Gardens of Babylon, but in one respect—its floor—
)avid Jones’s garden is a long way ahead of that
of the Queen of Babylon. This impervious floor
is the roof of the huge emporium beneath, and is
made of P. & B. concrete, and is of a nature quite
new to Australia, of materials supplied by the
Paraffine Paint Co.* J

plexions; or, what is infinitely worse, cover a rough, coarse
skin with injurious paint and powder—a subterfuge
apparent to all but the most vulgar 1 In times gone-b v
such foolish methods were excusable ; but science has
made such strides in recent years that ladies troubled
with wrinkles, pimples, freckles or any facial blemish
need no longer worry. They have now a most reliable
and dainty cure in Patti’s Beauty Cream, which has added
three years of the highest Australasian reputation to a
quarter of a century of the most distinguished European
appreciation as the only perfect skin food and complexion
beautifier. It is found on the toilet tables of every ladv
of refinement because it is absolutely pure, harmless
ana a perfectly natural and never-failing* remedy for all
complexion troubles, no matter how bad they may be It
instantly clears the skin of all pimples, rash, &0., caused
by injurious cone ictions and imitations against which
ladies are cautioned. All chemists and stores, price 3s. 6d
and 6s. per pot.*

a correspondent tells me, a tall, good-figured girl'
with a strong face, lighted by dark eyes and
crowned with dark hair. Her frock was a lovely
thing of white chiffon, sprayed with big pink
roses. Consensus of press opinion is that Miss
Ainsley has a fine voice of great promise.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Senate, for some foolish reason, con-
trived to get it laid down as a fixed principle that
the provisions of the Federal Lands Acquisition
Bill should not be used in order to acquire land
for a Federal capital. That is just the ust, above
all others, to which the measure should be put,
but the Senate was in a small-minded mood in
one of its parish pump moods, in fact.
When the Dalby-Cattle Creek railway was
before Bananaland Parliament two years ago,
Philp and co. advocated the betterment principle.
Premier Kidston afterwards proposed a better-
ment tax, and Philp and co. declared excitedly
that it was a land-tax in disguise. Now that
Kidston has given them a chance to support their
own proposal, they argue that the old Guarantee
Act is quite good enough. “Janny” Leahy lost
much breath in expressing his horror of a measure
in which he plainly saw the “claw of the ser-
pent.”
Ex-Democrat Jenkinson , M.L. A.—known
to all sections of Q. Assembly as “Uriah ’—sits
on the front Opposition bench beside Philp and
Leahy. His presence isn’t cherished by his imme-
diate neighbors, but Jenkinson doesn tmind that.
He wants the world to know that he is out for a
portfolio, and means to get one before anyone
else. Philp’s old henchmen —Macartney, For-
syth, and otliers—are content to fight the boodler’s
battle from obscure corners of _ Parliament; but
“Uriah” insists upon parading his political
apostasy and advertising his aims.
G. Findley, M.L. A.-— known as “ Jumbo ”
—is the first Morganite to secede from Kidston.
He left over the Betterment tax. Jumbo, who is
a land auctioneer, took his large, elephantine way
across the floor of the House, made a rambling,
incoherent “ explanation,” and subsided beside
that other notorious turncoat, “Dinny” Keogh.
He then gazed at the Premier, as if expecting the
immediate resignation of the Government. But
nothing happened. The business of Parliament
proceeded as if Jumbo had never been. The
renegade, by the way, is member for Wide Bay,
a constituency that was also responsible for'the
original political existence of such awful circum-
stances as Tpzer and Jenkinson.

A joint

Plunkett, M.L. A., wants Premier Kidston
to “arrange for a referendum to ascertain whether
the people of Queensland are, or are not, favor-
ably inclined towards seceding from the
wealth.” But why leave it at that? Why not
also find out if they are favorable towards seceding
from the British empire, and''the globe, and the
human race; likewise whether North Queensland
desires to secede from South Queensland ? And
what’s the matter with a referendum of Mrs.
Plunkett to find, out if she wants to secede from
Mr. Plunkett —not that .The Bulletin supposes
for a moment that she would, but there is no
harm in having a referendum.
“Mul”.: Has citizen J. Wren been in-
stalled as financier of the Labor party? “His
vote and his money are at the disposal of Labor,”
wrote an enthusiastic Senator, some time back.
It is currently reported that the law-breaker of
Collingw'ood has helped another Labor member
out of a heavy financial difficulty. I'(want to
know if the Labor party is going into-the fray
with that useless and unoruamental parasite, the
illegal tote-man, on its shoulders. Its association
with Wren would lose it more votes than all his
badly-got wealth could buy for it.
N.S.W. Assembly has been a dull place
these last few 7 days. Nobody has said or ** *
or ! —! —! It is hard, in fact, to find a record of
a single damn. One member, with the best inten-
tions in the world, tried to raise a little excite-
ment by spreading the report that the Govern-
ment had “squared ’’the Crick cases against the
Speaker and the Sergeant-at-Arins. That being
about the sort of thing Joseph might do, some
actually believed the “snake.” Later, there
seemed a prospect of something of the old spirit
getting loose when one member got up to explain
why he had charged another member with having
accepted £1 for finding somebody a billet on the
railway's. The other member rose up, and was
much affected, and forgave his slanderer. But
there was no language. N.S.W. Assembly is
evidently in a tired mood just now 7 .
Thynne’s Bananaland friends are preparing
to give him a warm time when he returns. Thynne
is their mouthpiece in the Upper House —the
House of Stinking Fish. But he is now in
England, trying to raise money for the Chillagoe
Co. to build the Etheridge railway with, and a man
who wants to raise a lot of money can’t afford to
say that his country is ruined, and gone to the
dogs and the devil. His friends can hardly
believe that it was A. J. Thynne vdio said at the
annual Queensland dinner : : —
We used to have great times and a great surplus when
we spent from 1 ,£’2,000,000 to £3,000,000 a year of loan
money. Things were lively then ; public and private
expenditure were on- a lavish scale. But we are happily
past that. Public expenditure has been cut down to the
very lowest figure to which a careful Treasurer could
possibly reduce it. Private expenditure has also been
diminished to reasonable proportions. And what is the
result? The people of Queensland —apart from a few
public speakers who are generally a great nuisance—have
turned their attention to actual work ; and it is by actual
work during the last two or three years that they have
been able, without the aid of borrowed money, to show a
magnificent surplus, and to make substantial progress
individually and collectively. They put their shoulder
to the hollar, pulled .their heavy load over the hill, and
have now in front of them a period of greater prosperity
than they have ever hitherto had in prospect.
The case of Jones, who recently got three
votes at Surry Hills (N.S.W.), suggests something.
Jones is one of the persistent rear-guards of
N.S.W. elections. His record shrieks for itself :
Darlington, 3 votes ; Glebe, 40 ; King, 9 ; Surry
Hills, 3. Now, in N.S.W., it is the custom, when
an old politician is finally rejected for the Assembly
as having outlived any popularity he ever had,
and any usefulness he ever possessed, to make
him a legislator for life in the Upper House.
The Council is the House of the Old Rejected;
why not make it, instead, the House of the
Young Rejected ? If there must be an Upper
House, let it be an elective body, contain-
ing the same number of members as the
Assembly, representing the same constituencies,
and lasting for exactly the same period. At each
election, let the man who tops the poll go to the
Assembly and the man at the bottom go co the
Leg. Council. Then the Council will fulfil its
destiny by being the House of Jones, the Chamber
of Remnants and Tail-ends, and the man who got
the smallest vote of all Could be appointed to the
Chair and called the Scrap. And, as regards its
remuneration, it might have a free railway-pass,,'
authorising it to ride anywhere by cattle or freight
train, and an order for free board and lodging at
a sixpenny restaurant.
This paper receives a cutting from ths
Montreal (Canada) Star (11/5/’O6), which sets
forth that “Mr. E. Vickery, member of the,
Australian Parliament,” says be has seen in
Australia “a happy and prosperous population
pass gradually under the control of a band of labor
union demagogues,” until “the country is being
deserted by capital,” and “thousands of unem-
ployed are walking the streets. ” ; “ E. Vickery,
member of the Australian Parliament,” is also re-
ported as saying that the population of Australia
is 1,500,000 (most people thought it was nearly
three times that), and that 60 years ago it was
only 25,000 (heaven knows where these figures
come from ; the census never showed them).
Now, who is this Stinking Eisli person —“ E.
Vickery, member of the Australian Parlia-
ment?” His name doesn't appear on any list
of members of the Australian Parliament!
Certainly, there is one Vickery who is an
obscure nominee in the Upper House of some
State legislature, but be is a person who has
grown enormously rich in this allegedly ruined
country, and he has so much confidence in it that
lie keeps his stupendous pile invested here, feeling
that it: will be quite safe. So it can’t surely be
that Vickery. -- _• < ; :
Sydney Telegraph is still publishing at
intervals impassioned articles on “Dry Farming ”
—it Dried with unusual energy and at great
length on oth August, and the subject is liable to
crop up at any time in any part of the paper. The
object of all this Dry literature is to convince the
people of N.S.W. that the West is. after all,
quite a desirable place to raise crops in, and that
they should hustle out there, instead of disturbing
the big landowner whose unimproved principali-
ties block settlement in the moist coastal dis-
tricts. “ Dry Farming ”is an American idea (or
theory, or dream, or something), and, according to
the Telegraph, it
shows that with as low as 12in. of rain per annum irri-
gation can be clone without.
This is all very interesting, but, unfortunately,
there is hardly a spot in N.S.W. west of the
mountains that.can rely on getting 12in. of rain,
and sometimes quite a lot of it gets down to 3in.,
and can’t find any water to irrigate with. Then
Australia doesn’t know much about “ Dry Farm-
ing” yet, and the Telegraph’s picture of the
whole country.between the Darling and the moun-
tains as one vast Dry wheatfield, while the big land-
lords remain undisturbed on the coast, may be
premature. Better to exhaust the possibilities of
Damp Farming first.
WHAT IT WILL COME TO.
The new Victorian Licensing Bill provides that the inside of every bar must be clearly visible through a
window easily accessible from the public street.
HKR MASH : “ I came to say good-bye. This is the first day of the new Licensing Act. Drey
as my love for you may be, conversation under these conditions is intolerable.”
Another Tory yarn has collapsed. It ran
to the effect that if the Wages Boards in Victoria
fixed a minimum wage, it would reduce all workers
to a dead level. Now' the annual report of the
Chief Inspector of Factories ai-rives to show that,
though the Board’s minimum was 455. for males
and 20s. for females in the clothing trade—a
typical case—the average wages actually paid were
535. sd. for adult males, and 225. 3d. for adult
females.
For the Victorian Senate elections Prime
Minister Deakin lends Government support to
sitting-Senator Styles (consistent Democrat
and Protectionist) and manufacturer Charles
Atkins, over whom Toryism wept when
lie took up tlie cudgels against tlie stink-
ing-fiction party. The sobbing Tories
are still in a state of suspense as
regards their men. They pin their
faith, of course, on Simian Phraser,
as the sole remnant in the Senate
of the old Victorian landlord
party, and a man who can be relied
on to"shriek against anything new or
progressive in legislation. And
they are beginning to boost Jimmy
M‘Coll, M.H.R., the man who
voted, to send manufacturing
monopolists to gaol, but moved to
let rural monopolists (including
butter-agents) go free. Even the
name of Croker, the cute and dan-
dified solicitor, has been breathed.
It would be a new thing for Aus-
tralia to possess a Senator who is ac-
cused of wearing corsets.
Bent lias just passed another vote of
censure on <l. Oarruthers’ methods. When Car-
ruthers decided to knock the State Fire Insurance
Fund on the head (by the way, Oarruthers hasn’t
explained yet what he did with the accumulated
funds), he handed over the insurance business to
a middleman broker, instead of dealing direct with
the cos. Bent lately found that the same thing
was in vogue in Victoria, and jumped on it with
all his tonnage. But he might have gone further
than that. If it’s a good thing, as he says, to have
the State’s interest paid to Australians, it might
have occurred to Thomas that it is also a good
thing to have the State’s premiums paid to the
same Australians.
Besides offering £ll,OOO a year to help the
fishing industry, as set forth in the Federal Bonus
Bill, the Commonwealth Government proposes to
run a steam trawler on its own account. Some
bull-necked person, with a large voice and a small
intellect, is bound to rise in red heat to protest
against this Socialistic interference with Private
Enterprise; but as the lish have been racing
round these coasts from time immemorial, and
Private Enterprise hasn’t tackled the job of
stopping them, it is hard to say where the inter-
ference comes in. You can’t interfere with a
thing, even the size of an elephant, if it isn’t
there.
Ex-Freetrader Irvine becomes more mar-
vellous than ever. His latest, from Dandenong
(Vic.): —
He felt so strongly on that point (i.e., preferential
trade within the Empire) that he did not care how high
the duties were raised against foreigners, so long as our
own kith and kin were treated fairly.
Not a word nowadays from Irvine to say that
England’s glory rests upon the Open Door, or
that only by unrestricted commerce can we hope
to retain the greatness won by our forefathers.
Irvine now turns to Protection (“he does not care
how high.’’) to save the British Empire from
wreck. On all his old theories he is silent. But
profoundest of all is his silence regarding the
land-tax, for Which he barracked with such in-
tense earnestness when he went into the State
Parliament in 1894.
Farrer, M.L.A., the very newest Anti-
Sosh member of the Vic. Assembly, who was
elected a week or two ago by the combined efforts
of the Employers’ Federation, the Farmers’
League and the Women’s Gnash Ass, made his
first move in Parliament, when he gave notice of
a motion favoring the ploughing of the land BY the
State. One of these days, the Anti-Sosh party
will be denouncing Watson for stealing all the
planks of its platform.

A female Victorian deputation lately asked
Bent to support a private Bill now before the
State Parliament giving the widow and infants a
lien on the old man’s money after he has departed
for the halo or the hob, as the case may be. lhe
Victorian Parliament has been assaulted with the
humane proposal for years, but the House of
Landlords has always thrown it out as an insult
to Proputty. Thewomen quoted thecaseofaGipps-
land man who left all his means to the Salv’armv,
and nothing to the wife who, for six year*
before his death, had toiled on the farm without
once going outside its gates ; another man whose
cash went to a Bible Society, and his wife to a
benevolent asylum ; and certain frisky old gentle-
men whose dying benevolence was all in favor
of their housekeepers. Bent promised that the
Bill to burke the aged saint or sinner in his evil
intentions towards his wife and children should
have Government support, so this time it seem*
to have a chance of passing.
