SPORTING NOTIONS.
Saturday last was chilly enough in Sydney to
make Rugby a game better worth playing than watching,
but* all the same, huge crowds gathered to look on. In
A Division Glebe played a remarkable game with South
Sydney, and won bv 10 to three. Balmain crushed
Mahlv by 20 to six—an awful drubbing, in spite of which
Maiily played gamely to the finish. Newtown downed
St. George by five to nil. In B division Sydney repulsed
University, the Collegians going down by five to six a
mighty small margin, but large enough to count.
Eastern Suburbs just managed to squelch Western
Suburbs by the same margin, in a game in which the
figures were 12 and 11 respectively. North Sydney had
the bye. The result is to leave Glebe still at the top of
the poll, with 23 points. The closest of the pursuers are
Eastern Suburbs and Newtown, with 19 points each.
South Sydney, University and Sydney follow with 17
each ; Balmain is 15, and Western Suburbs 11.
The burning interest that rival sectaries took
in each other’s immortal loss in the days when Calvin
and other strong-minded people were rather gone on
metaphysics, was quite equalled by that which raged
between Glebe and South Sydney teams on Saturday.
The barrackers of both sides were quite mad, and shouted
frantic requests to have the inner machinery of the oppo-
site side strewn on the field. Although the players did
not take this advice, some of them, at least, did what
they could. It was a fast, strenuous struggle, and had
South been able to keep all its men on the field there is
little doubt that it would have won the match. South
attacked, but the charge was brought to an end by the ball
being thrown out. Glebe secured the throw in and came
away, but South took the leather from Glebe and worked
back. Both sides had been cautioned for rough
play. Much dribbling and heavy ruck work followed,
relieved by marks and futile shots at goal by
both sides, and further numerous warnings re rough
play. A Glebe man struck the original idea of tossing the
South three-quarters men to prevent them following up,
and the referee granted a free kick to South. Nothing
came of it, and the gambols resumed. Cotter scored for
Glebe, but failed to convert. South attacked strenuously,
and, after a severe struggle, got over, but also failed to
convert. Then a South man smote the Glebe forward on
the jaw in the scrum, and was ordered off the field.
Another South man had been hurt, and the spell came to
an end with scores equal and South two men short.
The second spell proved that the lack of two men
was an impossible handicap. South played mag-
nificently, and let loose a series of splendid charges,
all of < which were pulled down, and the weakened
team hadn’t the strength or the weight to break
through Glebe defence. Right at the back Iledley, the
great full-back, sent the charging Southerners back again
and again by .the simple expedient of finding the line.
Then Glebe scored a field goal, and it was seen that the
South men’s case was hopeless ; but they played on splen-
didly. In the bitterness of impending defeat the game
became very, very rough, indeed. Another South man
was. hurt, and was carried off. South still sent its
charges right for the Glebe line, and played a game wor-
thy of Thermopylie, or some other place where nothing
hut the object matters. But it was all in vain. Glebe
always secured and kicked steadily down the touch line,
and South Sydney foamed at the mouth as it was slowly
forced back till, right on time, another try was scored by
Glebe, making that club winner by 10 to three. In the
dressing-rooms there were whispers that something was
going to happen, and the Glebe men waited till the crowd
should have thinned. Then they got aboard their ’bus
and started with speed, But before they got properly
going a shower of stones smashed windows* cut faces,
and laid men out insensible. Then all was chaos!
* * * »
Some of the other Rugby matches were equally
interesting from the point of play, but had not the added
distinction of being between the old premiers and the
probable new ones. University nearly fell down dead
with astonishment when it discovered that its forward
pack was not in the same street as that of Sydney. The
Uni. backs never got a show to work in any of their
pretty passing charges, the Sydney pack crushing
the faintest attempt to get the leather out. But for the
sound defence and splendid line-kicking of the Uni. backs
the game would have ended in a rout. As it was, Uni.
Was lucky to escape with a one point defeat—Sydney’s
superiority was worth many points. The Eastern
Suburbs victory over Western District was the narrowest
of close shaves and a streak of marvellous luck. The
speed of the Western forwards was terrific, and they ran
through the Eastern forwards and bustled the backs,
breaking up even the redoubtable Messenger and Rosen-
feld combination. The first half ended with Western
District leading by three to nil. flay was very
rough at times, both sides having a man sent
off the field. Ward Prentice played a sterling
game, and took much of the limelight that usually falls
on Messenger, whose brilliancy the speed of the Western
Subui-bs forwards had temporarily dimmed. West con-
tinued to score—eight to nil. Then Rosenfeld got over
and Messenger converted, making the score eight to five.
Western scored again, but, failing to convert, got only
three, and, with time running out fast, Eastern Suburbs
saw itself six poihts behind. Then, for the first time in
the game, the Eastern forwards showed some speed.
They took the leather to the line, and, after a struggle,
got over, but;\ydre penalised for being off side. At half
way the Eastern machine got going, and scored at the
corner. The kick failed, and the sun dipped down a bit
lower, and hope went with it. Western district was still
leading by three. The Eastern captain hurled his whole
team right at the line—a forlorn hope for a draw. West
stood the shock gallantly, and Ward Prentice got the
leather and strove to kick clear with the Eastern for-
wards right on top of him, It flew out low, and D’Al-
puget, East full-back, gathered it in, and right on time
the ball soared up and over the bar, a four point field
goal, leaving Eastern Suburbs winners by one point.
* * * *
They have some debacles in Maoriland Rugby
too, occasionally. Last Saturday! Auckland trampled
Canterbury under foot, winning by 29 to six in the inter-
provincial contest.
In the British Association circles of N.S.W. the
Gardiner Cup contests are attracting as much attention
as the competition matches. Sydney and Ultimo put up
a stern struggle at Epping course on Saturday; excite-
ment was at fever pitch when, at half-time, Ultimo led
by three to two. Sydney changed the positions of the
players in the team, but it apparently made no difference.
Ultimo increased the score to four to two, and Sydney’s
barrackers were suddenly tongue-tied. But the city
team was far from being beaten. It suddenly gave its
opponents an exhibition of speed that just paralysed
them, and two goals were scored in very short order,
making the game a draw—four all. Glebe and Woonoona
also got off their struggle for the Cup, and the country
team was scrunched—nine to three. Out at Granville,
Pyrmont, the premier club of the season, wiped the floor
comfortably with the local team,winning by four to one.
'» * * *
Something like a boil-over occurred in the
League Competition (Australian Rules) in Sydney last
week. Newtown was leading in the competition with 22
points; Sydney second With 20 points. On Satur-
day last Balmain, which was third on the list, with 16
points, beat the leading club by 65 to 52. Sydney wal-
loped East Sydney by 80 to 46. This brought Sydney
level with Newtown, with 22 each, and made Balmain
second on the list, with 20. The shocking example of the
day was Paddington, which Redfern dished by 120 to 13.
Another event like that and Paddington will decide to
confine its attention to skittles.
Saturday’s football in Melbourne showed pretty
clearly that the Association’s contention that the League
gives it no show for a “ gate ”is mere blather. Were the
eight League clubs to perish to-morrow, the Association
“gates” wouldn’t gain 20 per cent. There were but
three matches for the afternoon. In the League, the
weak “ Saints ” beat the weaker Melbourne by 73 points
to 40, before a crowd of about 3000, while at Carlton a
good 20,000 watched Fitzroy wallop the locals. All Asso-
ciation matches were suspended while a combined team
met S. Australia’s chosen 18, j’et the East Melbourne
ground, where they played, could have accommodated
six times the number of spectators—probably 3000 all
told. And those who stayed away were fully justified.
The weakest League club, Melbourne, could have easily
beaten a team picked from the combined Inter-Staters.
Their “systems” were non-existent, they kicked
and passed anyhow, their marking was more lucky than
happy, and, in fact, they did everything a beginner would
do and a past-master avoid. Yet, it was not a bad game
to watch, for, though Victoria led right throughout the
proceedings, the individual play of two or three Crow-
eaters was so vigorous that there were always hopes of
their doing something. They did it in the last quarter,
piling on seven goals five behinds, to Victoria’s one goal
one behind, but the effort came too late, and Bent’s State
won by 103 points to 87. The huge crowd that trammed
itself out to Carlton knew what to expect—and got
it. A very fast, open game with just enough “devil”
about it to keep one’s nerves strung from the bounce.
Carlton, as usual, played the losing individualistic game,
but was sure in picking up and straighter in kicking. At
quarter-time ’Roy had scored one goal five behinds to one
goal one behind. At half-time the maroons had added
another behind, while Carlton had put on two more goals.
The third quarter saw Carlton kick another goal and a
behind, while ’Roy added two goals and four behinds —
leaving it two points ahead of the blue-and-whites.
The final quarter saw Carlton make a determined rush,
and, in spite of a magnificent defence, its prize kick,
Caine, snatched the ball on a bounce, screwed swiftly
and punted a goal. That gave Carlton four points
of a lead, and the barrackers clapped the Lizzies on the
back and yelled, “ What’d I tell yer? Blime, ain’t it
prime?” But it wasn’t all over. The ’Roys’mettle was
up. Steadily they passed the leather on to within shoot-
ing distance. Twice they tried, but only scored minor
points. Then Trotter, their surest kick, got a mark at
an angle,'.6oyds. away. He placed the ball, and, taking
along run, drove it right across the wind fair
through the posts, and ’Roy yelled itself hoarse.
There were still five minutes to play, and Carlton
played for all it was worth. It got the chance,
and a long straight drive would have given it
victory, but though Caine sent the ball far over the
backs’ heads, he allowed too much for the wind, and the
globe went a foot outside the post, leaving ’Roy winner
by four goals 14 behinds to five goals throe behinds. The
better team won, but it almost deserved to lose for its
wretched kicking. This match leaves four teams only
with a chance of the pennant—Carlton, Essendon, ’Roy,
and ’Wood—and on present form there is little to choose.
* * * *
The whole of the suspended cricketers, having
put their case in as favorable a light as possible, and
become fairly ’umble, have now been re-admitted
to playing privileges by the N.S.W. Cricket Asso-
ciation, but are debarred from holding any office
for three years. On the whole, the Association
was magnanimous. Curiously enough, some of the
dailies were shocked at what they called the grudging
spirit of the Association, but that body was quite right.
In the meantime it is rather humorous to find that the
players, who said they only joined in with the Mel-
bourne Club for the purpose of ensuring a visit from an
English team, have been the real means of preventing one
coming next season. They inflated themselves on top of
their precious dignity so long that by the time they got
down again to their real size it was too late, according to
the Marylebone Club, to arrange for the tour. Where-
fore the players are likely to be further disliked by that
section of the public which deemed a visit next season
essential to the existence of the country.
* * * *
Twenty-nine started in the 30-mile race for the
Dunlop Individual Championship of N.S.W., the most
important amateur cycling race in the State, last
Saturday. The clubs represented were Marrickville,
Balmain, Enfield, Botany, Northern Suburbs, Swift,
Redfern, Annandale, Western and Eastern Suburbs.
Last year M‘Lean of the Western Suburbs ran away with
the event, and so fast did he ride from the jump last
Saturday that before the first 10 miles had been left
behind all but eight had dropped out. Starting on the
second round he went even faster, and attempted to
break away ; but had he done so it would have meant
acting as his own pacer, so he stayed with the bunch. At
20 miles the eight had dropped to five. In the last round
MT.ean came down, ripping spokes out of his back
wheel, and shaking himself up considerably. But he
remounted and flew on, and another man dropped out.
It then lay among four men, who were bunched together.
At the finish, H. Coleman, of the Marrickville Club,
sprinted, and M'Lean dropped into second place, with
Thompson, of Eastern Suburbs, third, and S. Goodben,
the winner’s club-mate, fourth. The time was lhr.
17min. 41 l-ssec.
* *• * *
Bathurst to Sydney road-race, promoted by the
Wallace Continental Tyre Co., will drop perspiration and
language along the country roads this Saturday. At
time of u'riting, somewhere about 100 have accepted and
are riding up to Bathurst on their own machines by easy
stages, making themselves acquainted with the track,
and the best places to break their necks. The Wallace
Co. has made the usual arrangements for supplying solid
and liquid refreshment to the cyclists as they whiz past
after the elusive loot. At Katoomba, about half-way, the
road has been swept clear for about five miles on both
sides of the town, and that garnished patch will be a rest
to the weary cyclist. t * *
The Cyclists’ Association got off its Camden to
Ashfield (N.S.W.) road race last Saturday. Over 100 co-
mpeted, so apparently the Association is fairly strong in
numbers, even if it is weak in cash and organising genius,
and, of course, there is always the chance of its raising a
good manager who can make things hum. The finish was
fairly exciting, A. Blakewell, the winner, defeating
Avery, the second man, by a couple of lengths, with third
arrival about 25 yards behind. This, in a run of 36 miles,
suggests that the handicapper has some horse-sense.
* * * *
Defections between date of nomination for
V. A.T.C. G.N. ’Chase and convincing day (Saturday) were
much greater than in connection with V.A.T.C. G.N.
Hurdles. Steeple list was reduced from 60 to 14, and yet
one more started than when Beilis scored last year. But
the race has never been contested by big fields ; no more
than 18 lined up at any time in its history, and there
have been occasions when only half that number showed
out.
* * • *
Isis, the only mare in the field, won Caulfield
G.N. Steeplechase last Saturday, running clean away
from Cardinal after they had cleared the last jump. Isis,
whose sire, Leroy, was a Musket horse, has been one of
the most consistent and safest performers at the game.
Last year she ran second to Beilis in the V.R.C. National,
and third to him at Caulfield. This year, with a lighter
burden and weaker opposition, she did the trick hand-
somely. Of the mare’s most dangerous opponents, Corin-
thus came early to grief. The General also fell (punters
must now be tired of expecting The General to get
round), and top-weight Beilis was pulled up. She had
nothing else to fear save Cardinal, and w'hen it came to a
question of stamina the son of Richelieu (a non-stayer)
was only good enough to finish in the second place once
Noteworthy that in the last three G.N. Steeple-
chases at Caulfield both Isis and Cardinal have been
twice placed, and in each of the three events the number
of competitors has been reduced to six or seven at the
finish.
* « * *
The N.S.W. pony-racing clubs are probably
sorry now that they spoke so loud. Had they gone round
the corner and settled their dispute quietly over a whisky
and soda there might have been no Gaming and Betting
Bill to-day. It was only when they started fighting over
one miserable day per week, and writing letters to the
papers about one another, that it really dawned on the
public generally that these clubs were racing six days out
of seven, and filling the working week with a stench of
horse, bad language, welshing, and the general villainy ot
the unregistered turf. Then Cholly Wade looked over
the fence, heard the language, heard the hum of the
villainy, sniffed the odors, and hurried away to prepare
his little Bill. They fought over a day in the week, and
now the pony clubs find themselves restricted to 16 days
racing each in the year on their own courses. No wonder
they yell—a fall from 156 to 16 racing days in a year is
calculated to make any club scream.
* * * *
The fatuous punter who specs his coin early has
been sorely dealt with during the past week. Maori-
lander Mahutonga, rated a good thing for one or more of
the coming Spring’s big plums, has broken down hope-
lessly, as far as present engagements are concerned, and
Melbourne Cup favorite Lord Ullin’s Daughter, up to her
eyes in a thorough Cup preparation, which is widely
different from the ordinary thing, has jarred a tendon
and been eased entirely for the time being. Trainer J.
Allsop fears the mare will not stand the necessary work
even if she survives present trouble.

“F.C.”: Do the V.R.O. and other “legitimate”
clubs license I. Noses with the view of safeguarding the
public, or simply to grab some more fees? If the former,
where does the protection come in ? A Sydney man put
£2 on Windy and £1 on Sweet Bird for the Valley Handi-
cap at Moonee Valley recently. The Ikey bore [the
usual “ltegd. V.R.C.” ticket; but the visitor |got a
hint that his man was a “ balancer.” He asked for his
stake back, and was refused ; then called the police and
offered to sign a charge of welshing. They declined to
do anything. Sure enough, when Windy won, the
“balancer” handed back the stake of £2, but held firmly to
the £1 put on Sweet Bird. And M‘Leod hadn’t been dead
a month. It looks as though the M’Leod incident has
scared the crowd more than it has the welsliers and
“ balancers.”
* * * *
Coursing scribe of Sydney Referee cheerfully
foretells a “pronounced revival” in the hare-torturing
game next season. An exceptionally optimistic nature,
surely, seeing that Kensington Club had to abandon a
meeting the other day, owing to poverty of entries, and
Belmore Proprietary Club is on the verge of putting up
its shutters. Also there is likely to be something doing
in the State legislature shortly owing to the cruelty of
the alleged sport.
* # * •
The Victorian A. A. A. boxing and wrestling
championships will be a five-nights affair, commencing
on the 18th, at the Athenaeum Hall (Melb.). It has been
suggested that Judkins be invited to view the contests,
in order to see that they are quite unlike the horrors he
has pictured in his mind. Among foremost local ’rastlers
is a policeman, whose chance in the heavy event was
rosy, but, owing to the absurd regulations restricting
members of the force from joining in such competitions,
he is a non-starter. This goes to explain why nine alleged
burly constables are sometimes needed to capture one
sinewy rat of a law-breaker; the cop doesn’t get the
practice he should have. Weber and “Delhi” Neilson
wasted much time at the Russell-street barracks in im-
parting their knowledge, but the classes fell through
owing to the stalwart pupils having severe attacks of that
tired feeling. The tired feeling might have been got over
if there had been the excitement and limelight of an occa-
sional competition to look forward to, but all the joy in
sight consisted of a prospective rough-and-tumble with a
pushite in an alley.
■K * * *
Clem Hill, with the help of a liberal handicap,
won the big open event in connection with the S.A. golf
championship. The famous batsman is really pretty
good at all sports. He has lately scored at both golf and
lawn-tennis, and at billiards and hurdle-racing events
promoted by the Adelaide Stock Exchange.
* * * *
Ex-ten-miler Stillwell effected a surprise in the
five-mile cross-country championship at Ballarat last
Saturday by defeating the best distance peds. in Victoria.
The course was 2J miles on the Miners’ racecourse, with
five hurdles, and a similar distance of level going across
ploughed fields, barbed wire fences, and sundry crab
holes—the recent rains making it a sloppy journey.
Eighty odd runners (representing 14 teams) went forth
to get their feet wet. The knowing ones’ tip was George
Blake for winner, with East Melbourne a cert, for team
supremacy, but the r esult put the tipsters to con-
fusion. At the gun a rush for positions to the first jump
saw Sylvester (Hamilton) ahead from Kirby (East Ballarat),
the old hands waiting for the squib division to lose their
steam. P. White, Bult and Ferguson displaced the leaders
on the back stretch, and passed the stand for the first lap in
that order, but gave way to Blake before leaving the
course on the journey cross country. Half-way turn-
saw Blake 40yds. ahead of Stillwell, who got no nearer
till on the course for the finishing lap, when he crept up
on the leg-weary leader at every stride, showing some of
the old dash that had won three “tens” in succession.
Blake made strenuous efforts to get a move on, but the
Bssendon man ran in a winner with 100yds. to spare in
29min. 11 2-ssecs.—a rattling exhibition of stamina,
pace and judgment. Stillwell was a non-competitor
during last season, and aforesaid event amply proves that
the spell did him good. Essendon secured the teams
supremacy, defeating East Melbourne by five points, with
Stillwell Bros., Alex, and John Beeson, and Arkley.
T’other surprises were “ Rufus ” Ferguson’s failure to get
nearer than eighth, and Bult’s run into third place.
Jimmy Riddell, a previous five-mile winner, was fourth,
with Aitken, a promising novice, close on his heels.
* * * *
Quigley, of Sydney, having broken up in his
training, Ted Nelson was left without a foeman worthy
of his style at Melbourne Cyclorama on the 4th, so Robert
Fraser undertook at short notice to deal with J ohn Black,
“ the Fighting Englishman.” Robert went into the ring
with his natural fat thick upon him, but he was so much
John’s daddy that “the Fighting Englishman” did not
succeed in planting half-a-dozen solid bashes on him
during the fifteen rounds that the scrap lasted. Having
taken in a few unpleasant shocks in the first two rounds,
41 the Fighting Englishman ” discovered the urgent
necessity of keeping away, and although he delivered
little he avoided a lot, but what he did not avoid was
sufficient to have wearied a working bullock. Black,
although showing the red badge of courage all over him,
kept going with great gameness, but was so plainly a
beaten man in the sixteenth spasm, that referee Easto
stepped in between, and declared in a loud voice that
Fraser was the winner. It was a case in which the text,
“ Blessed is the peacemaker,” was very applicable.
* * * «
There is just a possibility that the O’Brien-
Squires fight for the premiership of the world may not
come off in Melbourne, after all. Wren proposes, but
Tommy Bent disposes matters like that, and Thomas
talks about introducing a Bill to suppress fights for
purses, and to make it a penal offence to charge admis-
sion to any battle, scrimmage, row', unpleasantness, or
boxing contest. If Bent fixes things so that to charge to
see O’Brien hit Squires and Squires retaliate, means
gaol, then the disagreement will not take place in
Melbourne. Of course, it is known that Thomas
has often threatened to do things which remain
undone. For instance, he threatened to open State
brickyards ; but they haven’t materialised, and it
is just possible that his Bill for the suppression of
prizefights may not get through Parliament till after the
O’Brien-Squires scrap has been decided. Anyhow, in the
event of such a measure driving the exhibition out of
Victoria, there is not the slightest chance of Carruthers
passing a similar Bill. His desire to score off Victoria
would cause him to regard a broken nose os part of the
splendid natural resources of his State.
* * * *
The Fraser-Black fight, at Melbourne Cyclo-
rama last week, was chiefly remarkable for the change of
referees. Joe Stokesbury has refereed there—and, on the
whole, has done it well —ever since the Olympic Club
started promoting mills. Some of his decisions of late
have, however, been uproariously questioned by the shil-
ling seaters, who, whatever their judgment may be worth,
can raise a fearful row. “ Joe ” objected to this, and con-
tracted the habit of yelling back at the “ bob ” multitude.
Then Stokesbury resigned, possibly because his voice had
given out with shouting at the “ bobbers,” and the Club
appointed Paddy Basto as referee, with two judges outside
the ring to keep his decisions in order. Considerable
question, is raised now as to who is to referee the big
championship fight in November. Stokesbury’s resig-
nation seems to put him out, and Basto is too excitable
a man to be given such a big job. O’Brien is noted
as a master of ring tactics, which means that he
knows more ways of beating the referee than
most other men. Squire’s method of fighting
contains so much of hustle and so little of what is
generally known as “ science” that a referee without fear
and w'ithout reproach will be needed. Wren’s sense of
grandeur, and omnipotence has increased to such an
alarming degree of late that it is quite possible he may
want to climb through the ropes and referee the fight
himself. He has never done it before ; but then he never
appeared as a barrister until the other day and he
considers that he made a brilliant success of it. Anyway
his argument will be that when a man provides the purse,
and the place and the pugs the least he can claim is to
act as referee. Perhaps, though. Bent will be as good as
his roar, and there’ll be no referee, and no fight.

A promising lad, Ted McGibbon, met Alf.
Byfield at the Olympic (Melb.) A.C. on Friday night.
The lads opened proceedings in willing fashion. Byfield
was specially aggressive, but was soon' steadied by
\leGibbon, who landed several stinging straight lefts to
he face. Second act proved as vigorous as No. 1. the
iads crowding as much work into the allotted three
minutes as is usually witnessed over twice the distance.
But it was evident that the pace couldn’t last, and
■i the following rounds Byfield was very rooky ; he hung
■n and wrestled, and the exchanges were feeble compared
nth previous efforts. Fourth and last rou.ud saw con-
restants tangled among the ropes. Alf., getting the
vorst of the deal, reclined for four seconds, but assuming
the upright position once more, hurled himself into the
fray, only to meet the boards again for eight, and then
for seven seconds. Finally, Byfield smote Ted one,
under circumstances that caused the referee to order a
foul, and he awarded the cash to Mac. It mattered not
whether the loser fouled out or was knocked out. He
couldn't save himself from going out.
» » » •*
About the best display of “boxing” seen in
Sydney for a long while was the meeting between Johns
and.Blaqkmore, at the National Sporting Club, last week.
Johns is the welter-weight champion of Australia, and
lias been for some years. They started to slow music,
but gradually increased the pace till the atmosphere
emitted a warm glow. Occasionally there were brief
flashes of fight, but, taken all through, it was a splendid
exhibition of footwork, head-manipulation, and all the
liner tricks and graces of the art of glove-fighting, the
absolute antithesis of the crude mauling which another
referee had allowed to disgrace the week before. As
a refined and elevating show it stood on the same footing
as skirt dancing at an afternoon garden party, and if the
dub could guarantee that fights would always be con-
ducted on the sariie exquisite lines, one' side of the
gallery might be set apart for ladies, so that they might
study the disturbances as essays in deportment. Only
once was “blug” visible—a mere streak across the
mouth and chin that suggested that Blackmore had
been struck with a bunch of poppies. The fearsome
part of the business was the tremendous swipes and
bangs which hit neither party. Some of them were ter-
rible, and damaged the atmosphere severely. In the end
Johns won the elegant dancing competition on points.
* * * *
“Brumby”: After the Felix-Kling brawl of
the previous week, the Jolins-Blaekmore scrap at the
National Sporting Club (Sydney), was a work of art.
Compared to it the crude efforts of strenuous laborers
whitewashing a fence would be labors of main
force and fury. The former was a scuffling struggle
between two huge, ungainly animals, who floun-
dered and fell over each other on to the floor, and rose
breathlessly', and closed helplessly', and hung, nearly a
quarter-ton of meat and bone, on the ropes, making
vague and un-Christian atteinpts to disable each other.
But the scienced bout between the lighter men was
something to remember in the days when the fire of life
is low, and memory is all that is "left of the sinful past.
Blackmore was the in-boring fighter, always clever in
evasion and attack, but, above all, a hitter of the smash-
ing, dangerous order. Johns shaped as the pastmasterof
skilled grace. In him were combined the effectiveness of
the natural fighter, the cleverness of the consummate
hoxer, and the airy lightness of the danseuse. He was a
toe-ornainent, a dodgy penny balloon, a butterfly, a thing
of air. The whole exhibition was a wonder of pug-cul-
rure and a revelation of the poetry of motion, and it ex-
hilarated the mind like a seven-course feed of beer.
Probably it takes a long term of weary practising
before a mirror to attain Johns’ pre-eminently
cultured sty'le, but it’s a pleasant result anyhow, and the
end justifies the means.
* * * *
For some reason John Wren, Melb. tote law-
breaker, was not at the Olympic Club fight between
Fraser and Black last week. Consequently the admiring
public couldn’t nudge one another and say', “ That’s
’im.” But there were three big, quiet men there that
everybody gazed at. They' were Mike Williams, Bill
Squires, and Billy M‘Coll. They made a fine trio.
Williams is burly but active, with a deep chest like that
of a bull, and a massive mahogany face like the figure-
bead of an old warship. He speaks with a rich Irish
brogue, suggestive of the bogs of Connaught. William
Squires is always William Squires—massive, blocky, quiet
and thoughtful, with never a smile until the ring is
mentioned, and then his face lights up. And Billy
M'Coll is dapper, neat, well-tailored, well-groomed, and
well-spoken, as unlike a prize-fighter as can be imagined.
* * * *
National Sporting and Olympic Clubs, which
pretty well monopolise promotion of boxing events in
Sydney and Melbourne respectively, have decided to
reciprocate, “ to elevate boxing, and for the good of the
sport and boxers themselves.” Boxers will probably be
“ benefited ” by the giving of reduced purses, and being
compelled to fight where and under what conditions the
reciprocating clubs desire.
* » * *
“0.K.”: That quaint purist, Bent, talks of
introducing a short'Bill to prohibit prize-fights in \ .e-
-toria, on the ground that they are “ brutal exhibitions.”
Bent, viewed in the present and the past, looks onq of the
last men on earth who might be expected to shudder at
the spectacle of two pugs hitting one another, with a
limited amount of violence, but for that very reason he
is an impressive supporter of the anaemic Kerristian
party. A form of entertainment that appears brutal to
Thomas Bent would probably invite the same objection
from a majority of men if that majority had any per-
sonal knowledge of the matter. As regards women, it
may be roundly asserted that all, save the very lowest,
would vote for the suppression of contests which
are expected to terminate in a knock-out. The
art of prize-fighting, as exhibited by its most successful
professors, is not a little bit beautiful, nor is it
much of an art, seeing that a hurricane puncher, on the
one hand, is said to be a “ natural ” or born fighter, whilst
the fellow who stands up against him is credited with “ a
fine capacity for receiving punishment,” which is not an
artistic attribute. Nor is it much of. a manly art, as far
as it is art at all; otherwise, women would crowd to prize
fights as they crowd to all exhibitions of maplv
strength, activity, and pluck. It is largely a brutal busi-
ness, when its immediate purpose is unmitigated stoush,
and its ultimate object is a knock-out. A man who says
this courts the ridicule and scorn of prize-fight patrons in
general. But who are they—in general ? The most,
animal-minded and least law-abiding section of the com-
munity, leavened in their multitude by a sprink-
ling of intellectual “ sports,” including, perhaps, a
Supreme Court Justice, who has, in his time, passed
sentence on quite a number of the excited spectators.
One can afford to be laughed at by the paying
patrons of stoushirig entertainments, and hated by the
hangers-on of the game who get in free, and the pals of
the hangers-on who assemble outside the hall of
“ science ” to drink in the distant shouts of approbation,
or otherwise, with their large, outstanding ears. A
prize-fight is distinctly not a game of boxing in the sense
that a test match is a game of cricket, or a trapeze
“ turn ”at Rickards’s is an exhibition of gymnastics. Its
claim to be a display of “art” is negatived by the all-
round expression on the sea of faces what time, a Bill
Squires is giving the other bloke beans. Bent says it is
brutal, and he’s about right. Nobody has ever yet
accused Bent of erring in the direction of spirituality.
* * * *
Two of the recent dawg shows in Melbourne
reveal some astonishing differences of judicial opinion.
At the Vic. Dog and Poultry Show first and special prizes
were awarded to Northcote Baby in the Bull-dog
puppy (bitch) class, and; among the unmentioned com-
petitors was Light Brigade, entered by Mr. Sevior, of
Prahran. Mr. Sevior’s other bull-pup, Up Guards
(dog), took 2nd prize in its class, no first being awarded.
Evidently the judges reckoned that Up Guards was a
fairly good sort of bull-pup, and Light Brigade a pup of
no importance. But .when the same two animals were
exhibited at the Prahran Show, only three weeks later,
each of them got a first and special prize in its class,
whereas Northcote Baby, the bitch that scored first
and special at the Vic. Dog and Poultry Show, was now
branded second to Light Brigade, the bitch that had
previously gone unmentioned.
* ■* * *
Rhodes, for the first time in his career as a
Yorkshire county player, finds himself a long way down
in the list of bowling averages. On Ist July his record
was 55 wickets at a cost of 22.71 each, whilst Haigh,
another Yorkshireman, had 99 wickets for an average of
11.06. But as a batsman Rhodes has been promoted to
the dignity of a stonewaller for his side. As a rule, he is
one of the first pair at the wickets, and he takes first
strike.
A young lady said to the professor of
anatomy at a southern university, the other
day : “I intend to leave my brain to you when
I die.” The unexpected response was: “ Oh,
goodness, I shall be in hell years before you, my
dear.”
In Melbourne, a few days ago. Miss
Weekes, a good-looking young woman, sued
Walter Mayhew, a middle-aged remittance man,
for £2OO, money lent. The lady’s tale was
that they had been engaged, and that the re-
mittance man’s usual trouble had encountered
him—his cheques had miscarried or something—
and she had tided him over sundry difficulties.
Also, she had advanced £5O in one lump to pay
his fare to the Cold Old Land, so that he could
prosecute his claim to a large sum alleged to be in
“Chancery.” He promised to repay her every-
thing “when they were married,” but instead
of that he went and married someone else,
although it was alleged that he already
possessed a wife in Westralia. The amazing
part of the matter was the remittance-man’s
defence. He swore that he had never had
any money from plaintiff at all, except
a trifle of £3, which he had repaid; but
that he and she had gone to a solicitor’s office,
at her suggestion, and that he had there written
out a statement that he owed her £2OO, and
ordered that the better part of his blessed remit-
tance was to be paid by the lawyer to her “in
order to keep it from his present wife”’ Also, he
swore that his intention was to desert his present
wife and go away with plaintiff. It was an amazing
sort of defence, and the cross-examining lawyer
spoke to defendant quite insultingly; but that
amiable person was no more ruffled than the skin
of his belltopper. As the lawyer! who drew up
the agreement backed up the plaintiff’s statement
his Honor enthusiastically gave her a verdict for
the full £2OO, and the remittance man departed in
a subdued but casual frame of mind.
A statement by Cardinal Moran that,
shortly before his death, Henry Parkes expressed
regret at the hand he took in the non-sectarian,
secular Public Education Act of N.S.W., has
brought from his retirement Francis Abigail, who
asserts that he was entirely in the confidence of
Parkes, and was assured by him shortly before his
death that the Public Instruction Act was the one
great work of which he was proud. And it would
be difficult to believe, even on the say-so of many
Cardinals, that the hairy old man ever regretted
having passed the measure—even though it wasn’t
his measure, but one which he calmly borrowed
from an opponent.
Goldsbrougli, Mort and Co. report: During
the past week there has been little inquiry for wool,
Buyers being disinclined to operate. It is difficult to
a ?fount for thepresent state of the market unless con-
ditions on the European Continent are similar to those
ruling in Bradford, where manufacturers have been lin-
able to obtain for their goods anything consonant with
the high prices paid for the raw material in May and June.

J?*? 1 '* s ,° we Isboul 1 sboult ‘ experience some improve-
ment m the local market a few weeks hence. Auctions
will be resumed on 4th September, when many of the new
clips will no doubt be submitted, and the result is awaited
with interest.*

BEEN CROWING SINCE 1891—15 YEARS’ RESULTS
MEAN SOMETHING TO YOU, DON’T THEY?
MITCHELL Soft Centre
Mould-Boards
Are the result of 20 years’ experience of Australian
soils—2o years’ effort to please you.
Are made of the highest grade of steel—hard
enough to cut glass, yet made so tough by a layer
of soft steel between the two hard wearing
surfaces, that it is a job to break one, if you try.
To be hard enough to cut glass is to wear
wonderfully well, isn’t it ?

PROTECT YOUR FEET A Wonderful Discovery.
DR. HOCYES’ PATENT ASBESTOS SOLES
Prevent and Absolutely Cure Hot. Perspiring, and Inflamed
Feet, Corns, Bunions, Wet and Bad Feet, and other Ailments.
DR. HOGYES, an Army Surgeon, discovered that at the end of a com-
paratively short march, soldiers suffered considerable pain and incon-
venience due to SORENESS OF XHE FEET, and not to ordinary
physical fatigue. After long investigation he found that the evil was
attributable to the irritation set up by the leather insoles in their boots.
Many attempts have been made to overcome this trouble in the bringing out
of Cork and other Soles, but ail these have more or less failed to give the
wearer any appreciable or lasting benefit. There is a unique virtue in the
wearing of DR. HOGYES’ ASBESTOS SOLES, or Socks as they are called, not
to be found in any substitute. The material is very ELASTIC AND SOFT,
and as the double movement is avoided, the powers of endurance are greatly
increased. ASBESTOS—a non-conductor—keeps in the heat and keeps out
the cold and wet. COLD, WET, PERSPIRING AND INFLAMED FEET, CORNS,
BUNIONS, &c., are thus prevented, and, where any of these complaints
already exist, they will quickly disappear after a short use of ASBESTOS
SOCKS, which ease the feet at once, and give the complaints a chance to cure
themselves in a most natural manner.
If, therefore, you are suffering, or liable to suffer, from SORENESS or
IRRITATION of the feet, or from any of the complaints of the feet which
come to us so often, CIVE DR. HOGYES’ ASBESTOS SOCKS A TRIAL, and
the result will surprise you.
Hogyes’ Asbestos Socks are used by the Hungarian Army.

Had to Pot Another Man on to Do His
WorK —-Body Became so Denuded of
Flesh that Clothes Hung LiKe a SacK.
CLEMENTS
TONIC
** Lifted me from that sorry state.*’
Mr. V. BorwicK, Storekeeper, of 286 and
288 Crown Road, Ultimo, relates Bis
complete cure to a Sydney reporter.
“It is six years ago now since I got all right
again,” Mr. Borwick remarked, “and I think it
was about up to me to have a change of luck,
considering that I had been suffering for fully
three years. The pains that ran along my gums
and reached up to the top of my head were some-
thing terrible, and half my time I did not know
what I was doing. My adversities set in when I
was ‘ running the plank,’ as they call it, on a boat
discharging blue metal, and I can assure you that
I was so broken down in health that I had to put
another man on to do my work about every other
week. Talk about neuralgia ! I’m sure nobody
could have it worse than I had, and as I found
such a grand cure for it the least I can do is to let
humanity know all about it, for I daresay my'
experience will prove very helpful. Well, out of
all the medicines I tried to cure neuralgia the only
one that was of any lasting benefit was Clements’
Tonic, and when I say that after keeping going
with that remedy for a few weeks the fearful pains
all left me and have never returned all these years,
it is the honest truth I am telling you.”
“ Is that the only ailment you have had ? ”
“Indeed, no. All the time the neuralgic
tortures were at me I had other troubles to deal
with, for my digestion was just as bad as it could
be. Both from want of appetite, and also owing
to the pains about my jaws, eating was almost out
of the question; but what was still more unfortunate
was the fact that the little I did force myself to
take to keep body and soul together had the
unhappy habit of disagreeing with me. And you
must remember that I was getting no sleep.
Neither night nor day—for weeks at a stretch
sometimes—could I get any rest, and, as you may
well imagine, the result was disastrous. I got so
terribly weak that ultimately I had to give up my
work altogether, and my body was so denuded of
flesh that my clothes hung about me like a sack.
Indeed, I was just going to have some of them
altered about the time that I started on Clements’
Tonic, and by some means or other the idea was
shelved for a while, for which I was afterwards
very glad, as in a few months I was as stoutas ever.
Yes; it was quite a matter for regular comment
how well I waslooking, and, thank God ! I felt well,
too. For three years previous to using Clements’
Tonic I was nothing but a total wreck, for every
part of my body was adversely affected in some
way or other. It was ray first experience of in-
digestion, and I sincerely trust it will he my last,
for I would rather be dead and out of the world
altogether than go through such misery again.
I got to feel as shaky and uncomfortable as if
everything in my inside was going to pieces, and
was so disturbed and unsettled mentally that the
least thing made me irritable. My eyes always
felt heavy and swollen to such an extent
that I could scarcely see to read, and I felt
so discontented and restless that I could not
stay indoors. Consequently I often wandered
about outside hardly knowing what to do with
myself, feeling as though death would be a
thousand times more welcome than the wretched
life I was leading, for I was in agony everywhere.
Ilie pains in my chest were really cruel, and I
thought I had strained myself or broken some-
thing between my shoulders owing to the pain
that was there. It was like getting hold of a
piece of meat and tearing it in two —that was the
kind of feeling I had between the shoulders, and
my loins ached badly into the bargain. ”
“ And have you never had a return of any of
those symptoms during the past six years?”
“ No, upon my honor ; and I have not had that
nasty bulged out feeling in the stomach either
after eating my food. No; Clements’ Tonic
removed everything, even to the bitter taste of a
morning, and the fur that used to cover my
tongue, besides which it gave me permanent relief
from the many attacks of giddiness that I had
previously been subjected to. How much
ray nerves accountsd for the misery I
had erdured is hard to say definitely,
but they undoubtedly had their fair share of
responsibility, for I must tell you that I was so
highly nervous tbit I could not stand the least
excitement without getting all of a tremble, and
I had also contracted an uncontrollable practice
of measuring the future with nothing but trouble
and worry in store. 1 ' All I could see before me
was a continuance of my suffering only to be added
to by misfortunes cf other descriptions, and now
that you understand the gloomy frame of mind I
was in, you will readily conceive why I speak so
highly of Clements’ Tonic for lifting me from that
sorry state to the happy and healthy condition I
am now blessed with.”

The very latest Federal capital Ananias—
from the Sydney Star :
When that most unfortunate decision respecting:
Ualgety is rescinded, there will be sites from which it
will be quite easy to pick one which, without the slightest
infringement of either the letter or the spirit of the Con-
stitution, will meet the reasonable wishes of New South
Wales. And is not the fulfilment of those wishes
solemnly covenanted for in the Constitution?
The Star could get quite a lot of money from
some of the advocates of Lyndhurst and other
sites if it would point out that “solemn
covenant” in the Constitution, which is just
what they have been looking for. And why doesn’t
it print the exact words of the solemn covenant,
and so make the biggest journalistic “ scoop ” of
the century ? A paper that knows a big thing
like that and doesn’t use it hasn’t sense enough to
keep its head out of its own printing machine.
Some people tried to run Hatter Storey on
to oppose J. C. Watson for the South Sydney
Federal seat. To a deputation urging him to
stand, last week, Storey inflated himself consider-
ably, and took it for granted that he could
“relegate Mr. Watson to private life.” The only
consideration that made him hesitate before
rushing into the fray was the “great need for
fearless, courageous men in the State Parliament.”
Were Storey to leave the State Parliament, what
would become of it?—he asked in effect. The
deputation gave the problem up. In the end
Storey gave a dignified refusal. He won’t oppose
Watson.

It begins to dawn on the Victorian
Laborites that if they persist in their treachery to
the Protectionist Liberals who stood by Watson
md 00. they will have to play a lone hand with
few good cards in it. They lately invited the
Federal party to send delegates to confer with
them so as to prevent the Federal and Vic-
torian land-tax schemes clashing. Watson
Fisher and Tudor were appointed. Watson had
a previous engagement; and though Tudor was
fairly amenable, Fisher put his foot down gently
but firmly, and told the'Vic.'Laborites that his
party had propounded its scheme, and intended
to go ahead with it.
It seems impossible for any subject to
come before the Australian Parliament without
Willie-Motor Kelly making some supremely
stupid observations. For instance, on the Defence
qu stion:—
Mr. Kelly (N.S.W.) questioned the application of the
system to Australian conditions. There was no parallel
between Australia and Switzerland, which was a small
country with a comparatively large and concentrated
population.
The Swiss system of defence is simply to train
every man as far as possible, supply him with
arms, and trust in Providence. Little Willie
apparently just hecause he must always be sayin"
something, doubts whether this would do in Aus°-
tralia. He probably thinks that, because we have
so much more country to defend, and so few men
to defend it, we should only train half our men.
Prime Minister Deakin has shown a
willingness to be persuaded into many unwise
things. He will surely draw the line when the
mesmeric Dr. Arthur fixes on him a baleful eye,
and suggests that he sanction the immigration to
Australia of a horde' of Russian Jews. The
country has enough pawnbrokers, money-lenders
and hawkers. And so long as there are over
200,000 British-born people leaving the United
kingdom every year—people who only want
reasonable inducements to come this way—we
have no need for the Badly Chosen people from
Kussia.
Victorian lawyers shudder at local law
statistics. In 1891 their County Court tried 9947
cases; last year the number sank to 582. In the
supreme Court 5744 writs were issued in the
golden year; last twelve months, but a poor 623
and of them only a poorer 117 ended in a trial’
lhe one court that has at all decently held its end
up is the Petty Sessions; and there isn’t much
spoil m that A-it’s a counter-lunch at best.

Ascertained in N.S.W. Parliament that
Bathurst Agricultural Society has been tak-
ing the Treasury down year by year by collect-
ing subsidy on the “subscriptions” of side-
showmen, who put up booths at the Society s.
shows. When Signor Bellabellini, with the most
impossible fat woman on earth, wanted to show
his bloated human goods, admission Od., on
Bathurst show ground, part of the price of the
concession was counted as his membership fee, and
State subsidy was collected on that fee. It was
an ingenious fake. Probably a close examination
of the accounts of other agricultural societies
would lead to similar revelations and do some-
thing to discredit the system under which the
Treasury is bled by “good local members to
subsidise shows which are supposed to represent
the “ great natural industries,” but are very little
agricultural and a good deal cheap-jack gaffs and
inferior circus. (The Sydney show is about the
worst offender in the way of inferior circus.)
During the last half-century, Dr. Hocken,
of Dunedin (M.L.), has spent much time and coin
in acquiring a unique collection of curios, books,
pictures, plans, aud what-not, all relative to the
early days of the State. There are 70011 books
alone, and the lot is probably worth £15,000. The
little medical person lately presented his collec-
tion to the city, and the local Scots waited on
Premier Jones and asked for a grant of £l5OO
to provide accommodation for the valuables
—they would find the other £5OO required them-
selves. Almost the last official act of Temporary
Premier Jones consisted in telling the deputation
that he was amazed at its cheek, and bidding it
go and get work. The committee felt so much
smaller when it left that it had to hold up its kilt
to keep it out of the mud, but Jones’s language
produced good results —Dunedin passed the hat
and got over £lOOO in one day.
The Mountain Brought to Mahomet.
“You should go round and see Brickley,” said
Mrs. Jubbins. “ He’s down with bronchitis
again, and I hear he’s very bad.”
It was Saturday afternoon, and .Tubbins was
going to trim up his front garden. He put down
his hoe. , , , ~,
“Is that so 2 ” he said. “ I knew he hadn t been
at business for some days, but I had no idea —.
Yes, of bourse, I’ll go round. Good sort, old
Brickley is.”
Brickley lived not far away, and when, a tew
minutes later, Jubbins was ushered into his
friend’s dining-room, he stared ; for Brickley—
lolling cross-legged in an easy-chair, and putting
serenely at an old black briar looked like any-
thing but a man who was “ very bad.”
“Why, you old fraud!” Jubbins said, “what
d’you mean by getting out a report that you were
at death’s door when here you are, as jolly as a
sandboy?” . , , ,
“Well,” said Brickley, with a laugh, its
something in the way of a miracle. Up till yester-
day morning I was about as bad as they make em.
Murderous cough, raw chest, pains all over me,
and not the ghost of an appetite. Well, you
know Halliday, don’t you ? ”
“ Hal lid ay, of Gresham and Halliday, do you
mean ? ‘ Husky Halliday,’ as they call him.
“That’s the man. But ‘Husky’ no longer.
When he came to see me, yesterday, Ins voice was
as clear as a bell.”
“ Well, there was certainly room for improve-
ment. He used to make me think of a crwv with
the influenza. But, what has that to do with
“ I’m coming to that. 4 Why,’ I said to him ;
‘ what have you been doing to yourself ? Had an
operation performed on your voice-box?’ ‘No,
he said ; ‘ nothing like that. The change is
due to a much more pleasant remedy, and 1
want you to try it, right away.” With which
be produces a- neat little metal box, takes out of
it a tiny roll of silver-paper, and extracts from that
a small brown lozenge. ‘ Put that in your mouth, ’
he says. I did. The taste was very pleasant,
and reminded me strongly of something I couldn’t
‘place’ at first. Then it suddenly came back.
You know I first studied mining in Germany—in
the Harz Mountains. Well, sir, the flavor of
that little lozenge had in it a thousand memories
of that long-past time. In my nostrils was the
very smell of the pine-clad slopes of the Ober
Harz; a region that has no lung or throat
troubles of its own, though it is thick with chest-
invalids, sent thither from less favored localities.”
“ I see,” said Jubbins. “ And the stuff did you
good ?
“Good 1 Why, I haven’t taken a dozen of the
things, yet they’ve simply made anew man of me.
He landed over the box, and Jubbins put a
lozenge in his mouth. “Very pleasant taste,
anyway,” he said, smacking his lips. Then he
looked at the label on the box. “ Peps, ”he said.
“Oh, I see; something to do with digestion.
• pepsin,’ ‘ dyspepsia,’ and all those words. ”
“ You’re on the wrong track,” Brickley said.
“ Indirectly, the remedy will doubtless benefit
digestion, and all the other functions ; but its
speciality is lung and throat trouble of every sort.
What it does, in a word, is, saves you the time,
trouble, and expense of going to the pine-forest,
because it brings the pine-forest to you. See ? ”
“I see,” said Jubbins. "But, why ‘Peps’?
What does the word mean ? ”
“‘Peps’ is the plural. Each of these magical
lozenges is a ‘Pep.’ P E P—a ‘Pine-Extract-
Pastille.’”
“ Oh, that’s it, is it? Well, they ought to be
the very thing for Mrs. Jubbins’ chronic sore
throat. Where are they to be had ? ”
“Oh, you can get them at pretty well any
Chemist’s or Storekeepers, or from the PEPS
PASTILLE CO., 39 Pitt-street.”
“Right you are. old chap! Good-bye. Glad
you’re better. Mrs. J. shall start on a box with-
out delay.’

“ Skull ” : My suspicions have been
aroused re the genuineness of the disturbances
at moral meetings for the regeneration of beer*
bibbers and tote associates in Melbourne. Having
visited several of those meetings, and observed the
rowdy element closely, I was surprised to find that
it was always the same element. I knew that the
grand panjandrums and worthy past-masters of
the tote and two-up shops have given strict orders
to their followers to abstain from advertising the
small evangelists by whooping-up their gatherings,
because that kind of whoop induces the press to
print excited headlines over the reports of the
same, so I wondered how it came about that these
professional wreckers should go from suburb to
suburb, just to howl at inconsequential little men
platitudinising on a bench, and have themselves
systematically ejected. Now, it’s a dead certainty
that, if no one disturbs the meetings of the small
crusaders, the papers will only mention them in a
cursory way in minute type away over the leaf
among the remarks about the fire that broke out,
and the horse that bolted ; whereas, when the
rowdy contingent rushes in, and howls, and
shakes up the furniture, the papers give the
accounts much space, a front place, and four black
scare-heads. When a lull convinced the wee
band of hysterical reformers that the press was
forgetting them, the disturbances broke out again
suddenly, and with increased virulence, and once
more the orators were widely advertised and
abundantly glorified. My belief is that the moral
revivalists have a tame disturbing element of their
own, which is turned on when things are going slow,
and, after supplying the necessary opposition that

A Maoriland woman has been brutally
interrupted while putting up a record. Her
maiden name was Easton, and she married a man
called Manlove. In a few years she parted with
Manlove and married Udale, a gardener. Later on,
during a domestic disturbance, she revealed her de-
ception, and Udale hoed her out of his little patch.
The lady then travelled north, and lived with
Henderson. He was just an ordinary Henderson,
as Hendersons go, and doesn’t call for any special
comment. But, after a while, Gros.venor asked her
rand in marriage! despite his knowledge of her re-
lationswith Henderson. So she married Grosvenor.
Then a silly ass of a policeman interfered, and
•Justice Copper called it three months—a month
for each husband and nothing for Henderson. A
humane feeling against allowing, a child to be
born in gaol was the cause of this large discount
on taking a quantity.
A Maoriland cohentingenter, who has
lieen doing people and time, seems somewhat
alxive the ruck of thieving swaddies. Archibald
McNeill left with the fourth of Maoriland’s
numerous contingents, and, on his return, when
everybody was blood-mad, he chummed in with
various stylish officers, who drove him round
and introduced him to the best sassiety, as
befitted a ’ero who had won the Victoria Cross.
It transpired afterwards that he had bought this
emblem of valor cheap in London. On the repre-
sentation that he hadn’t received a penhy of his
pay, various people advanced him £l4O in all, and
cursed the Government for its dilatoriness. Then
gas happened to leak, at. the meter, and
M‘Neill went to gaol for three years. Subse-
quently he convinced somebody that he was a
long-lost relative, and secured more money, and
incidentally, more gaol. Immediately on his
release he- we.nt forth in all ,the . glory of
bis new hair-cut, called on an adjacent farmer,
shook him heartily by the hand, and assured
him that lie was his cousin. The agriculturist
scratched his ear dreamily, while the cousin told
of the sheep run in Argentina, which he had just
sold for £12,000 ; then the farmer fell on his
golden breast, and lent him £2O till he could get
his money out of a Wellington bank. This is the
latest trouble, and Archibald McNeill’s new de-
parture is interesting as showing that the old
South African farm-burning narrative is no longer
a good asset to borrow money on, even in Maori-
Jand - Five years ago nd Maoriland swindler
would waste time being a bogus squatter from
Argentina. Everybody was from South Africa
then, and the dust of neglect gathered thickly on
even that good old “prop,” the uncle in Fiji,
xs ow there is a tendency to call for Towzer when
any stranger mentions, as the foundation of
his tale, that he came from the Boers’ burnt
country.
N.S. W. annual Prisons report shows that
expenditure has been reduced from £123,892 in
1890 to £88,356 in 1905. In addition, prisoners’
work represents a saving of £20,000 a year. Gaol
population decreased by 775 in the decade.' The
Comptroller, Mr. Neitenstein, suggests that a
further reduction in the gaol population could be
effected by adopting the system of allowing minor
offenders and quasi-criminals, a period of proba-
tion with liberty, but under police surveillance,
before actually sending them to gaol. He is
optimistic ns to the good results that will follow
from the Habitual Criminals Act and the
Neglected Children Act, recently passed.
The Australian National Party, forwarded
by the indefatigable Charles Atkins (the local
factory hands sing of him as “Tommy Atkins”),
is getting a quite serious acceptance in Mel-
bourne and elsewhere. The 'first object of
the A.N.P. is to collect 2s. 6d. from male
members (ladies are admitted for eighteen pence),
and the money is spent in diffusing information
calculated to stimulate Australian faith in Aus-
tralia—present and future. “ Tommy ” Atkins
is a barracker for the Commonwealth and all that
therein is, or that can be created there by public
endeavor. He shouts plain facts at it, and the
facts are expected to distil public spirit from the
public understanding. In a sense the A.NiP. is
an Anti-Stinking Fish Party, likewise a Band of
Political Hope. In fact, it is so joyously patriotic
that the head master of the Scotch College pro-
poses to form a branch in the school. It will
somewhat counteract the “ By Jingo” element in
public school education.
“ E. J.S.” writes from Tasmania :
Here in Tasmania work in the agricultural districts is
hard to get. Fanners, when they do employ men, pay
only 14s. or 15s. per week and tucker. Hence the exodus
of most of the young, strong and healthy farm laborers to
Maoriland. Many young men with a small capital cannot
purchase land ; therefore migration follows, and Queens-
land and other places benefit. A Credit Foncier system
was talked of, two or three years back, to assist those with
only a little capital; but it never came to pass. Recently,
one Cameron (brother of the Colonel and Norman;
M.H.R.) purchased another estate of 20,000 acres along-
side the one he already holds. These 20,000 acres could
easily support 100 families in this climate ; but instead
they will be used to grow wool and mutton, and about five
families will work them. Another big man named Hart has
acquired nearly 3000 acres within a mile of the rising town
of Devonport. This land is about the finest agricultural
and grazing property in the State, and would easily sup-
port _3O families (say five to a family ; 150 more people) ;
but instead the owner employs an overseer and about
five other men to work it. Send along your Federal
graduated land tax quickly.
Isn’t it about time 3tate members dropped
the absurd prefix “Honourable” wnen addressing
one. another in the House? What’s the matter
with : “Me cobber, the Bloke for Blank Flat?”
It Would be more appropriate and less Irritating
than that weary phrase “ Hon’rable member for
Blajzes.”
Insectarianism is doing the Westralian
Laljror party no good. Every by-contest since the
general election has been largely fought on the
walls of ’Derry, and some alleged Laborites have
done a good deal of the fighting, and have also
made uncalled-for remarks about the Flames of
Smithfield.
A sort of Protectionists’ religion tract,
prepared by Charles Atkins (the Judkins of this
crusade), is being scattered broadcast by the Pro-
tectionist Association of Victoria. The following
remarks on Australia’s woollen industry, or want
of industry, should appeal to every Christian
soul
It looks like a national trip into the realms of insanity
that, while needing work and sustenance for ourselves,
we should go oh giving the foreigners all the work, pro-
fit, and sustenance derivable from our raw material. In
1903 we imported £2,000,000, and in 1904 £2,500,000
worth of manufactured woollens, and this in face of the
facts that the world needs our wool, and that we can say
in what condition we will give it—manufactured or other-
wise. We should ourselves be the woollen manufacturers
of the world. At any rate, immediate steps should be
taken to ensure to us the work and profit evolving from
the manufacture of our own woollen requirements.
Another step might be taken whereby work for thousands
of men would at once be available— viz., by the Govern-
ment forbidding the export of wool in the grease.

Recently Maoriland decided that it wanted
more population, and started to advertise vigor-
ously for liuraansin the English papers. One adver-
tisement appeared, stating that 1000 navvies were
urgently required for the construction of the
North Island Main Trunk Railway. The result
was that a hungry crowd of almost indigent
laborers swarmed into the State, and, having
tramped up to the railway works, took one glance
at the gloomy hills, all veiled in winter rain and
mist, scraped the accumulated mud off their legs,
and then fled hack to the towns to offer to work
at anything at all for about one half of the cur-
rent wages. The result is that an epidemic of
1 ‘ sacking ” has broken out, and men who thought
they Were liiiaerjtaid at SOs. per Week are being
teplabed by gtarviHg rieW arrivals who are abjectly
glad to get 3<js. Meanwhile ,the new Premier has
been fdolirig rdurid the U.S.A. With a reciprocity
treaty, Which can only open the niarkets of Maori-
iarid Wider.still to the Yankee dumper and patent
thief, and make work scarcer, and the rush for
30s. a week more urgent.
The Australian Parliamentary Labor party,
which persistently refuses to plump for high Pro-
tection, got the straight, sad truth, the other day,
from an unexpected quarter. Vic. Chief Secre-
tary Gillott, being asked to appoint a Wages
Board for the Harvester industry, replied that the
employees themselves objected to it; and the
employees at M‘Kay’s Bray brook works presently
backed him up as follows :
Wages Board rates have been paid at Braybrook for
many months, in some cases above the minimum. The
employee? generally recognise that the first thing to be
done is to secure adequate Protection against foreign
dumping, and until this is effected by the Federal Parlia-
ment, any restrictions are uncalled for.
Applying fair working conditions to an Australian
industry, so long as the employer is not adequately
protected against the foreign sweater, merely
means closing up the industry and creating more
unemployed.
Just how preferential trade May Work out
is shown by a circular issued by a Glasgow firm of
nail-exporters. In this the prices of the American
Steel Trust’s nails are quoted, with a footnote,
that “ these prices are for the Australian mar-
ket; if for Maoriland 7£ per cent, less.” Now
Maoriland has, in these items, a preferential
tariff in favor of Great Britain ; this the Yankee
has carefully noted, so he sends his nails through
Britain, knocks off per cent, to cut the Britisher
and the Australian out of the market, and, unless
the Maorilander hag sense enough, to raise his
duty Still Higher, Will presently cut HiM out of his
dwii Market;
J?liii]d, the spendthrift, got in an early
Effort; to pose as an economist by proposing that
the Queensland Assembly be reduced from 72 to
50 members. Philp thus flatters Kidston by pro-
fessing to imitate him. But it is all sham. He
didn’t propose to touch the Legislative Council —
his friends are there. And he didn’t say that he
wanted to deal with the 1 blazing anomaly, which
he allowed to exist all his Ministerial days, by
which some electorates have 5000 voters on the
roll and some not 800.

Business, Robbery, etc.
One of the most neglected of Australia’s natural
industries is the distillation of perfume from the boronia
flower The boronia plant for ages seems to have wasted
its sweetness in Westralia, where it is indigenous to the
soil Of late years, since the discovery of Coolgardie
jrold the plant has been cultivated in the other States,
and ’nowadays it grows apace in cold Victoria, and big
bunches are sold for twopence by hawkers where a
little buttonhole bouquet used to cost a shilling.
The air of Sydney and Melbourne will presently be heavy
with the scent of boronia in its season, and the crowd will
remark “ How nice ! ” and then the unique aroma will
be forgotten for another year. The odors of violets and
lavender are always with us when the plants are out of
flower ; but boronia, which seems to yield more of the
verv essence of its sweetness to the distiller (though
the distillation may be more difficult) has not been
brought into popularity as a toilet perfume. All
the chemists’ stuff with winch women appeal to the
noses of mankind is imported. They want it from
Paris or London, yet they might get a delightful
native boronia essence for less money if they had the
mind to bring it into Australian fashion. There is a
“ True Boronia ” perfume distilled, in a small way, by men
with the unromantic names of Blogg Bros., Melbourne—a
cdm o-entle perfume, that carries a guarantee of good
character with it, and is entirely free from the
common or boudoir suggestion of disguised patch-
ouli such as many of the vaunted perfumes carry.
This honest Australian .flower scent is, it appears, better
appreciated in London than in Melbourne, where it is
made The larger quantity of a limited output is sent to
England, and Bloggs have at least one high-born con-
noiseur on their books—a countess, or a duchess, or
something—who sends an order at regular intervals for
another box of “ True Boronia.” Also, she is careful not
to teil the other countess cats where she gets it from,
which is a delicate compliment to the article, but not
good for trade. Evidently, a large demand for boronia
perfume could be easily created. Some of the leading
feminine lights of the Commonwealth should approach
the question in a commercial spirit with a view to starting
an Eesthetic^distillery. # # .
“Private Enterprise” (this is not a case of
“ business ” nor of “etc.”) is sometimes very enterprising
indeed After the ’Frisco quake one telegraph co.,
though it had no wires available, accepted many thousands
of messages for despatch by citizens wishing to reassure
distant friends. Those telegrams simply couldn’t be
despatched, but the co. is estimated to have netted
£2OO 000 bv fraudulently accepting them for despatch.

OK”: Touching a Bulletin par. (July 16th)
on *.i. "revival of the “ tea gift ” dodge in Melbourne, not-
withstanding the suppression of the parasitic “ Coupon ”
Co It is remarkable that the indignant MTntyre Bros,
and other Melbourne firms trading in tea of recognised
quality have not bestirred themselves to prevent these
evasions of the anti-coupon law. The thing 1 is being
carried to ludicrous limits. Where coupons or fancy
goods of trivial value were formerly given away with a
pound of tea, it is now the tea that is given away with
crockery worth almost what is charged for it at ordinary
shop rates. For instance, a grocer lures custom by
offering two cups and saucers with a pound of
“shilling” tea. The gift, so-called, represents lid. at
a fair trade computation, and the women of the district,
needing cups and saucers, devote them to the awful
purpose of holding the boiled mystery. Practically it is
penny tea to the purchaser, and it tastes like just what
might be expected for the money. A somewhat less
deadly preparation is inflicted in bob packages upon the
purchasers of one cup and saucer at other shops. This is
perhaps a worse evil for the legitimate tea-merchant to
i contend against, because the drinkers don’t immediately
get internal warnings. People have been known to drink
the tea for three months before they felt the effects. The
question as to how these gift teas come to be passed by
the Customs officers as rubbish fit for consumption pro-
vokes the question whether they are imported at all.
Possibly they are manufactured from Australian scrub,
or are blown in by the Melbourne dust-storms.

Jan who fell into a vat in a Queensland sugar mill, and
was fished out of the treacle three days later, reminds
me that, when assistant in a S. American colicha, I
several times found big junks of horseflesh-once nearly
an entire leg—in casks of Spanish wine. Horse is said to
give body to thin wine.
PIRATES OLD AND NEW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Those pirates of the bad old days, the rangers of the brine,
The highwaymen of many smiling seas,
They all were soulless scoundrels, but there s some-
thing rather fine
In the red-capped, swarthy rascal who in every fell design
Had the courage of his scarlet villainies.
He went out upon the waters, and a truthful sign he had
Not a Bible nor a Book of Common Prayer—
For the flag he flew proclaimed him as a robber. He was
clad
In the costume of his calling. He was honorably bad,
And to that extent his piracy was fair.
The buccaneers who sailed abroad to spoil the ships of
Spain
Were villains black and bloody, truth to tell ;
They were tyrants after power, and they drew the sword
for gain,
But their hearts were stout, their limbs were strong, they
“chanced it” with the slain,
Nor tried to dodge the loser’s share of Hell.
The brigand on his mountain track was something of a
thief,
And deep and wide his tide of carnage ran.
But he fought for all his plunder, and the vaunting
Tuscan chief
Staked his life upon the venture rash, and if he came to
grief
Then he simply took his hanging like a man.
We have our brigands nowadays, our slavers neat and
clean,
Our buccaneers maintain the evil search,
But, oh 1 the scoundrel crew has grown degenerate and
mean,
And on the trim, suburban streets the Pirate Chief is
seen,
As he bears his little brace of books to church.
They’ve all forsook the Spanish Main ; no more upon the
waves
The rover braves the fiercest winds that blow.
The brigand has an office in the city where he slaves,
And the pirate with advertisements your valued custom
craves,
While the buccaneer is Somebody and Co.
They murder still, but now the gang is cowardly and sly,
Its business signs are writ in blue and gold ;
It wears the blackest coats and sleekest hats that cash
can buy,
It robs, and rogues, and plunders yet, but when it comes
to die,
The church will point its spirit to the Fold.
The pirate stands in safety now, a pillar of the State,
He has comfort, he’s a coward, he is fat.
Our horror of the old-time one is nothing to the hate
That the heart of man is saving up to squander soon or
late '
On the pirate in the prim belltopper hat 1
VIC.
EDWARD DYSON.

THE STOMACH

When the stomach is right,

When the stomach is

1 le is out of sorts, he is nerv-
