ALLCOCK’S-The Original and only Genuine Porous Plaster.
A Page from a Swagman's
Diary.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE reached Mount Hutton, on Island
Plain Creek, soon after noon. Four
men were wanted for the mustering
camps, and I proposed going out; but George
objected. He was unfavorably known at Mt.
Hutton, though that wasn’t the excuse he gave
for not calling on the good people down there.
The horses were notorious buck-jumpers. They
could kick the eye out of a mosquito without
touching its eyelash. The cattle were very wild,
many of them impossible to yard. The calves
and yearlings were lassooed in the bush, and
lashed down and branded. Some were branded
and ear-marked at full gallop.
“ How do they do that, George ? ”
“By jes’ ridin’ alongside an’ leanin’ over, with
the reins on one arm. This way ”
Unfortunately, he touched the mare with his
spur, and a sudden side movement put George on
his head.
“ I should think that’s rather a dangerous
game, George,” I remarked, as George picked
himself up and looked about for his pipe.
'* But they don’t all do it that way,” he said,
smiling sheepishly.
“ Wkatabout the brand?” I asked. “ Wouldn’t
it get cold ? ”
“T’asn’ttime. Y’oughter know speed generates
heat. Heven’t yer been to college ?”
“ Well, I did go to some such institution one
time; but I can’t say as I ever took much intellect
with me.”
“Greatmistake,” said George, seriously. “It’s
a waste o’ time goin’ to college without intellect.”
“ How is it the shoes on the horses don’t get
heated ?” I inquired.
“’Cause they don’t’ave any. Them ’orses ’as
hoofs hard as flint. Y’oughter see ’em goin’ down
mountains, and the blokes shootin’ wild bulls full
gallop. M’Donald was the cove could ride ’em.
Seen a ’orse buck with him one mornin’ till he
bucked the bridle off an’ slung the saddle from
under him without breakin’ a strap or unbucklin’
a girth. That’s true’s yer there ! I ken show yer
the paddick he bucked in. Mac. stuck to him,
an the ’orse kep’ on buckin’ till he bucked both
his boots an’ socks off. True’s yer born ! Then
he bucked ev’ry button off his togs, an’ loosened
his back teeth. The buckle of his belt broke an’
he was cornin’ undressed. But Mac. still stuck
to him, an’ that ’orse would ’a’ been performin’ yet
if his hoofs ’ad stood. But they got loose. Wot
does he do then but ketch his tail in his mouth—
between his legs—an’ roll over an’ over like a
hoop. They revoluted right down the hill an’
into the waterhole below the ’ouse. The hole’s
there yet. Show yer as we go by. The ’orse
stood in it to let his hoofs swell tight. Meant t’
’ave another cut in, but the bell rung an’ Mac.
went up for refreshments.”
“I suppose you’ve heard of the horse that
bucked his brand off, George ? Do they do any-
thing like that here ? ”
“ Do that !” said George, and he blew a whiff
through his nose. “ What a Mount ’Utton ’orse
can’t do is a utter nonimpossibility. Yer’ll see
’em with their tails kinked all shapes an’ their
ears dislercated. Seen one kick his brand off
wonst. Was cow-kickin’ at his rider when he
done it.”
“ Where are we going to camp to-night,
George ? ”
“ At the Mud Springs, three miles up.”
We were riding quietly along, when suddenly
my interest became centred in a most remarkable
individual. He was a real sundowner, the best
type of the genus I had seen. He was squatting
under a tree at a waterhole fronting Mt. Hutton
homestead. With the aid of two glasses, he was
cutting his hair close to the scalp. His excuses
for boots were bound together with greenhide and
wire; round his feet were pieces of blanket which
he called “ Prince Alberts.” He smiled benignly
as I pulled up, but did not stop shearing.
“ What’s the next place ahead ?” I asked him.
“ Dunno.”
“Well, what’s the manager’s name?”
“ Dunno.”
“ Didn’t you come the way we’re going ? ”
“ Dunno.”
He finished his bush-crop, and had a dry wash—
that is, he wiped himself with an old shirt. He
■evidently came from a dry-blowing district.
, ‘‘l never want ter see bosses, let Tone know
em,” he remarked. “All I want’s tucker—an’
plenty of it. Gorra match on yer?” He lit his
pipe. “As fer names o’places,” he resumed be-
tween puffs, “I gives ’em names o’ me own,
cordin’ to th’ treatment I get there. Fust place
yer come to’s ‘Hardnut’; then yer’ll run agin
‘Saltjunk,’ an’ if yer don’t get bogged on ‘Stick-
fast Flat,’ yer’ll hit ‘ Stinkin’-Stringy ’ safe
•enough. After that there’s ‘ Greenhide Codger,’
Pannikin Dog,’ ‘Dustbag,’ ‘ Pinch-o’-Tea,’
Hardcase,’ ‘Dead-’orse,’ ‘Skinflint,’ ‘Hungry-
Gut,’ ‘ Pipe-o’-Weed,’ ‘ Meengy-Beggar,’ an’ 50
more, from ‘ Bleedin’ Hill’ on’ards.”
You seem to have had bad luck in your
travels,” I ventured.
“Dunno. I’m alive; an’, luck or no luck, I’ll
get to where I’m goin’.”
“Where’s that?”
‘‘Peg-out.”
The old sundowner had one thing that we
would have been glad of for a pack-cover. This
was an oilskin coat—home-mkde by the look of
it—which he used for a tent.
line things t’ ’ave when it’s rainin’,” said
jeorge, as we rode away. “Keeps out every
•drop—an’ they make good water-buckets, too.
Gver carry water in yer pocket? I’ave. ’Bout
ten year ago it was. Had a oilskin made to
Wler-tremenjus big pockets in it, but no flaps.
Didn notice that till a storm copped me cornin’
from Miles. I feels the bloomin’ thing gettin’
eavier an’ ’eavier, an’ by’n’bye, feelin’ about, I
shoves me two ’ands inter them pockets, an’—
blest if there wasn’t ten gallons o’ water in each
of em, an’ I had ter stand on me thinkin’ end to
empty meself.”
“Why didn’t you take it off?” I asked,
deferentially.
, “Wasn't it rainin’? What d’yer think I was
umpm’a hoilskin for?” And George’s expres-
sion betokened sorrow for my stupidity.
Camped on a flat that was riddled with mud
springs. Hot water and mud bubbled up to the
surface, at times flowing over like lava from a
volcano, and forming into a concrete mass on the
nat. A mound of this stuff encircled each spring
giving it the appearance of an old well. Some of
them were covered with a thin crust of brittle
stun, lo walk upon this meant to drop into
eternity.
More n one man’s disappeared mysterious
-bout this part,” George remarked, as we peered
down a muddy orifice. “Shouldn’t wonder if
they’re doin’ a camp down there.” (Pause.)
“Ever walk in yer sleep?”
“Once—a long while ago.”
“ If yer done it once, there’s no tellin’ when yer
might break out again. Better tie yerself up to-
night.” (Longpause.) “ Ye’d be fit for tli’bone-
yard long fore we could fish yer out, an’ I’d ’ave
ter go ter th’ inkwest ter evidence how yer got in,
an’ prove as I ’adn’t a ’and in it. ’T’ud put me
ter a lot o’ trouble. I’ll lend yer the grey mare’s
’alter.”
“ Suppose I break away and go down with your
halter on me?”
“ By cripes ! ” said George, “ that ’ud look sus-
picious. Better not tie anythin’ on yer. ”
We had tea.
“Dunno wot to do with you to-night, I’m
sure,” George reflected, lighting his pipe. “Never
travelled with a bog-blinded somnambulist be-
fore.” (Long smoke.) Suddenly he jumped up.
“Th’ very thing!” he said, and disappeared in
the darkness. In a few minutes he returned,
jingling the horse-bell. “’T’ull wake me if yer
try to get away,” he declared, as he strapped the
thing round my neck. “Now I’ll be able ter
sleep peaceful.”
George was soon asleep. Then I took the bell
to a bush a few yards away, tied one end of a long
rope to it, and, crouching behind the saddles with
the other end, gave it a few vigorous tugs. George
stirred quickly and listened. “ Tingle—jangle—
jingle !” Up leaped George, and rushed towards
the bell with his hair bristling. “Hey—come
back ! Coo-ee ! Coo-ugh ! ” The rope was
jerked out of my hands, and a thud told that
George had got tangled in it. Reinvestigated on
his hands and knees, and presently came back
with a look on him like a thunderstorm.
“Fine game yer’avin’,” he growled, as he re-
turned to his blankets. “Pity yer didn’t break
yer blamed neck !’’ Getting no answer, George
turned his back to me and covered his head,
finally renouncing all responsibility in muffled
tones: “Ken go down forty blinded wells if yer
like !”
• • * #
We spelled a day at the Heifer Station, an
empty hut and a horse paddock on the bank of a
big lagoon. George knew all the country now,
and could find his way to Westgrove without a
road. Leaving the hut, we came presently to a
road running to right and left. George was look-
ing both ways at once. He was puzzled.
We took the road to the right, George peering
all ways. Struck a creek, and just below we saw
the hut we had started from. .Returned to the
cross-roads, and George went on a tour round
whilst I minded the pack-horses. He “toured
round ” for an hour before he found himself. Ex-
plained that the road was made since he was
through here last, and he had thought it was a
short cut. Then we came to burning grass, got
smoke-blind, and lost the road again dodging
round fires Discovered Westgrove accidentally.
George knew the woman who cooked here. Old
friend of his. So we turned out on the flat, and
he walked up to interview her. He came back
shortly—empty-handed, and looking as if the
interview had disagreed with him.
“ By cripes ! I’ll get even with her,” he growled,
picking up a pannikin.
“What’s happened ? ”
George sipped his tea. “ Jes’ called on her in
a friendly way,” he went on. “ Thought she
might get offended if I rode by, knowin’ her so
long.” George sipped more tea. “ Stuck me up
for a bridle what her jumpin’ ’usband never lent
me in his natchrel. Asked her if she’d a loaf o’
bread to spare, an’ the old cow went an’ asked
the missus. The missus made some veiled allusion
’bout leavin’ the premises. ’S if I wanted to take
th’ blighted premises with me. They’re nice
people about ’ere.”
• • * »
Next day we crossed the Great Dividing Range
on to the Western watershed. Approaching
Mooyar Downs we passed a dead swagraan. He
was lying in a little belt of scrub, with his elbow
on a iog, and his hand under his head. His swag
was about 40yds. away. Nearer to him was a
brass comb and a looking glass, and a quart-pot
in a half-rotten bag. His face was partly eaten
by ants ; yet a clay-dabber was still gripped
between his bare teeth, as though he had expired
suddenly while having his smoke. Two gins, who
had been hunting wallaby, carried the swag on a
pole into Merrivale. When asked about the
corpse, one said: “Baal mine touch dat one;
him too much dead.” It was buried later—un-
identified.
Occasionally the skeleton of some unknown
and unmissed wanderer is discovered in the bush,
with indications pointing clearly to murder. I
remember one which was found in a dense scrub
with the skull riddled with shot. Another came
to light near Goombungee (Q.), having the mark
of an axe on the skull, and a similar mark on one
of the shoulder-blades. Some decayed pieces of
clothing still clung to portions of the bones, but
there was nothing that could be identified. Such
cases start one pondering as to how many Butlers
there are prospecting about the bush. In the lonely
backblocks of Queensland, for instance, where
the narrow tracks wind through scrubs and gorges
and lignum, and houses are anything from 20 to
50 miles apart, police are scarce as princes, and men
travel for work where they are unknown and un-
noticed. Hundreds of murders could be committed
there with impunity. A swagman may be well-
known at stations; but when he passes one place he
is not expected back, and is not expected anywhere
else, so he is never definitely missed. It is a grue-
some subject, full of possibilities, and inclines you
to treat your casual mate like an aborigine—never
walk in front of him, and sleep with a bulldog
chained to your leg.
George called at Mooyar Downs, a selection, to
obtain settlement of a debt. He left me at the
sliprails, and advised me to let the horses feed
about, as he would be delayed some time. These
were old friends of his, and were sure to have a
lot of news to tell him, and would persuade him
to take afternoon tea. In the meantime, I might
get the meat-bag ready. He could have as much
meat here as he liked to take away. So I settled
down for a long time to wait at the sliprails.
George disappeared behind the house. A few
minutes after there came the sound of high-pitched
voices from that direction, and presently George
re-appeared in a great hurry. He was running,
and the owner of Mooyar Downs was running
behind him with something in his hands like a
clothes-prop. George ran all the way to the slip-
rails, but the owner of Mooyar Downs stopped
half-way and contented himself with shaking his
fist and the clothes-prop alternately, while he
delivered a spasmodic peroration to the vanishing
audience.
The horses were ready, and George mounted
with more celerity than he had shown for some
time. He kicked desperately at Lord Brumby’s
sides, and jerked savagely at the bit, whilst ever
and anon he cast furtive glances over his shoulder.
“Get any meat, George?” I asked, jogging to
keep alongside him.
“Meat! ” he said, his limbs still working faster
than the horse’s. “ Did you expect meat from
that cow? He wouldn’t give yer enything if yer
tongue was hangin’out an’ he was a iumped-up
Beef Trust.”
He treated Lord Brumby to another dose of
kicks, his eye on the rise ahead that would shut
out the view of a hateful place called Mooyar
Downs.
“Said he didn’t owe me no money. He’s a
dirty prevaricator.” (Pause.) “ I’ll show th’ cow !
.... Called me a crawler—not to mention other
things I wouldn’t say to a dog ! . . . . An’ hit me !
.... Said he paid me. He’s a liar !.. .. I’ll sum-
mons th’ swine !
“ How much does he owe you, George ? ”
“Seven bob. Says I owe it for board. Never
boarded with the miserable dog in me born days.
Wouldn’t be seen in the same paddick with him.”
“Oh ! I was wondering what you were run-
ning for. Was it to get out of the paddock ? ”
George looked at me with pitying eyes.
“ Did yer think I was frightened?”
We had brought no cooked tucker from last
camp, having depended on George’s friends to
fill the void, and it was two hours after dark
before we had anything to eat. Men in the bush,
particularly travellers, like to have their evening
meal over by sundown.
George had mostly a song to sing or a yarn to
tell at night time, but to-night he only whistled
softly to himself, and spread his blankets with
more deliberation than usual. I felt sorry for
George, for he wasn’t a bad fellow. As he often
said to himself: “There were plenty worse, if
you only knew where to find them.”
Edwahd S.

I have met one or two Australian farmers land-hunting
here. But they had better stay at home. My business
brings me continually amongst the farmers, and I have
never met one who could say he possessed the horses and
machinery he worked. The landowners nearly all have
big general stores, where the tenant has to purchase his
goodg To buy land from the State you have to go too
far out; and there are no such things as roads and cul-
verts, nor deputations to Ministers.

Three Sinners.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was in a country train,
Rolling onward through the rain,
That three mischief-loving Tones,
Strove to cause a cleric pain,
By relating sundry stories
(Rather blue and nasty stories)
Of a very fervent, amatory strain.
Neither frightened glance nor look
Did the cleric give—nor took
Any heed of their narrations,
As they swung and bumped and shook,
And meandered through the stations
(Very slowly through the stations);
But, instead, just calmly sat and read his book.
Then three men, both bad and old,
Whispered fiercely : “ Are we sold ?
Does he think that he will beat us ? ”
At the thought both hot and cold
Went the three. “Or will he eat us !
(Perhaps he thinks that he can eat us ! ”).
And the train still onward bumped and slowly
rolled.
So they whispered, and they winked,
And the laughing metals clinked,
When the leader of the party,
As through tears of mirth he blinked,
In a manner gay and hearty
(And he could be gay and hearty),
Told a story—which is happily extinct.
But the cleric calmly read,
As by field and stream they sped ;
Never paid the least attention
Was as silent as the dead.
And the three—l cannot mention
(No, I really cannot mention)
All the nasty, bad and spiteful things they said.
“ Nerve ? Oh, yes, he has got some !
Doesn’t mind it ! Rather rum ! ”
So they muttered indiscreetly,
Looking dazed and very glum,
While the reverend most neatly
(Notice how they do it neatly ?)
Turned the pages of the work on Kingdom Come.
Mad were they and very sore.
But ere they could whisper more
Dashed the train into a station,
Where a man approached the door
And he caused much consternation
(Very bitter consternation)
When he shouted in a voice that was a roar.
Then the sinners sitting near
Felt abashed and rather queer,
When that cleric, calm and saintly,
Put his hand up to his ear ;
Thus conveying to them quaintly
(Very quietly and quaintly)
He was deaf, and so, of course, he couldn’t hear !
VIC.
KODAK.
Our Village.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THOUGH men may come and men may
go, our village (like the brook) will pursue
its changeless course for ever 1
And what a sleepy village it is ! The church,
the shops, are all asleep ; the police station, the
public-house, the State school, the bowling-green,
the “ reserve,” the shabby rows of cottages, the
villager, and the sparrow, cat, dog, stray horse
and cow that wander within his gates.
Why is our sleepy village here at all ? To what
purpose does it exist ? Of what use are its crazy
tenements (which will stand till they are con-
demned), its inn (known as the “White Horse” ;
the sign is a white horse chasseing), and its
chemist’s shop, where—in addition to the ordinary
medicines—mustard-plasters, sticks of liquorice,
lozenges, scent and stamps are supplied at the
lowest possible prices ?
If a landslip came and swallowed the village-
crazy tenements, “ White Horse,” chemist’s shop
and all—would the great world be a whit the
wiser ?
Would it even hear of its loss ?
I think not. I fear not. But I am expressing
this opinion in the strictest confidence, for it
would be as much as my life is worth were my
rank heresy to come to the ears of the villagers.
Our villager is essentially conservative —a model
of dull, self-satisfied contentment. Barren of
ambition and purpose, he talks of standing by
“ the good ways,” while he turns up his eyes in
horror at your jargon of electric lighting, surpliced
choirs, Queen Anne architecture, and the like.
Mr. Villager opines that the gas-lamp which
served his father will “do ” the son ; and, as to
your Papist fal-lals and nightgowns, he will have
none of them !
The gimcrack villa turns his stomach like the
bazaar cake (all plaster of Paris and no satisfac-
tion) ; and then, having exhausted his own
eloquence and your patience, Mr. Villager turns
his stubborn back, yawns, rolls over on his side,
and resumes his interrupted snooze.
Oh, the parson, the parson’s wife, and their
seven maiden daughters—what typical villagers
they are 1
I can see the parson’s wife now, nodding over
the reins of the pony chaise, while the brown pony
whisks his tail, and the fat parson pays his listless
compliments to the more listless parishioner
within doors. And I can see the seven maiden
daughters blinking their lethargic eyes, as they
boil over the front forms at the cricket-club con-
certs and friendly socials —drowsy little socials,
whose tedium is only broken by the appearance
of “light refreshments, supplied by the Guild,” in
the foi m of weak coffee and gingernuts, cream (?)
cakes, and wedges of thick Victoria sandwich.
Our policeman is the policeman of Phil May,
the policeman of “ The Slide ” !
Solemnly, and with unspeakable dignity, does
our policeman patrol the village streets, pausing
at rare intervals to administer a pompous warning
on an orchard-robbing urchin (who listens with his
tongue in his cheek), for the legs of the urchin are
fleet, and our policeman (alas !) waxeth fat ; more-
over the wicked urchin is well aware that the
forbidding dignitary is merely a John A Dreams,
walking in his sleep ! Our garrulous wool-gather-
ing chemist (whose prosings are warranted to lull
to slumber the liveliest buyer of two halfpenny
stamps) is surprised that his poppy-heads and
sedatives are so seldom in request .
“They wake me in the small hours for a mus-
tard plaster,” he grumbles, “ and call me from
my dinner for a stamp! And, talking of
At this point a dull glaze settles on the eye of
the unfortunate customer for he knows that
“Jones has begun,” and that the sun will be low
in the west ere he has ended.
The greengrocery is superintended by a maiden
lady (their name is legion in the village) whose
sallow complexion and heavy lids attest to the
truth of her statement that she rises before the
sun, and that it don’t suit ’er but that one must
be an early bird if one wants to catch the worm
at the morning market.
The maiden lady is said to have had a disap-
pointment,” but appears too tired to worry over it.
The butcher is a handsome man who has buried
a large family and is indolently raising a second.
A handsome man, but as torpid as the summer
fly that invades his mutton chops. His hand-
some, large-eyed children inherit their parent s
Creole airs. . ,
The grocer is (for our village) brisk, and the
baker (who is a widower and wears a kiss-curl) is
called a terror for the girls. And the girls—our
girls, our village girls—one and all they wear the
same expression —waiting !
Plain and comely, wise and foolish, young and
old, bold and sweet, all have this waiting air
expectancy tempered with resignation, resigna-
tion struggling with dejection, and a conscientious
determination to make the best of things. A sad
“ beast-of-burden,” wistful, querulous, longing,
disappointed kind of look have our girls as they
Stand in the sleepy sunshine, listening to the
sleepy birds twittering in the sleepy elms that
wave in the sleepy streets of the sleepy village !
DOROTHY McORAE.
“Reserved for Officers.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GOLDSMITH’S veteran “wept o’er his
wounds and deeds of sorrow done ” ; but,
although my veteran had been badly hit
at Eland’s River, and his deeds of sorrow ranged
from the picking, on the end of a bayonet, of a
“ hands-upper ” from a patch of mimosa bush, to
the liberation of an infuriated swarm of bees
inside an officer’s tent, the only moisture observ-
able on his face came from contact with a beer
glass.
Nor, unlike most veterans, was he vain of his
geographical knowledge. The scene of his ex-
ploits was always identified in the same way. If
it wasn’t the place where he and his mates were
drunk and smashed something, then it was the
place where they were sober and stole something.
In fact this recital of burning, smashing, and
stealing was growing monotonous, when suddenly
came a welcome relief in the evidence of the Aus-
tralian’s desire, under all sorts of conditions, to be
clean—physically, that is. The last flicker of lime-
light left over from the war was just then
focussed on the case of Major Smith. Someone
had mentioned Smith, and, after a gloomy pause,
my veteran broke out savagely.
“ Blow Smith ! ” said he. “I ain’t got no time
for ’ini. We got a down on him for somethin’
what happened up Rhodesia.
“O’course, you’ve heard all about the tough
times and starvation tracks we struck up there,
but what settled us—we was mostly pretty hard
doers, too—was the dirt ’n’ fleas ’njlcther things
what comes to a man from turnin’ in all standin’
for weeks at a time. We was all the same, officers
’n’ men, ’n’ it was awful. In p’int of cleanliness
you couldn’t tell a dook from any old Domain
’owler.
“ Well, we was camped in the scrub up there,
bush veldt they call it, and one day Barcoo Bill—
he was alius skitin’ about urovin’ ’n’ we christened
him Barcoo —found a spring. Barcoo was alius
findm’ things. Once he seen a allygator ’n’
another time he reckoned he was woke up by a
lion roarin’ in his ear. But any’ow, when he put
us onter the spring, I tell yer we rushed it like a
second Wyalong.
“ It was bubblin’ outer some rocks, ’n’ at the
bottom was a hole ’bout six foot deep, a sorter
natural basin, an’ it was lovely. So we felt like
strikin’ a tank out back after a ‘Darlin’ shower’
on’y about a hundred times better. An’ then this
Major, ’oo be rights wasn’t our boss at all, puts in
his spoke. They must ’a’ noticed we was lookin’
light ’n’ frisky on it, ’n' next day Smith gets the
strength of it. And would yer b’leeve it, ’e ’ad
ther immortal hide to run some barb wire round
our spring an’ put up a notice, ‘ Reserved for
Officers,’ same as some o’ the chaps nailed on a
ladies’ carriage at De Aar ? An’ mind yer, ’e
wasn’t our boss at all.
“Well, o’ course, ther mob went away sore
over it, an’ Barcoo cussed until some o’ the fellers
in ther next tent told him it was bad manners to
be whisperin’. ‘I found ther flamin’ ’ole,’ said
Barcoo. ‘ An’ I’d like to get me lamps on the
cove what’ll stop me from swimmin’ in it.’
“Just then Sonny Hanson, ’oo had come over
to invite Barcoo to a little game, chips in. Sonny
was a quiet, sleepy sorter bloke. ’ls old man
was a parson, but he was terrible fond of a game,
an’ would play for anything, from matches
up. ‘Nice sort of a soldier, you are,’ says
Sonny. ‘Begrudgin’ the poor officer a bath.
I say let ’im ’ave it, an’ welcome.’ An’
then, just as Barcoo was goin’ to fasten on to him,
he smiles in a sleepy sorter way. ‘ Come over to
ther fire, Barcoo,’ says he, rattlin’ a dice-box ho
allers carried with his prayer-book ’n’ things, ‘ ’n’
if you like to bring them dog tins over I’ll show
yer a new game.”
* » * ♦
“There was a ’ell of a row next mornin’ when
the officers came back from their dip. I thought
Barcoo ’ad found another allygator when I ’eard
them in the distance. One of ’em ’ad his shirt
tied round his ’oof, an’ a English johnny who was
peggin’ in with them looked as if he’d ’ad a willin’
go with an indignant cactus plant.
“ Seems the fence ’ad disappeared in the night,
an’ while they was wonderin’ where it ’ad got to
most of ’em suddenly found it in the bottom o’
the spring, ’n’ about a cartload of empty tins
with it, not to mention some thorn bush ’n’ a
broken bottle.
“ The ‘ Reserved ’ notice was still standin’, ’n’
afterwards Sonny wrote on it ‘ You’re welcome.’
“We reckoned ther Bores must ’a’ done it.
Any’ow, we blamed it on ter them. We knew
they wouldn’t mind.”
